LEBANESE RE'PUBLIC
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION AND MODERNIZATION PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Loan and Project Summary
Borrower             :  Lebanese Republic
Implementing Agency :  Council for Reconstruction and Development
Beneficiaries            Ministry of Hydraulic and Electric Resources
(MOHER); Ministry of Agriculture (MOA);
Affiliated Institutions of MOHER and MOA;
Regional Water Irrigation Authorities;
Water Users' Associations and farmers
Loan Amount          :  USS 57.23 million equivalent
Terms                :  Standard variable interest rate, with 17 years
maturity, including 4 years of grace
Project Objectives       The main objectives of the project are to
increase agricultural production,
agriculture-based income and employment in
previously neglected rural areas and to
achieve a sustainable and improved management
of water resources, through the rehabilitation
and adequate O&M of irrigation infrastructure,
and the provision of basic agricultural
support services.
Project Description :  The project comprises two major components:
(a) rehabilitation and modernization of
surface irrigation infrastructure covering
about 27,000 ha; and (b) provision of support
to national public institutions involved in
the irrigation sub-sector (MOHER, MOA and
their affiliated agencies), and to regional
and local institutions involved in the
operation and maintenance of irrigation
schemes. Under the infrastructure
rehabilitation component, the project would
support: (i) rehabilitation and modernization
of irrigation works and facilities long
neglected as a result of the hostilities; and
(ii) operation and maintenance of irrigation
systems as well as the procurement of
irrigation equipment and materials. The
institutional support component would include:
(a) strengthening of irrigation and water
management public institutions; (b)
strengthening of basic public agricultural



- ii -
services, such as applied research, extension,
quality control of agricultural inputs and
produce; and (c) support to Water Authorities
and Water User Associations in the operation
and maintenance of irrigation systems and
improving the efficiency of on-farm water use.
Cost recovery and cost sharing would, as
indicated in the Government's draft letter of
irrigation sector policy, be consistent with
the National Water Management Strategy
currently under preparation.
Environment              The project has been classified as a Category
B project for the purpose of OD 4., Annex A,
as its aggregate impact on the environment
would be positive. The project would enhance
the availability of surface irrigation water
for agricultural production and would thus
reduce the overuse of groundwater resources.
By regulating and improving use of pesticides
and fertilizers and promoting Integrated Pest
Management practices, the project would also
reduce the potential hazards associated with
the misuse of pesticides and fertilizers.
Benefits                 Implementation of the project would generate
benefits arising from: (i) an increase in
agriculture production and income of about
34,000 families as a result of improvement of
greater accessibility to surface irrigation
water and increased water use efficiency; and
(ii) improvement in the monitoring, and
sustainable use of irrigation water and in the
operation, maintenance and management of
rehabilitated irrigation infrastructure, as a
result of institutional support and
strengthening.
Risks                :  The major risk would be the inability of
MOHER, MOA, and their affiliated institutions
to implement the project efficiently. This
risk has been minimized by the organizational
arrangements agreed upon with the Government,
which place project implementation under the
responsibility of the already existing CDR's
Program Management Unit and MOHER's Water
Sector Implementation Unit, established under
the Emergency Reconstruction and
Rehabilitation Project. In addition, the
project would include provision for training



- iii -
and technical assistance to Government
agencies, Regional Water Irrigation
Authorities and Water Users' Associations.

LEBANESE REPUBLIC
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION AND MODERNIZATION PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
I. BACKGROUND
A. The Economy
1.01        Lebanon, a prosperous upper middle-income country in the mid-
1970s, has been devastated by 15 years of turmoil resulting from violent civil
strife and military occupation. In the period between 1950 and 1975,
Lebanon's free market economy expanded by about 6 percent per annum.   This
growth was largely driven by the service sectors, namely trade, tourism and
finance, which attracted business from surrounding oil-based economies. After
the eruption of the civil war in 1975, the economy deteriorated markedly, with
intermittent recovery during the two periods of relative calm, 1978 to 1981,
and 1986 to 1988. Although resumption of economic activity following the
cessation of internal fighting boosted per capita income to about US$820 in
1990, it was still only about one third of the 1975 level in real terms. The
total damage inflicted on the economy during nearly two decades of fighting
has been estimated by the United Nations at $25 billion. All principal
sectors of the Lebanese economy have been affected. Damage is both a direct
result of the war as well as the accumulated effects of a near total
disruption in capital investment and maintenance.
1.02        The prolonged civil strife had a devastating impact on Lebanon's
trade performance. The value of total exports in nominal terms amounted only
to $0.6 billion in 1992, compared with $1.5 billion immediately prior to the
outbreak of hostilities. In contrast, import requirements grew unabated and
have accelerated since 1990 when heavy demands for renewed consumption and
reconstruction efforts had to be satisfied. As a result, the trade deficit
widened quickly from $1.7 billion in 1988 to $3.6 billion in 1992, equivalent
to about 60 percent of GDP. No significant shift has taken place with respect
to Lebanon's major trading partners. The Gulf States still account for the
largest share of exports, both agricultural and non-agricultural, just as they
did two decades ago. Similarly, most of Lebanon's imports continue to
originate from Europe and North America.
1.03        The Government envisages a phased strategy for rebuilding
Lebanon's ravaged economy and addressing social problems, consisting of a
short-term emergency reconstruction phase followed by a medium-term recovery
phase. The National Emergency Reconstruction Program (NERP) provides for
initial urgent investments, which will be made concurrently with the
identification and implementation of longer-term development strategies in the
various sectors. The NERP is designed as a multi-sectoral operation,
focussing on removing critical infrastructure bottlenecks, alleviating
pressing social problems, and strengthening the existing institutions in
charge of planning, financing, and implementing the NERP. The proposed
project would support the irrigation sub-sector component of the program.



B. The Agricultural Sector
1.04        Agriculture in the Economy. Since the beginning of the civil war
in 1975, Lebanese agriculture has been adversely affected by the loss of
export markets, the neglect or destruction of public irrigation
infrastructure, the elimination of public services and the displacement of the
rural population. After the end of the war and despite the determination of
the private sector, agriculture GDP has remained significantly below its
pre-war level (20 percent), and currently accounts for about 10 percent of
total GDP. Its contribution to the trade balance also declined sharply during
the course of the war as agricultural exports fell from 43 percent of
agricultural imports in 1970 to only 26 percent in 1990. Agriculture in
Lebanon has always been limited in scope relative to domestic consumption
requirements. Food self-sufficiency ratios have traditionally been between 20
and 30 percent and may be even lower today as a result of the sharp drop in
output. While most of the low value added crops grown in Lebanon are sold in
the domestic market, high value added crops, such as fruits and vegetables,
constitute a major source of foreign exchange earnings.
1.05        The Role of the Private Sector in Agriculture. In sharp contrast
to the public institutions, the private sector remained remarkably active
throughout the nearly two decades of civil strife and has filled many of the
voids created by the absence of the public sector. However, private supply of
agricultural services remains limited to advice on the use of chemical inputs
by firms making those products and to geographically limited research results
obtained by the research institutes. Essential services, such as
phytosanitary regulation, technical support, and quality control of
agricultural export produce, are still weak. At the grass root level,
unofficial water users, groups exist in almost all schemes. In the large
schemes, their participation in the operation and the management of the
irrigation is generally limited to the most downstream segments of irrigation
systems. There are also farmers' cooperatives and chambers of commerce. The
cooperatives are generally organized according to specific agricultural
commodities (citrus or fruit cooperatives); they supply their members with
some farm inputs, provide cold storage facilities and marketing assistance.
Chambers of commerce are organized on a regional basis, they provide their
members with marketing information and certificates of origin for their
agricultural export products.
1.06        Agricultural Sector Policy.  The fact that many private companies
have survived the war and were ready to begin rebuilding as soon as stability
returned speaks for the resilience and determination of the Lebanese private
sector. On the other hand, the virtual absence of the public sector during the
civil war has led to an irregular exploitation of natural resources. The need
for a stronger government role in agriculture, particularly in terms of
enforcing existing regulations and providing basic services, is essential for
the successful rehabilitation of agricultural production. This need is
recognized by the private sector, which has called for the strengthening of
public institutions in regulating and serving the agricultural sector in
Lebanon. To interact with grassroot sector players, the MOA has recently



conducted a national consultation on agricultural development, and is
presently formulating a national agricultural development policy within the
context of the High Agricultural Council. Government's priorities for the
agricultural sector are reflected in the draft proposal for the Ten-Year
Development Plan currently under preparation. The Plan aims at creating an
enabling environment for the private sector by focusing public investment in:
(i) natural resource management and environmental protection; (ii) development
of rural infrastructure; and (iii) promoting accessibility of credit in rural
areas. The sectoral policy under preparation would further improve the
economic environment by addressing the following issues: (i) fiscal and tariff
policy for agricultural produce; (ii) agricultural land zoning policy; (iii)
agroindustry and export promotion strategies; and (vi) respective roles of the
Government and the private sector in delivery of agricultural services and its
implication on the public expenditures program for agriculture. The Bank has
carried out some preliminary sector work on Agriculture and the issues and
priorities outlined above emerge as the more important ones.
1.07        Physical and Human Resource. Lebanon's geographic location
provides favorable conditions for agricultural production, in particular when
compared with other Middle Eastern and North African countries. The climate
is determined by the geographic location and the topological characteristics
of the country. In the coastal areas, moderate rainfall and year-round warm
temperatures allow for intensive cultivation of vegetables, bananas and citrus
fruits. Further inland extends Mount Lebanon, reaching peaks of over 10,000
feet in the northern part of the country. The northern and central mountain
ranges receive the highest amounts of precipitation, much of which falls
during the winter and spring seasons in the form of snow. Some of the
mediterranean slopes in the lower elevations of Mount Lebanon are used for the
cultivation of various fruit trees. Mostly, however, the mountain ranges are
not used for agricultural production except as grazing areas for small
livestock. The main agricultural areas are located in the Bekaa valley which
lies east of Mount Lebanon at an elevation of about 3,000 feet. Temperatures
in the Bekaa Valley are moderate, but annual rainfall is limited by its
geographic location. Annual precipitation ranges from 1270 mm in the central
mountains, to just over 800 mm in the south, which is roughly the average for
Lebanon as a whole, and to only 240 mm in the north.
1.08        Current estimates indicate that agricultural labor in Lebanon
decreased from 38 percent of the total labor force in 1969 to about 10 percent
in 1988. However, agriculture remains an important source of income in rural
areas. Although it is difficult to estimate the number of full time farmers
(140,000 in 1970), most families have agriculture as a part time activity.
Seasonal labor represents between 30 percent to 40 percent of the present
agricultural labor force in Lebanon. It is supplied by the local rural poor
population, and by foreign seasonal labor. Depending on the region, women can
either provide a significant part of the farm labor requirements or
negligible amounts.
1.09        Land Use and Production Patterns.  Of the 215,000 hectares (ha) of
agricultural land, about 60 percent is cropped under rainfed conditions and
about 40 percent (87,000 ha) under irrigation. Most of the irrigated land



- 4 -
(67,500 ha) is cropped year-round, but because of the lack of irrigation water
storage capacity, 20,000 ha are irrigated during the winter and early spring
seasons only. Perennial tree crops, vegetables and livestock make up a
significant proportion of agricultural production. The share of tree crops
over annual crops has increased from 41 percent in 1974 to more than 51
percent in 1990 and about 40 percent of the area planted to tree crops is
irrigated. Major tree crops include olives, citrus, apples, grapes, stone
fruits and bananas. Of total cultivated area, the area devoted to cereals
decreased from 30.4 percent in 1974 to 18 percent in 1990. The area
cultivated to industrial crops (mainly sugarbeets) also decreased in favor of
olive trees, and vegetables irrigated during the summer season. Major annual
crops cultivated in Lebanon include cereals, pulses, potatoes, tomatoes,
cucurbits and other vegetables.
1.10        Agricultural exports, mainly apples, citrus and vegetables, play
an important role in the Lebanese foreign exchange earnings. It is estimated
that agricultural exports ranged from 20 percent to 25 percent of total
exports for the past few years. Staple foods, on the other hand (wheat, beef,
milk, sugar) have to be imported in large quantities (70 percent to 80 percent
of consumption needs) every year. The future of agricultural exports will
heavily depend on the Lebanese ability to keep net production costs at
competitive levels and to develop effective marketing channels.
1.11        Agricultural Institutions.  The Ministry of Aariculture (MOA) is
mandated to set agricultural policy, plan and support its sustainable
development through its central and regional services and satellite agencies.
These include, inter alia, the Agricultural Research Institute of Lebanon
(ARIL) and the Green Plan (GP). MOA has been extremely weakened by the civil
war, in fact both its Central and Regional Offices are operating at low
capacity. Only ARIL and the GP maintain some operational capacity at the
moment. Present level of staffing is very low, as only about 14 percent of
the allowed positions are filled (310 over 2,200). The Agricultural Research
Institute of Lebanon (ARIL) is an autonomous body, with a central research
station at Tell Amara, in the Bekaa Valley and specialized regional stations,
including Fanar (Beirut), and Sour (South Lebanon). Before the hostilities
began, ARIL conducted valuable applied research for practical needs and
problems of agriculture in the country. However, most of the ARIL's stations
were heavily damaged and put out of operation. Notwithstanding the
hostilities, private and international research institutions have remained
operational: the Agricultural Research and Education Center (AREC) of the
American University of Beirut (AUB), at Haouch Sneid, the Exverimental
Research Center of the Saint Joseph University in Tanayel, and the research
stations of the International Center for Agricultural Research in the Dry
Areas (ICARDA) at Terbol and Kfar Dan. The Green Plan (GP) is an autonomous
agency of the MOA responsible for land preservation, land reclamation, road
construction, small hydraulic developments and on-farm level infrastructure
works. It has maintained a valuable part of its personnel and operational
capacity in five regional branches and has about 300 agents, of which 50 are
engineers.



1.12        Agricultural SupRort Services.  Although theoretically represented
in six major regions covering the whole of Lebanon, MOA's extension services
are practically non operational and most of the technological improvements are
selectively introduced and spread by the private sector (para. 1.05), mainly
through input suppliers and a few of the existing agricultural produce storage
and marketing cooperatives. The remaining extension staff, though
experienced, has been inactive during the war and has lost touch with field
realities. Salaries are extremely low and are not competitive with the
private sector. Input supplv is in the hands of very active traders in
fertilizers, pesticides and seeds; they also provide a form of credit to
farmers, to be recovered after harvest, through higher prices charged for the
inputs sold. Input supply companies are also the only available source of
technical assistance to the farmers, but such assistance is limited to the
marketing of their own products. The quality of inputs is often reported to
be below standards, particularly for fertilizers and pesticides. There is no
source of institutionalized credit for farmers in any of the project areas.
Farmers may receive credit assistance from local money lenders and commercial
firms (input suppliers and/or wholesalers) for periods which do not exceed 12
months. Credit from input suppliers is usually denominated in US Dollar
equivalent at competitive interest rates. However, the interest charged by
local traders and wholesalers on informal credit in local currency is very
high. A common complaint on the part of farmers is the sale of vegetables at
low prices from neighboring countries, and for delays in payment on the part
of some exporters.
C. The Irrigation Subsector
1.13        Water Resources.  Lebanon is relatively well endowed with
renewable water resources when compared with other countries in the region.
Total annual rainfall amounts to about 9,700 million cubic meters (MCM), which
is potentially adequate to support extensive agriculture. However,
agricultural development is limited by unfavorable regional and seasonal
distribution of water availability. Annual amounts of rainfall vary between
1270 mm in the central mountain ranges of Mount Lebanon and 240 mm in the
northern Bekaa valley. Seasonal factors and limited storage capacity lead to
a relative abundance of water during the wet season, but limit the available
surface flow during the dry season to only 818 MCM. This pattern is
particularly pronounced in the mediterranean slopes of Mount Lebanon, where
water available during the dry season amounts to only 21 percent of the annual
total. The Litani and other rivers running through the Bekaa valley limit the
seasonality of water availability slightly, but water available during the dry
season still accounts for only 29 percent of the annual total.
1.14        Hydrological data collection and processing has been severely
disrupted over the last two decades as a result of the destruction of many of
the weather and gauging stations, the abandonment of stations in areas of
unrest by the staff and the unavailability of funds to replace or maintain
equipment and to pay staff. There is currently a serious need for a revised
integrated national development plan for the management of water resources.
This will require updating the existing studies for each watershed to assess



- 6 -
available surface and groundwater resources, and present and future water
needs for municipal/industrial and irrigation needs. Plans will have to be
drawn up for an integrated development program for water use and, where
needed, institutional reorganization will have to be introduced to ensure
sustainable systems. With the support of the Emergency Reconstruction and
Rehabilitation Project (ERRP) the Government is preparing a Water Management
Strategy and is considering the establishment of a National Water Resources
Authority, which would be the highest water resources policy formulating body
and would also supervise the implementation of the development plan ensuring
the enforcement of policy and compliance with norms and regulations.
1.15        Water Sector Institutions.  The Ministry of Hydraulic and
Electrical Resources (MOHER) is the main institution involved in irrigation
through: (i) the Directorate General of Hydraulic and Electrical Equipment
(DGEHE), which has overall responsibility for water development planning and
the development of natural water resources including irrigation through its
Irrigation Service; and (ii) the Directorate General for Operations. The
Litani River Authority (LRA) is a public corporation, created in 1954 for the
multi-purpose development of the Litani river basin and is governed by a Board
of Directors nominated by the Council of Ministers. The LRA is in charge of
irrigation and hydropower production in the Litani river basin and for
hydraulic flow measurements throughout the entire country.
1.16        Nineteen Regional Water Authorities (RWA) and 209 Reaional Water
Committees (RWC), come under the administrative supervision of the MOHER's
Directorate General for Operations, and are responsible for water supply
management for both domestic and irrigation water, including systems operation
and maintenance (O&M). Although, RWAs and RWCs are autonomous commercial
enterprises, the revenues they derive from water sales are not sufficient to
cover O&M expenses, with Government hitherto providing annual subsidies to
cover the deficit which it has decided to discontinue this year. In line with
its water sector policy (Annex 16), the Government of Lebanon (GOL) intends to
restructure water management institutions. RWAs and RWCs will be grouped into
8 Regional Authorities. Four would be responsible for water supply and
sanitation, and four Regional Water Irrigation Authorities (RWIAs) would be in
charge of irrigation. Water Users' Associations (WUAs), organized around the
selection and hiring of ditch riders (Shawi) at the village or irrigation
module level, are operational in most irrigation schemes. They are handling
O&M on the tertiary systems. During the war, they often made up for the
deficiency of Water Authorities and Committees by going beyond routine
maintenance and taking charge of rehabilitation and sometimes of construction
of tertiary infrastructures, through community work. However, in some of the
schemes, there is now a need for intermediate institutions (Schemes Water
Management Committees(SWMC)) to facilitate the dialogue among all the users of
the same irrigation water in order to solve conflicts, organize water
distribution and progressively take over O&M responsibility from RWAs and
RWIAs. The Ministry of Agriculture's Green Plan (GP) (para. 1.11) is
responsible for small hydraulic works and farm-level infrastructure.
1.17        The Council for Development and Reconstruction (CDR), attached to
the Office of the President of the Council of Ministers, is a financially
autonomous public institution entrusted, since 1977 with the task of preparing
a national reconstruction strategy. It has recently prepared and has the
responsibility of implementing, in conjunction with other institutions, a



National Emergency Reconstruction Program (NERP), which includes, inter alia,
the preparation, execution and monitoring of projects for the rehabilitation
of irrigation infrastructure, and coordination of the studies to prepare the
Water Management Strategy. The CDR manages the Bank-financed Emergency
Reconstruction and Rehabilitation Project (ERRP).
1.18        Existing Irrigation Schemes and Future Developments. Existing
irrigation schemes have essentially been unchanged since the 1970s. They
include about 10 medium and large schemes, in addition to more than 50 small
schemes covering about 87,000 ha. Of the total irrigated area, about 25,000
ha have modern or semi-modern irrigation networks while the remainder receive
water from traditional systems, all of which are in need of urgent
rehabilitation and improvements. Of the area presently under irrigation, over
47,000 ha are irrigated from surface water supplies, and the remainder from
groundwater abstracted from private wells. The irrigation systems consist of
either small diversion weirs on rivers and streams or direct offtakes from
streams and springs with conveyance being by open concrete-lined main canals
and earth or concrete secondary canals. The poor state of the irrigation
infrastructure and inadequate management, however, limit the present potential
of efficient use of available water resources. New irrigation developments
had started by the implementation of the first phases of Litani Basin
Development by the completion of the Karaoun Dam (1965) and the construction
of irrigation pumping stations, 14 km of main canal and first phase
pressurized network for sprinkler irrigation of 700 ha (1974) which, however,
due to the ensuing hostilities have never been commissioned. Future
developments are expected to include an additional 51,000 ha concentrated
mainly in south Lebanon, the Bekaa Plain and the northern coastal areas.
D. Rationale for Bank Involvement
1.19        The Bank approved in March 1993 the Emergency Reconstruction and
Rehabilitation Project (ERRP), designed to finance high priority components of
the Government's National Emergency Reconstruction Program (NERP). The
proposed Irrigation Rehabilitation and Modernization Project (IRMP) would
support the irrigation sub-sector component of the program. It would assist
the GOL to rehabilitate destroyed, damaged, or neglected irrigation
infrastructures, and would be in line with the Bank-assistance strategy in
Lebanon, which focuses on: (i) helping to rebuild and strengthen a public
administration to establish an enabling environment for private sector
activity; (ii) rehabilitation of selected physical infrastructure in close
coordination with private sector initiatives; and (iii) addressing social
needs and environmental concerns. The project design benefited from the
Bank's experience in irrigation projects analyzed in an Operation and
Evaluation Department Special Study and is consistent with the draft
guidelines on the formulation of Water Management Strategies and with the
Region's Water Resources Paper.
II. THE PROJECT AREA
A. General Characteristics
2.01        Location. Climate and Soils. The overall project area of about
27,000 ha is spread over several priority locations. A first group of five
irrigation schemes, comprising about 17,200 ha, has been selected on basis of



- 8 -
the magnitude of war damage or intensity of the social needs: the Yammounsh
scheme (5,600 ha), located in the north-eastern part of the Central Bekaa
valley; the ouasmieh-Ras El Ain scheme (3,800 ha), located in the southern
coastal area of the country, to irrigate the narrow plain extending from Saida
in the North to Ras Nakoura in the South; the pannive scheme (5,000 ha),
located on the north-western flank of Mount Lebanon, 20 Km east of the town of
Tripoli; the Akkar El Bared scheme (800 ha) located in the northern coastal
area; and the South Bekaa scheme (2,000 ha), located on the western flank of
the Anti Liban mountain, on the left bank of the Litani river, upstream of the
Karaoun dam. A second group of schemes comprising about 10,000 ha, would be
selected from 28 schemes totaling 27,000 ha, distributed in the various
regions of the country.
2.02        Climatic conditions in the subproject areas vary from the
Continental type in Yammouneh, Danniye and South Bekaa, with average rainfalls
of around 400 mm/year, to Mediterranean in Quasmieh-Ras El Ain, and Akkar El
Bared, with an average annual rainfall of about 800 mm and relative mild
winters. Soils in the project area are of the colluvial type; they are deep,
fairly well-drained and suitable for irrigated agriculture.
2.03        Population. Target Group and Land Tenure.  The Yammouneh scheme
area has a farming population of about 41,000 in 3,630 extended households.
Presently, only about half of the population resides permanently in the area,
with many of the young people migrating to urban areas in the summer. Per
capita and farm incomes, about $508 per capita, have become among the lowest
in Lebanon since the prohibition of cannabis and poppy growing. Small farmers
are by far the largest population group in the Yammouneh scheme, representing
99 percent of the rural population of the area and farming over 80 percent of
the agricultural land. The agricultural population in the Ouasmieh Ras El Ain
scheme area is about 8,000 in 1,200 households whose incomes depend heavily on
agriculture. The agricultural population in Dannive 5,000 ha, is about 83,000
persons in 9,300 households. The average farm size is about 2 ha. The project
would target small farms, average 0.5 ha, at the bottom of the valley. For
these small farms, family labor provides more than 90 percent of the labor
requirements. In Akkar El Bared the total population targeted by the project
is estimated at about 1,900 persons in 170 households. Small farms, less than
1 ha, represent 64 percent of total. In South Bekaa, the land is extremely
fragmented, with 2,000 ha owned by 6,900 farmers (41,300 people). Many of
these fragmented farms are now consolidated under rental or share cropping
arrangements to allow their exploitation; actual farm sizes, therefore, vary
from 3 to 30 ha. Women participation in agricultural labor varies widely from
scheme to scheme and from village to village. This participation is low in
the coastal schemes, with less than 10 percent of women participating in
Quasmieh Ras El Ain and Akkar El Bared. In Danniye, women are involved in
fruit harvesting. In some villages of the Yammouneh area, up to 80 percent of
the women are involved in farming, especially in small animal husbandry.
2.04        Development Constraints and Potential. In the subprojects where
water is supplied by springs with high variations of seasonal flow, such as
Yammouneh and Danniye, the main constraint is the shortage of water for summer
crops (mainly vegetables and fruit trees). For the subprojects where water is
supplied by perennial rivers or artesian springs (Quasmieh Ras El Ain, Akkar
El Bared, and South Bekaa), the main constraint is the growing reliance on
costly and unsustainable underground water because of the poor condition of



- 9 -
the surface network. Competition and sometimes conflicts to get access to
meager resources in the former case and private investments in boreholes and
pumping stations in the latter are signs that irrigation water is in high
demand in the project area. Moreover, farmers in the Yammouneh area who have
lost a major source of income after the prohibition of papaver and cannabis
cultivation was enforced by the Government two years ago, have now a strong
incentive to intensify and diversify their production. In Quasmieh Ras El Ain
and Danniye, farmers are faced with the loss of a significant share of the
fruit export market, caused mainly by the periods of civil strife, and also by
a severe deterioration in produce quality standards. The establishment of
quality standards for exports would help Lebanese fruits to regain their
previous reputation and market.
B. Irrigation and Drainage Systems
2.05        General.  The existing irrigation and drainage systems are
generally between 30 and 50 years old and due to the conditions associated
with the civil war and acts of hostilities, very little maintenance work has
been possible, with actual war damage having been inflicted on various schemes
of the project. Although water resources would appear to be plentiful, the
distribution over the year is very unfavorable since only 24 percent of these
resources are available during the eight dry months of the year. Irrigation
conveyance efficiency is low with considerable water loss due to the poor
condition of deteriorated canals, and with water shortages being experienced,
at the end sections of systems due to violations and poor water management.
This situation is compounded by the inefficient on-farm water use.
2.06        Water Availability and Reguirements.  All the subproject areas
receive their primary irrigation water supplies from existing water management
infrastructure much of which is old and has deteriorated considerably over the
last two decades. The Yammouneh scheme is supplied by irrigation water from a
series of springs situated in synclinal structural depression along the
mountainous slope in the West Bekaa valley with an average annual flow of
about 60 to 80 mcm which is not regularly distributed during the year when
discharge drops to 10 percent of peak spring season flow in the dry months.
The main source of irrigation water for the Ouasmieh Ras El Ain is primarily
the Litani River System in addition to the Ras El Ain springs which are free
flowing artisan aquifers with a relatively stable flow. Groundwater is also
used to supplement the shortfall from canal supplies, particularly at the tail
end of the main canals with an estimated 20 percent of the Quasmieh scheme
relying on supplemental groundwater supplies. The Danniye scheme is irrigated
from two main and two secondary springs with large variations of flows between
spring and summer seasons (40 to 0.35 m3/sec). Due to poor condition of
conveyance system, 50 percent of water is lost in conveyance. The Akkar El
Bared scheme has a current irrigated area of 800 ha. Irrigation water is
diverted from the Nahr El Bared River by two diversion structures with about
16 mcm being diverted annually. Water use (20,000 m3 per ha) is considered
excessive due to large conveyance losses in the old lined main canal and
earthen secondary canals. The South Bekaa scheme was designed in the 1970. to
use water from the Karaoun reservoir. The main pumping station at the foot of
the dam was destroyed in 1982 shortly after its completion. Since then, the
main canal (13 km) has been used to store the groundwater pumped by private
pumps. The subproject includes the rehabilitation of part of the left bank
Litani Command in the Karaoun, Joub-Jannine areas forming 2,000 ha of the



- 10 -
total 8,600 ha of this command for which about 10 mcm per annum will be
supplied as part of the 44 mcm for the total left bank command and additional
4 mcm will be provided by groundwater.
2.07        Rapid initial assessment surveys have been carried out by
consultants for 28 small and medium irrigation schemes to determine suitable
areas to incorporate in the project. These include 10 locations in north
Lebanon (7,156 ha), 7 locations in Mount Lebanon (4,791 ha), 11 locations in
Bekaa (13,807 ha) and two locations in South Lebanon (292 ha). It is
anticipated that water availability and requirements will be verified by the
assessment based on which schemes will be considered for inclusion into the
project.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Proiect Obiectives
3.01        The main objectives of the project are to increase agricultural
production, agriculture-based income and employment in previously neglected
rural areas and to achieve a sustainable and improved management of water
resources, through (i) the rehabilitation of and adequate O&M of surface
irrigation infrastructure; and (ii) the provision of basic public support
services.
B. Proiect DescriPtion
3.02        The project consists of two major components: (a) rehabilitation
and modernization of surface irrigation infrastructure for selected irrigation
schemes covering an area of about 27,000 ha; and (b) provision of support to
public institutions involved in (i) irrigation and water management (ii)
agricultural support services and (iii) project implementation and
coordination.
3.03         The infrastructure rehabilitation component has been designed
under a program approach. Infrastructure rehabilitation works have been
divided into two groups according to socioeconomic priority, readiness for
implementation, and absence of resettlement or riparian issues: (i) a first
group of five schemes totaling about 17,200 ha for which feasibility studies
have been successfully completed: Yammouneh (5,600 ha), Quasmieh-Ras El Ain
(3,800 ha), Danniye (5,000 ha), Akkar El Bared (800 ha), and South Bekaa
(2,000 ha); and (ii) a program of small and medium schemes comprising about
10,000 ha to be selected from 28 schemes for which rapid initial assessment
studies have been completed.
3.04        Under institutional strengthening, support would be provided to
MOHER, MOA, the Agricultural Research Institute of Lebanon (ARIL), the Litani
River Authority (LRA) and the Green Plan (GP) to enhance their capacities,
rebuild their technical expertise and enable them to provide the necessary
technical support to insure the attainment of project benefits. This would
also enable these organizations to implement the project efficiently. The
support would include additional contractual staff, office equipment,
vehicles, training and technical assistance. Consultancy services and
technical assistance would also be provided for preparation of feasibility
studies, preparation of detailed designs and bidding documents and for
supervision of works.



- 11 -
C. Detailed Features
Irrization Infrastructure Rehabilitation and Modernization
3.05        The infrastructure rehabilitation component would support: the
rehabilitation and modernization of irrigation works and facilities; repair
and/or procurement of irrigation equipment and materials; and feasibility
studies for the program of small and medium schemes. The on-farm irrigation
improvement utilizing modern pressurized and localized irrigation systems for
all schemes would be left to the beneficiaries with water delivered at farm
outlets. The project would provide technical assistance and demonstration
equipment which may be rented to farmers to encourage adoption of advanced on-
farm irrigation systems.
3.06        Feasibility studies have been successfully completed for five
schemes totalling 17,200 ha. Detailed description of the schemes can be found
in Annexes 1 to 5.
(a)   The Yammouneh scheme (USS6.16 million, base cost) would include:
(i) improvements to tunnel entrance, head gates and critical
repairs to tunnel; (ii) repairs to covered canal, modernization of
20 turnouts on covered canal, sealing of about 30 outlets, and
replacement of gates for six offtake at head of chute; (iii)
construction of a new open chute to ensure efficient conveyance of
irrigation water from the end of the covered canal to the Bekaa
plain with a 3.5 m3/sec capacity, and stilling basin and
distribution system at Chifla; (iv) rehabilitation and repair of
concrete lining on four main canals (38 km) with structures and
gates; (v) repair or lining of concrete and earth canals in the
chute area (18 km) and secondary and tertiary canals (80 km) with
outlets and gates in the plain area; and (vi) rehabilitation of
the access road to the head of the chute.
(b)   The Ouasmieh-Ras El Ain scheme (USS7.85 million, base cost) would
include: (i) repair and reconstruction of the Zrarieh diversion
structure; (ii) reconstruction of damaged segments of the main
canals, with related structures and water control equipment; (iii)
reconstruction of the Zahrani siphons (750 m), to irrigate 300 ha
at the tail end; (iv) construction of a diversion weir on the
Litani to divert water to the existing pump station; (v)
rehabilitation of the existing pump station of Quasmieh and
provision of five new pump sets; (vi) installation of a high
voltage power line (15 km) to the Quasmieh pump station; (vii)
rehabilitation and concrete lining of secondary canals (25 km);
(viii) reconstruction and equipment of about 100 turnouts with new
calibrated offtake on the main canals and closing up about 300
unofficial offtakes; (ix) construction of 8 km of lateral canals
to feed directly into secondary or tertiary canals: and (x) other
works, such as repairs to the Quasmieh siphon, opening of
expropriated land along the main canals, construction of an 8 km
long service road to the Zrarieh diversion structure, weed
clearing and desilting of canals.



- 12 -
(C)   The Dannive scheme (USS4.92 million, base cost) would include: (i)
rehabilitation and construction of seven concrete main canals
(42.2 km) with related structures and gates to improve irrigation
and water availability to about 1,700 ha; (ii) rehabilitation and
construction of associated secondary canals (41.4 km) with outlets
and gates; (iii) improvements to headworks and urgent selective
repairs and rehabilitation for the remaining main canals of the
scheme covering an additional 3,300 ha of irrigated area; (iv)
containment and improved diversion for Buraisa spring; and (v)
construction of 10 km of service roads.
(d)   The Akkar El Bared scheme (USSl.83 million, base cost) would
include: (i) rehabilitation of the existing main canal (11.1 km)
feeding 1,600 ha of total irrigated area; (ii) rehabilitation of
El Abde branch canal (5.8 km); (iii) reconstruction of concrete
secondary canals to replace existing deteriorated concrete
channels (11.2 km) and earth channels (22.8 km) as well as repairs
to existing secondary concrete canals (5.7 km) feeding 800 ha; and
(iv) provision of water control equipment.
(e)   The South Bekaa scheme (USS12.25 million, base cost) would focus
on 2,000 ha, first phase development of 8,600 ha on left bank of
the Litani river, and would include:(i) rehabilitation of main
pumping station, water delivery pipeline and main canal with its
structures and water control system to eventually serve 8,600 ha
of irrigated area; (ii) rehabilitation of pumping stations
reservoirs and piped distribution network for irrigation water in
Karaoun I and II areas (700 ha); (iii) extension of main canal
(4.4 km); (iv) construction of new Joub Janine pumping station,
reservoir and irrigation water piped distribution system to
irrigate 1,300 ha; (v) provision and installation of equipment for
main pump station, water control equipment, pumping equipment,
tubewells and irrigation hydrants; and (vi) rehabilitation or
construction of 800 m2 of existing office and service buildings
and 500 m2 of workshops.
3.07        Small and Medium Irrigation Schemes Program.  Studies for Rapid
Initial Assessments (RIAs), covering 28 existing irrigation schemes with a
total irrigated area of about 27,000 ha, varying in size from about 50 ha to
about 2,900 ha each, have been successfully completed by local consultants.
These studies provide considerable valuable information about irrigation
schemes in different parts of Lebanon. The studies are currently being
reviewed by Government in consultation with the Bank to determine a selection
of an estimated 10,000 ha (US$14.47 million base cost) which would be included
for rehabilitation and modernization under the project. The areas selected
will be based on criteria reflecting a combination of economic rate of return,
rural poverty, and community participation. Assurances were obtained at
neqotiations that GOL would select sub-prolects on the basis of criteria
acceptable to IBRD. includina: (i) internal rate of return equivalent to at
least 12 percent; (ii) Promotion of community Particivation in the
rehabilitation: (iii) impact on rural income; (iv) absence of adverse impact
on the environment: and (v) absence of involuntary relocation of the local
Population rpara. 8.01 (a) and (Annex 17)1.



- 13 -
3.08        Proiect Facilities including Operation and Maintenance (USS4.85
million base cost). The project would support Regional Water Irrigation
Authorities (RWIAs) and Water Users' Associations (WUAs) which would be
responsible for 0&M, through providing office technology equipment for 5
offices, 25 vehicles, 59 motorcycles, and 8 loader-backhoe, as well as
incremental operating costs.
3.09        Technical Design. In designing the project more than one
technological alternative was considered. Depending on the location and state
of the irrigation scheme to be rehabilitated, both pressurized and gravity
water conveyance alternatives were examined. Replacing existing gravity, open
canal conveyance systems with pressurized piped systems, in Yammouneh,
Quasmieh and other schemes, did not present clear cut advantages. For many
farmers, a well implemented and operated gravity surface irrigation system is
sufficient to attain a relatively high and sustainable productivity while
providing high conveyance efficiency. The choice was, therefore, made to
rehabilitate the systems in such a way so that both the gravity and other
types of irrigation systems (sprinkler, bubbler, drip, etc) could be used by
farmers. In the south Bekaa scheme, the pressurized pipe conveyance chosen in
the original design of the scheme (1972) would be implemented as designed with
a gravity system being used for the first five years in transition to
sprinkler system. Sprinkler or localized irrigation would be possible in
other schemes with on-farm system implementation being carried out by the
beneficiaries. Farmers wishing to irrigate with sprinklers and drip
irrigation, for example, would build their own small farm storage ponds and
carry out on-farm irrigation by pressurization and conveyance using small
booster pumps. The exercise of this option would be left to the discretion of
farmers; they would have to invest in the necessary on-farm irrigation
equipment that would allow them to use any of the modern, water saving
irrigation techniques, with project providing technical assistance and
demonstration equipment.
Institutional Stren2thenin2
3.10        Irriaation and Water Management Institutions. The project would
support selected departments of MOHER, and LRA as well as Regional Water
Irrigation Authorities directly involved in the implementation and the
operation of the schemes.
(a)   Irrigation Services. The project would support: (i)  MOHER's
Irrigation Department (US$1.35 million, base cost), to supervise
and control execution of studies and construction work, by
financing office equipment, 2 vehicles, rehabilitation of offices,
studies and detail designs, technical assistance [78 staff months
(sm) of civil and irrigation engineers], training (overseas: 10.5
sm and local: 70 sm), incremental O&M of vehicles and office
equipment; (ii) LRA's Department of Studies and Proiect, (US$0.97
million, base cost), to supervise and control the studies and the
construction works for irrigation schemes in the Litani basin and
in South Lebanon, by providing office equipment, 3 vehicles,
rehabilitation of offices, technical assistance (51 sm of
engineers), training for engineers and technicians (overseas: 10.5
sm and local: 45 sm), incremental O&M of vehicles, building office
equipment; and (iii) MOA's Green Plan (US$0.77 million, base
cost), to strengthen present capacity to implement small schemes



- 14 -
through provision of office equipment, 6 vehicles, rehabilitation
of offices, studies for detailed designs of small schemes,
technical assistance (24 sm each of an irrigation and rural
engineer), training of engineers and technicians (10.5 am of
overseas training and 70 am of local training); and (iv) RWIA. RWC
and Water Users' Associations (US$0.70 million, base cost), would
be involved in the detailed design of the rehabilitation works and
will be responsible for O&M. The project would finance, 12 sm of
overseas training, 45 staff weeks of local training, workshops and

assistance (15 sm of expatriates and 204 sm of local consultants).
(b)   Environmental Monitorin . The project would strengthen MOHER and
LRA capability to monitor the quality of groundwater aquifers,
especially in the coastal areas where the danger of sea water
intrusion exists. It would also support the gathering and
interpretation of hydrogeological data in the project area (para.
1.14). The project would rehabilitate MOHER's Hvdroaeolooical
Department (US$0.50 million, base cost), to initiate a groundwater
monitoring program in the project area by providing laboratory and
office equipment, one vehicle, technical assistance (9 sm of local
and international consultants as computer specialist,
hydrogeologist and environmentalist), studies (Yammouneh storage
reservoirs), incremental O&M of vehicles, etc. The project would
also support LRA's Devartment of Water Resources (US$0.37 million,
base cost), by providing hydrometric and office equipment,
rehabilitation of office building, 2 vehicles, technical
assistance (7 sm of local computer specialist and international
hydrologist), and incremental O&M of vehicles and equipment.
3.11        Agricultural Services. The project would support the
rehabilitation of essential public services in applied research as well as
quality control of agricultural inputs and pesticide residues in agricultural
produce. The project would also finance an innovative approach to provide
farmers in the various subproject areas with on-farm technology testing and
extension services.
(a)   ADplied Research (US$1.88 million, base cost).  On-station applied
research would be conducted by ARIL and would focus, inter alia,
on subjects dealing with verification trials for new cereals,
pulses, vegetables and tree crops varieties and species,
irrigation techniques, plant disease control, integrated pest
management and soil investigations, mapping and classification,
etc. To enable ARIL to undertake the above research, the project
would finance: (i) the construction of a 250 m2 soil and plant
protection laboratory at Sour, 2,000 m2 of green house at Fanar;
(ii) the rehabilitation of the soils, plant protection and
irrigation labs (2,000 m2) at Tell Amara as well as 1,000 m2 of
green houses at Fanar; (iii) equipment, which would include
computers, laboratory, irrigation and field equipment as well as
vehicles; (iv) training in-country (42 sm) and abroad (10 sm);
(v) long-term technical assistance (144 sm) and short term
consultancies (6 sm), would be provided for programming, analysis
of research results and training of local staff.



- 15 -
(b)   On-Farm Technoloov Testing and Extension (US$1.43 million, base
cost). At present, the public extension service is not fully
operational; therefore there is an opportunity to develop a
lighter system geared to the needs of small farmers. In order to
ensure that expected project benefits (increased crop productivity
and cultural intensification) are attained, the project would
finance demand-driven On-Farm Technology Testing and Extension
(OFTT-Extension) services, which would complement the existing,
private, supply-driven extension services. These would be
specific to each of the Bubproject areas, but would encompass all
agricultural activities (irrigated and rainfed crop productions,
including fruit trees, livestock, bee keeping and silk production)
in the area with a special emphasis on small farms. Possible
topics would include, among others, supplemental irrigation and
mechanization of wheat and pulses productions (Yammouneh);
improvement of on-farm irrigation techniques and Integrated Pest
Management (IPM) Techniques etc. In keeping with the project
objectives of increasing farm income, OFTT-Extension would also
focus on women income generating activities (small animal
husbandry, processing of agricultural products and handicraft
activities, etc.). Implementation of OFTT-Extension activities
would be contracted out to autonomous, mostly private institutions
such as ICARDA's Terbol station, consulting firms or consultants,
and universities, which would conduct on-farm demonstrations and
field testing of relevant technologies as well as organize
information field seminars for farmers and their families (para
4.12). OFTT-Extension activities would be financed through an
OFTT-Extension facility under the control of MOA. In addition to
the long term consultant in charge of OFTT-Extension planning and
monitoring, the project would finance 5 sm of expatriate expertise
in OFTT-Extension (research/development and extension), 4 em of
overseas training for MOA's extension staff, 2 computers, the
purchase and production of extension documentation and materials
(leaflets, audio-video messages, etc.), 2 cars and incremental
recurrent costs for the operation and maintenance of office
equipment and vehicles. For the testing and demonstration of
agricultural technologies the project would finance, from the
OFTT-Extension facility, the procurement of equipment and inputs
required by the contractors for the conduct of the
testing/demonstration and extension activities.
(c)   Ouality Control of Agricultural Inputs and Pesticide Residues
Monitoring (US$0.61 million base cost). Farm input quality
control and pesticide use regulations are adequate and in
compliance with international norms. However, they are not
applied for lack of equipment and skilled personnel. To implement
farm input quality control regulations, and enforce quality
standards on agricultural produce (pesticide residues), the
project would finance the rehabilitation of two ARIL laboratories
at Fanar, where pesticide formulation, residues and chemical
fertilizer analyses are done. The project would also finance the
procurement of laboratory equipment as well as two 2X4 pick ups,
for sample collection and field inspections, 3 and 7 sm each of
expatriate and local technical assistance and 8 sm of training
overseas for laboratory technicians. In addition to the above,



- 16 -
the project would enhance the capabilities of MOA's planned Plant
Protection and Quarantine Services Department, by financing the
procurement of one computer and accessories, two 2X4 double cabin
pick ups and 4 sm of overseas training. Costs of incremental
recurrent expenditures for operation and maintenance of vehicles,
buildings and laboratories would also be financed under the
project. The project will not be involved in the procurement of
pesticides.
3.12        Support to Proiect Implementation (US$0.25 million, base cost).
The project would complement the existing MOHER's Water Sector Implementation
Unit (MOHER) by providing a computer, a vehicle and 12 em of a consultant
irrigation agronomist.
Status of Proiect Preparation
3.13        During initial Bank identification mission in July 1993 and the
subsequent joint FAO/CP and Bank preparation mission in October 1993, six
irrigation schemes had been identified and selected from among the
Government's proposed priorities based on intensity of war damages and social
priorities: Yammouneh, Quasmieh-Ras El Ain, Danniye, Akkar El Bared, South
Bekaa, and Adonis. The feasibility studies for these schemes were prepared by
local consultants financed under a Japanese PHRD Grant, and were reviewed by
the appraisal mission. They showed that the first-five schemes are feasible
and have, therefore, been incorporated into the project. However, the study on
Adonis indicated that it should rather be considered in combination with a
water supply project. Preparation of detailed designs and bidding documents
for the five schemes is underway. These are financed by the Government, and
are expected to be completed before loan effectiveness.
3.14        During the same period, Rapid Initial Assessments for 28 schemes
totaling 27,000 ha, have also been carried out by local consultants financed
by the same Grant. Out of these schemes, a program of about ten small and
medium schemes, covering about 10,000 ha, will be selected for financing under
the project. As the next step of the selection process, MOHER is launching a
number of socio-economic studies to assess the social conditions (poverty
assessment) and the efficiency of existing water users' associations. These
studies for the selected schemes are expected to be completed by the end of
June, 1994. Feasibility studies would then be carried out the selected
schemes during the first year of the project.
D. Prolect Costs and Financing
Proiect Costs
3.15        Total project costs including physical and price contingencies are
estimated at US$70.5 million, of which 85 percent or US$59.8 million, are in
foreign exchange. Project cost estimates for civil works have been based on:
(a) detailed quantities for the main irrigation infrastructure works schemes
for which draft tender documents have been prepared by consultants (Quasmieh
Ras El Ain, Yammouneh, Danniye, Akkar El Bared and South Bekaa schemes)
amounting to about 70 percent of total cost of irrigation infrastructure; and
(b) for the remaining irrigation infrastructure, cost estimates have been
based on prevailing per hectare development costs (US$1,300/ha equivalent).
Unit rates were obtained from various contractors, suppliers and quotations
for equipment and consulting firms. The cost estimates for the five schemes
under (a) above have been refined during appraisal and agreement has been
reached with the Government to proceed with the detailed designs and



- 17 -
preparation of bidding documents for the five schemes which is to be financed
by the Government. Feasibility studies for a selected number of schemes,
subject of the rapid initial assessment, will be carried out from which
eventually a 10,000 ha area will be included in the project using a program
approach. All cost estimates are expressed in May 1994 prices with base costs
converted at the floating rate of 1,690 Lebanese Pounds (L.L.) to the US
Dollar.
3.16        Physical contingencies included in the project cost estimate are
based on the degree of preparation and firmness of details available, as
follows: 8 percent for main and secondary irrigation network, 5 percent for
buildings, studies and consultant services, 10 percent on civil works for
small schemes, O&M equipment, agriculture and miscellaneous activities and 10
percent for other equipment. No physical contingencies were applied for
technical assistance, office facilities and vehicles. Total physical
contingencies amount to US$4.8 million. Costs due to expected price increases
over the implementation period amount to about (US$ 4.5 million) based on the
following annual inflation rates.

Financing Plan
3.18                    The proposed Bank loan of US$57.2 million would finance about 81
percent of the project costs net of duties and taxes, and would meet most of
foreign costs. The International Fund for Agricultural Development (IFAD) has
been approached by the Government and is considering parallel financing of the
project. However, at the request of the Government, the financing plan does
not take into account a possible IFAD contribution. Should IFAD financing
become available the GOL may either ask the Bank to cancel part of the IBRD
loan or propose to use the proceeds of the loan to finance additional
irrigation schemes. The proposed financing plan is as follows:

E. Procurement and Disbursement
Procurement
3.19        As the project implementing agencies' experience in procurement is
very limited, the CDR, which has shown a good procurement performance under
the on-going Bank financed ERRP (para. 1.17), would be responsible for
procurement under the project with the technical support of MOHER and MOA
(para. 4.01). All goods and works to be financed from the Bank loan proceeds
would be procured in accordance with the Bank's Guidelines for Procurement
under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits. The procedures to be adopted are set out
below and procurement arrangements are summarized in Table 3.3 and Annex 8.
3.20        Civil Works.   The rehabilitation and modernization of irrigation
infrastructure for at least five main schemes valued at about US$5.0 million
each, would be suitable for International Competitive Bidding (ICB), and would
represent about 50 percent of the total civil works. For the medium-sized
irrigation schemes, particularly where geographical proximity would permit the
grouping of schemes into packages having each a value of US$3.0 million or
more and which represent about a further 15 percent of civil works,
procurement would also be by ICB. For these larger civil works contracts,
contractors would be prequalified. Civil works for smaller projects, where
packaging would not be practical, and for building contracts which would be
scattered throughout the project areas, Local Competitive Bidding (LCB)
procedures, acceptable to the Bank would be applied, using standardized
bidding documents modelled after the Bank's Standard Bidding Documents for
smaller works. Implementation of rehabilitation of small and scattered
irrigation schemes not exceeding 150 ha in area, or rehabilitation of minor
tertiary canals, would not be feasible through normal contracting procedures.
In such areas, and on exceptional basis, rehabilitation would be through
direct contracting either with the local communities and their small local
contractors, or by force account by the Green Plan, according to procedures
agreed with the Bank. These works would include any small pilots and trial
areas early in the project cycle and tertiary systems connected on farmers'
facilities. These works would be in small packages not exceeding US$100,000
equivalent each, with an aggregate amount not exceeding US$1,000,000
equivalent.
3.21        Ecruinment and Materials.  All equipment and materials estimated to
cost more than US$150,000 equivalent per contract, mainly O&M equipment,
specialized hydro-mechanical and agricultural equipment and vehicles would be
procured under ICB procedures. For more economical procurement, the
requirements of the project would be packaged, specifying the number of items
for each scheme or department. Lebanese manufacturers competing under ICB
would receive a preference in bid evaluation of 15 percent of the CIF price or
the prevailing customs duty applicable to non-exempt importers, whichever is
less, provided they can prove that the value added to the product in Lebanon
exceeds 20 percent of ex-factory bid price. International shopping (IS) with
at least three quotations from three eligible source countries will be used
for contracts estimated to cost less than US$150,000 equivalent each, up to an
aggregate amount of US$0.6 million equivalent. Local shopping (LS) will be



- 20 -
used for off-the-shelf items costing less than US$50,000 per package up to an
aggregate amount of US$0.4 million equivalent. Direct Contracting (DC), under
procedures acceptable to the Bank, will be allowed for items of a proprietary
nature or items required to ensure compatibility with the existing equipment
up to an aggregate amount of US$1 million equivalent.
3.22        Consultancv assianments will involve both individual consultants
and consulting firms, mostly those hired to support the project implementation
agencies and to organize and conduct various training programs. The selection
of consultants will be in accordance with the Guidelines for the Use of
Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing Agency
(August 1981), using the Bank's standardized Letter of Invitation (LOI) and
Sample Form of Contract for Consultants' Services. There will be a total of
about 28 short term assignments with a total value of US$2.6 million.
Specific details of various consultancy assignments appear in Annex 9.
Training services (US$2.0 million) will be obtained mostly through consulting
organizations associated with the project or through specialized UN agencies,
such as FAO, and universities. Three local consulting firms have provided
services during the preparation and appraisal stages for the various project
areas. Selected by the Government under procedures acceptable to the Bank,
these three firms are competent and are currently involved with the main
schemes under the project. Their services could be retained under the project
for engineering services for the main schemes as this work does not require
expatriate expertise; local consultants are efficient and their use will
enable rapid implementation of the schemes.
3.23        All contracts awarded through ICB, as well as all civil works
contracts above US$1 million equivalent, goods and equipment contracts
estimated to cost more than US$150,000 equivalent, and all consultant
appointments above US$100,000 will be subject to prior Bank review. Civil
works contracts costing between USS 0.5 million and US$1 million equivalent
would be subject to full post-award review. All other contracts will be
subject to selective ex-post review by the Bank. These limits would result in
prior review of over 80 percent of total procurement.
3.24        Procurement information would be collected and recorded as
follows:
(a)   prompt reporting of contract award information by the borrower;
(b)   comprehensive quarterly reports to the Bank by the borrower,
indicating:
(i)   revised cost estimates for individual contracts and
the total project, including best estimates of
allowances for physical and price contingency;
(ii) revised timing of procurement actions, including
advertising, bidding, contract award, and completion
time for individual contracts; and
(iii) compliance with aggregate limits on specified methods of
procurement.

Disbursement
3.25         The proposed Bank loan of USS 57.2 million would be disbursed
against the categories and at rates indicated in Annex 10 and Table 3.4
Disbursement would be made against full documentation, except for payment
against contracts of less than US$50,000 equivalent, which would be reimbursed
against statement of expenditures (SOEs), for which the related documentation
would be retained by CDR. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that, for
all expenses made on the basis of SOEs, GOL would: (i) maintain or cause to be
maintained records and accounts reflecting such expenditures; (ii) ensure that
all records evidencing such expenditures are retained until at least one year
after IBRD has received the last audit report; (iii) have the records and
accounts of the SOEs. including those for the Special Account, for each fiscal
year, audited by independent auditors acceptable to IBRD, and (iv) furnish to
IBRD the audit reports, including a separate opinion on the SOEs, no later
than six months after the end of each fiscal year rpara. 8.01 (b)l.
3.26         Given the long interruption in lending to Lebanon, there is no
disbursement profile for the country. The implementation period for the
project is estimated at six years as shown in Annex 13. The estimate is based
on the duration of contracts, specified construction methods and
implementation experience of CDR. Project implementation will use
institutions and procedures already in place for the Emergency Reconstruction



- 22 -
and Rehabilitation Project, which has a four-year implementation period (para
4.1). It is anticipated that disbursements would be completed by June 30,
2001, about six months after scheduled project completion. The disbursement
profile closely follows the disbursement for Bank-wide water sector loans. A
schedule of estimated disbursements is detailed in Annex 10, and summarized in
Table 3.4 below.

3.27          Special Account. To facilitate disbursement against eligible
expenditures, the Government would establish a Special Account in the Central
Bank and operated by CDR under terms and conditions satisfactory to the IBRD.
The IBRD would make an initial deposit of US$1.5 million in the special
account. The CDR would submit replenishment applications for the special
account on a monthly basis, or when about half of the initial deposit has been
utilized, whichever comes first. The replenishment applications would be
supported by the necessary documentation, bank statement of the special
account and a reconciliation bank statement of the special account against
Bank records.
