F.  Accounts. Audits and Reports
3.28          MOHER, MOA, CDR, ARIL, GP and affected RWAs are Government
agencies, and LRA is established under the law governing public authorities.
The accounts of all Government agencies and corporations are subjected to
audits by the Government Accounting Office (GAO). In addition to its ex-post
audits of all public expenditures, GAO's responsibilities also include ex-ante
control over public expenditures above LL 5 million (equivalent to about
US$3,000 at the current exchange rate). However, because of the war, GAO's
capability is very limited at present. The Government intends to review the
functions of GAO and is considering using professional accounting firms to
carry financial audits under contract with GAO.
3.29          The CDR will keep separate accounts for project expenditures in
accordance with accounting principles and practices acceptable to the IBRD.
It will have ultimate responsibility for project budgeting and accounting and
would coordinate project activities executed by the various institutions
concerned, as well as consolidate the accounts. The CDR would also prepare
reimbursement requests and forward them to the IBRD, as well as ensure that
up-to-date information on expenditure and disbursements would be available to
the IBRD supervision missions.
3.30          Assurances were obtained at negotiations that CDR would: (i)
have its records. accounts and financial statements for each fiscal year
audited, in accordance with appropriate auditing Principles by independent
auditors acceptable to the IBRD: (ii) furnish to the IBRD no later than six
month after the end of each year. certified copies of its financial statements
for such year and the reports of such audits rfara. 8.01(c)l.
G. Environmental Aspects
3.31          The project is expected to have positive environmental effects
as a result of its activities as follows:
(a)    Rehabilitation would reduce the present use of groundwater from
private tubewell; this is particularly important in the Quasmieh
Ras El Ain irrigation scheme where over-pumping would eventually
lead to a dangerous intrusion of saline water into the
freshwater table. The project would address the subject by
providing an alternative to groundwater in Quasmieh-Ras El Ain,
regulating groundwater use in South Bekaa and making the users
responsible for correct use of irrigation water. At several
schemes, due to their poor operational condition and non-
utilization, long reaches of canals are transformed into garbage
collection dumps which rehabilitation would eliminate;
(b)    Ineffective pest control has been identified amongst priorities
that would be addressed by the strengthening of agricultural
institutions. Applied research activities would be geared to the
formulation and the demonstration of Integrated Pest Management
(IPM) practices for major irrigated crops to be rapidly adopted



- 24 -
by farmers. This would lead to a reduction in the use of
environmentally damaging pesticides, and a possible
contamination in the food chain. The creation of the office for
quality control, to become operational in the screening of
inputs and the assessment of pesticides residues on agricultural
products, goes in the same direction. Farmers training programs
would focus on potential damage from pesticides abuse and
misuse, as well as on benefits from the adoption of IPM
techniques; and
(c)    There are numerous sources of water contamination, such as
domestic sewage and solid waste and also agricultural chemicals.
Water pollution from nitrates has been reported from analysis
carried out at Tel Amara. At present, there are no standards
applied in respect of irrigation water quality. The
municipalities have virtually no power to control water
pollution and no resources to monitor the quality of irrigation
water. Strengthening of the soil and water laboratories would
allow for the adoption of fertilizer management advice based on
site specific recommendations, which should aim at reducing
present excessive use of fertilizers. The project would ensure
that investments proposed for such areas would address this
issue as part of the process of environmental screening and
assessment during the preparation of detailed designs.
3.32          The project would support institutional strengthening and
training activities for environmental monitoring at the national and local
levels. Support would be provided for the MOHER's Hydrogeological Department
and to LRA's Department of Water Resources (para. 3.10 b).
IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Institutional Arrangements
Implementation Arrangements
4.01          General.  To ensure the IRMP's full integration in the National
Emergency Reconstruction Program (NERP) and its full coordination with the on-
going Emergency Reconstruction and Rehabilitation Project (ERRP), the proposed
project would be managed by CDR. CDR would be assisted by the Program
Management Unit (PMU) established under the ERRP. Actual project execution
would be carried out by MOHER and MOA, assisted by the Water Sector
Implementation Unit (WSIU). This unit was recently established in MOHER to
implement the water sector program of the NERP and has the mandate and the
capability to handle irrigation. Procurement and disbursement under the
project would be handled by the CDR's PMU (paras 3.19 and 3.27). This would
avoid the cumbersome and time-consuming procurement procedures under standard
government regulations and would benefit from the streamlined procurement
procedures of the CDR. The PMU would also keep the project's accounts and
manage the Special Account.



- 25 -
4.02          Execution of the Irri2ation Rehabilitation Component would be
the responsibility of the existing MOHER's Water Sector Implementation Unit
(WSIU). The WSIU is under the authority of the Director General for Hydraulic
and Electric Equipment and is assisted by a team of international consultants
(WSIUC) financed for a period of three years by the European Union (EU). The
team leader of the WSIUC is the manager of the WSIU. Three Deputy Managers
have been nominated by MOHER to work as counterparts to the WSIU manager and,
after completion of the WSIU contract, are intended to become managers of
their respective water subsectors: water supply, wastewater and irrigation
programs. The Head of MOHER's Irrigation Service has been designated as deputy
manager for the irrigation subsector. This will ensure the appropriate
coordination with the water supply and wastewater rehabilitation projects
implemented by the same Sector Implementation Unit (SIU). Rehabilitation
works would be contracted out to private contractors (para. 3.20).
4.03          The WSIU would have full responsibility and accountability for
the technically related project features and would be responsible for the
following tasks: (i) preparation of feasibility studies for specific schemes
through consultants and preparatory work for the recruitment of consultants;
(ii) supervision of the preparation of detailed engineering and tender
documents and contract packaging; (iii) management of consultants; (iv) all
technical aspects of procurement of goods and services; (v) monitoring of
civil works contracts and project supervision; (vi) periodic reporting; (vii)
management of studies and actions required for organizational and
institutional development, and adequate cost recovery; (viii) liaison and
coordination with MOA, LRA and RWAs. The WSIUC consists of the following
units: technical services, contract management, program monitoring and
control, and administration, already staffed by five international consultants
in addition to the team leader financed by the EU. The proposed project would
complement EU assistance by providing an irrigation agronomist, as well as a
four wheel drive vehicle and a computer. Assurances were obtained at
negotiations that CDR would (i) implement the proiect through its PMU and
through MOHER's WSIU, under arrangements similar to those already in place for
the ERRP: (iil put in place alternative implementation arrangements in
agreement with IBRD, in case that any of the above units ceases to exist, and
(iii) would recruit no later than December 31. 1994. an irrigation agronomist
to assist MOHER in the carrying out of its project's function, on terms of
reference satisfactory to IBRD rparas. 8.01 (dland (ell.
4.04          Operation and Maintenance of main headwork, main and secondary
canals, would be initially under the responsibility of existing Water
Authorities and Water Committees and would be automatically transferred to the
new Regional Water Irrigation Authorities (LRA, North Lebanon, North Bekaa,
Mount Lebanon) as soon as they are established (Annex 16). Water users
associations would be responsible for O&M of tertiary canals. The
Government's Letter of Irrigation Sector Policy (Annex 16) indicates that the
composition of the board of the new Regional Water Irrigation Authorities will
be revised to include representatives of farmers. Project implementation
would lead, after rehabilitation of the selected schemes, to their substantial
self-management and self-financing. In line with GOL's policy, present scheme
management and administration procedures would be revised with the purpose of



- 26 -
introducing tighter management control, stricter financial control and greater
accountability. To ensure efficient management and to establish fiscal
accountability, the GOL intends to establish Regional Water Irrigation
Authorities (RWIAs) to handle O&M of headworks, main and secondary canals and
organize cost recovery. RWIA will have a lean structure and will contract out
O&M to private contractors and would progressively transfer their
responsibilities to autonomous Scheme Water Management Committees (SWMC).
Water user.' representation in the boards of the RWIAs will ensure control of
their operating expenses.
4.05          Water Users' Involvement.  Although farmers are currently
involved in the operation of the schemes through unofficial Water Users'
Associations organized around the Shawis (para 1.16), they are not involved in
the management of the schemes. This has two major consequences: (i) farmers
are not aware of the real cost of irrigation O&M, especially when they see no
improvement in water distribution and are, therefore, sometimes reluctant to
pay the corresponding water charges, and (ii) simple O&M tasks, which could be
more effectively handled by farmers themselves, are sometimes neglected by the
Authorities. To increase the role and responsibilities of farmers in the
management of downstream irrigation systems, measures need to be taken to
allow for their involvement in the management of schemes through the support
and empowerment of existing WUAs grouping farmers at the outlet or village
level.
4.06          The rehabilitation sequence would include, as a necessary step,
beneficiaries' review and approval of rehabilitation proposals; this dialogue
would start at preparation and appraisal stage, for schemes yet to be
selected. To that effect, MOHER is launching social studies in all 28 schemes
to evaluate social priorities, and asses the willingness of the population to
participate in the project. As part of the feasibility studies, MOHER or LRA
personnel would visit the schemes and explain the engineering rehabilitation
plans to farmers' representatives, and Shawis, at village level. If the
farmers' representatives wish the works to go forward, they would, through the
RWA, formally approve the plans, and commit themselves and the farmers they
represent to using their own resources to fully finance and to carry out the
scheme maintenance as foreseen in the plans. In selected complex schemes like
Yammouneh, a Scheme Water Management Committee (SWMC) would be created. The
SWMC would comprise representatives from the Water Authority and
representatives designated by the WUAs. The SWMC would make decisions based
on discussions in which users' representatives would voice their demands; and
users would receive guidance on management of the secondary and tertiary
networks.
4.07          During project implementation, RWAs and RWIAs would support the
WUAs in the operation and maintenance of the tertiary system, through: (a)
informing water users on O&M and cost-recovery procedures; (b) training the
Shawis in the O&M of the rehabilitated structures; and (c) regular supervision
and assistance to the Shawis. As execution of the civil works nears
completion, the WSIU and the RWIAs would prepare a manual of O&M which would
describe in detail the maintenance and other operations and provide adequate
training. They would also propose an annual O&M program that would detail the



- 27 -
division of responsibilities and functions for O&M between RWIAs and WUAs.
Assurances were obtained at negotiations that CDR. in coordination with MOHER.
would prepare and submit to the Bank an O&M program for the first year of the
proiect no later than July 31. 1995 and that implementation of the Plan would
commence no later than December 1995 rpara. 8.01 (ffl.
4.08          Execution of Agricultural Support Activities would be the
responsibility of MOA. However, the volume of work in implementing the
agricultural support activities sub-component of the IRMP does not warrant the
creation of a specific SIU at MOA. The local technical assistant in charge of
the OFTT-Extension at MOA and one of the local technical assistants to be
hired by ARIL would, with some assistance from the SIU at MOHER, prepare the
required documentation for tendering project financed procurement and
consultant services, etc. Procurement and disbursement would be handled by the
CDR's PMU.
4.09          On-Station Agricultural Research would be executed by ARIL's
staff and the four applied research experts to be financed by the project
would be recruited before March 31, 1995. Assurances to this effect were
obtained at negotiations [vara 8.01 (all. After their recruitment, the
experts would each produce an applied research program aimed at resolving the
immediate problems facing Lebanese agriculture in general and those of the
subproject areas in particular. ARIL's research would be coordinated with the
OFTT-Extension activities by the MOA group in charge of evaluating the
performance of the OFTT-Extension contractors.
4.10          On-Farm Technology Testing and Extension (OFTT-Extension)
activities would be implemented through multi-year service contracts with
private institutions or consultants such as the American University of Beirut
Housh Sneid station, Saint Joseph University's Tanayel station, ICARDA's
Terbol station, NGOs, foreign universities or development institutions,
consulting firms or private consultants. Planning (formulation of TORs,
evaluation of consultants proposals, etc.), supervision and monitoring of this
activity would be undertaken by a long term expert, to be financed by the
project. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that the Extension
Specialist would be recruited before June 30. 1995 rvara. 8.01 (a)1.
4.11          Quality Control of Inputs and Produce.  Input and agricultural
produce quality control activities would be conducted by the inspectors and
assistants of MOA's Department of Quality Control and Quarantine Services and
by laboratory staff of ARIL at Fanar. Staff of MOA's Department of Quarantine
Services would inspect produce packing houses, markets and input supply
outlets, collect product samples, and have them analyzed at ARIL's
laboratories at Fanar. Traders, farmers, public health institutions and
private agro-processors and exporters are also expected to have the Fanar
laboratories analyze their produce and input samples for a fee. Farmers and
traders have expressed their interest for this service.
Coordination Arranaements
4.12          Technical Coordination Committee. Overall coordination of
project implementation would be under the responsibility of the Technical
Coordination Committee (TCC) established under the ERRP and composed of the
PMU and of NERP's SIUs. MOA, GP and ARIL would be invited to join TCC
meetings on an ad-hoc basis. The main functions of the TCC in its dealings
with the IRMP would be to: (i) ensure coordination with the ERRP projects on
water supply, waste water management and solid waste management; and (b)



- 28 -
coordinate with other Government Departments and resolve problems that might
emerge during execution. Assurances were obtained at necotiations that the
TCC would coordinate the implementation of the IRMP with the implementation of
the ERRP and that representatives of the MOA. GP. LRA and ARIL would be
invited to attend the meetings of the TCC fpara. 8.01 (hfl.
B. Implementation and Supervision Plan
4.13          The project would be implemented over a six-year period.  This
is justified by the fact that the project is part of the NERP and would
implemented by CDR which is also implementing the NERP. An implementation
schedule is given in Annex 13. The implementation of Yammouneh, Quasmieh-Ras
El Ain, Danniye and South Bekaa would start in the first year after
appointment of consultants and finalization of bidding documents and would be
completed in the first three years of the project. Agreement was reached
during appraisal that GOL will finance the detailed designs and preparation of
biddino documents for the first five schemes (Para 3.13). Implementation of
Akkar El Bared scheme would start in the second year and also extend over two
years, and would be completed by the end of Project Year 4 (PY4). The small
to medium schemes consisting of about 10,000 ha would be implemented using a
program approach with the various contracts being implemented over the period
of the project, starting towards the end of PY2 and last contracts being
completed in PY6. The preparation by consultants of final designs and tender
documents for civil works would be carried out in PYI.
4.14          Proiect Monitoring and Evaluation would be the responsibility of
the CDR, who would submit progress reports. The basic tool for monitoring
would be the Annual Work Plan and Budget, to be prepared by each institution
involved in the IRMP and approved by the TCC and the Bank. This would be
submitted to the Bank by December 31 of each year. Assurances were obtained
at negotiations that CDR, in coordination with MOHER and MOA, would: (i)
prepare and submit to IBRD, no later than December 31 of each year. a work
nroaram together with a budget for the followina year: and (ii) submit. on a
semi-annual basis commencing June 30. 1995. a report describing the progress
of the various components of the pro-ect Ipara. 8.01 (ill. The CDR would also
propose any remedial actions to be adopted by the TCC in agreement with IBRD.
Monitoring and evaluation of agricultural development activities will be
handled by a task group, made up of the heads of MOA's Departments of
Coordination and Studies, Extension, as well as ARIL and the long term expert
in charge of the OFTT-Extension program. The task group would evaluate would-
be contractors' proposals and implementation performance, on a yearly basis,
and make recommendation to MOA on whether or not contracts should be
continued. The OFTT-Extension long term expert (see TORs Annex 9) would also
be responsible for monitoring and analyzing project impact indicators related
to agricultural development in the various schemes to be rehabilitated by the
project. Performance would be reviewed during Bank supervision missions.
4.15          World Bank Supervision Missions would normally be conducted
twice a year, preferably in May and November. A tentative implementation and
supervision plan is shown in Annex 14; it provides for 16 review missions over
the life of the project, requiring an estimated 197 staff-weeks. Because of
the number and dispersion of subprojects and the lack of recent experience of
the Borrower and the Bank, a minimum supervision time of 25-30 staffweeks
would be required in each of the first three years of the project. To
expedite project start up, a Project launch mission is planned for the second
half of calendar 1994. The mission would review the status of effectiveness
and review the implementation arrangements for the 1995 work program. A mid-



- 29 -
term review of all project activities would be conducted jointly by CDR,
MOHER, MOA, LRA, ARIL, together with the Bank and other possible co-
financiers. The mid-term review would be held at the beginning of PY4 to
assess progress in implementation, verify the continued validity of the design
assumptions in light of implementation experience, and undertake any necessary
adjustments in project objectives, design and implementation arrangements.
The mid-term review report would summarize achievements under the
rehabilitation contracts, analyze difficulties encountered, especially in
marketing, and suggest measures to overcome them in the future. Specifically,
it would assess the progress on developing scheme-level self-finance,
maintenance and administration. It would also evaluate the development impact
of physical rehabilitation and agricultural support on yields, production
costs and cropping patterns. Main criteria for impact evaluation have been
finalized during negotiations. Assurances were obtained at neaotiations that
CDR would prenare a detailed Progress report for submission to IBRD no later
than October 31. 1997. for a ioint review no later than December 31.1997
[para. 8.01 Mi).
V. AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION
A. General
5.01          The improvements proposed under the project would result in
increased agricultural production arising mainly from: (a) increases in
cropping intensities, made possible by a greater and more reliable supply of
irrigation water; (b) increased crop yields, resulting from better and more
timely irrigations and improved use of production inputs and cultural
practices; (c) a modest expansion of the irrigated areas, resulting from the
reduction of water losses previously incurred through the dilapidated
conveyance system. The following is an analysis of the agricultural
development that is anticipated under the project.
B. Expected Channes in CroRping Patterns
5.02          Yammouneh.  Present cropping patterns (Annex 11, Table 1),
indicate that of a total of 5,600 ha of cultivable land only about 3,995 ha
are being cropped to annuals, giving an overall low cropping intensity of 75
percent. In terms of cultivated area, winter rainfed subsistence crops are
the most important. Wheat occupies 3,025 ha and pulses 160 ha, representing
together about 80 percent of the total cropped area. The most important fully
irrigated crops are potatoes (520 ha), winter and summer vegetables (290 ha)
and fruit trees (305 ha). Since the eradication of cannabis and papaver
cultivation, irrigated crops, particularly summer vegetables, potatoes and
cucurbits have become the major source of cash for farmers in the project
area. The area devoted to these crops is rather limited, because the water
conveyance system covers only a portion of the cultivable area and because
there is lack of sufficient irrigation water in the summer. In the zones
where some irrigation canals remain, farmers provide winter wheat and pulse
crops with supplemental irrigation.
5.03          Because irrigation water availability will continue to be
limited during the summer months, even after the rehabilitation of the scheme,
it is anticipated that many farmers will take advantage of the abundant water
supply available in the winter to intensify their farming operations by
eliminating the fallow, significantly expanding the area under wheat and
pulses and providing their winter crops with supplemental irrigation. Summer
crops will benefit from a more reliable supply of irrigation water and better



- 30 -
irrigation techniques (better levelling, irrigation dosage and frequency). It
is expected that the area under summer irrigation will expand by about 1,000
ha, corresponding to the amount of water saved through improved conveyance
efficiency and better on-farm water management. At full development it iu
anticipated that about 5,295 ha will be under cultivation to annual crops in
Yammouneh, giving an overall cropping intensity of 109 percent. Because of
the high demand for these commodities, the area devoted to winter pulses would
expand significantly at the expense of winter wheat, which has a lower return
per hectare. In the summer, the additional irrigated area will be devoted to
varying types of crops, depending on market demand and prices. However,
because of their particular adaptation to the area, potatoes and cucurbits
will continue to be major crops.
5.04          Ouasmieh-Ras El Ain. Existing cropping patterns (Annex 11,
table 1), make citrus the most important crop in the area with 2,325 ha or
about 60 percent of the cultivable 3,850 ha. Bananas are next in importance,
with 825 ha, followed by watermelons (and other vegetable crops), with 500 ha,
and the citrus/bananas association, with 370 ha. The poor condition of the
conveyance network and the lack of sufficient irrigation water moving through
the system in the summer has compelled many of the farmers who can afford it,
to dig tubewells in order to make up for the shortfall in surface water
availability. This has made irrigation frequency low and dosage very high,
causing the leaching of essential nutrients from the soil, increasing the
incidence of cryptogamic and other diseases, hence increasing production costs
and reducing crop yields. The incidence of frost which either reduces yields
or totally destroys banana plantations is also a constraint to banana
production.
5.05          Many of the large farmers in the Quasmieh area are financially
secure and highly receptive to new production techniques and plant materials.
Some have established contacts with public and private research institutions
abroad to obtain and introduce, inter alia, tissue-cultured, cold tolerant
banana cultivars, tropical fruit and ornamental trees as well as flowers.
Hence, banana cultivation and specialty crops (kiwi, passion fruit, mango) are
expected to expand at the expense of citrus. As a result of increased
urbanization and tourism activity, vegetable growing would very likely see
further expansion (+30 percent) and intensification, through green houses,
protective plastic tunnels and intercropping. The total area cultivated in
annual crops is expected to increase from 500 ha to 660 ha even when the area
cultivable to annual crops will decrease slightly from 330 ha to 325 ha. As a
result, the cropping intensity would increase from 151 percent to 203 percent.
5.06          Dannive.  Surveys conducted by consultants for the feasibility
study indicate that the whole project area is planted to perennial fruit tree
crops. The survey also indicated that plant population per unit area is high
and may reach 1,600 to 1,800 plants per ha, not planted on regular lines or
spacings. Most orchards, located in the upper part of the valley near the
water springs benefit from 3 to 4 irrigations per season. They are usually
cropped to apples and some pears and produce high quality fruit readily sold
at relatively high prices. Those that are further down, in the middle part of
the valley, get a little less water and are devoted to pears and peaches. The
quality of the produce in this area is not up to standard, mainly because of
inadequate cultural practices (poor pruning and fertilization, untimely
treatments and irrigations, poor on-farm water management), as is the case for
the other areas in the scheme. Production reaches the market at the peak
supply period of the season and is, therefore, sold at low prices. At the
lowest part of the valley, the availability of irrigation water becomes



- 31 -
problematic. Fruit trees grown in this part of the valley are mainly composed
of prunes and apricots. Because of favorable winter temperatures and exposure
to the south, the prunes and apricots are produced early in the season and,
therefore, benefit from good markets and high prices despite their doubtful
quality.
5.07          Present cropping pattern is not expected to change even after
the rehabilitation of the water conveyance systems. Waterlogging in some
terraces is expected to decrease with the improvement in irrigation practices.
As Lebanon regains lost export markets, it is likely that farmers will change
tree species in response to market demand (Annex 11, table 1).
5.08        Akkar El Bared.  The area that will directly benefit from the
project is currently irrigation by the Aarka river is about 334 ha, of which
142 ha are occupied by citrus. The remaining 199 ha are cropped to wheat (35
ha), and rainfed lettuce (52 ha) in the winter and okra (33 ha) and potatoes
(79 ha) in the summer. In the future, when the cheaper and more dependable
irrigation water from the Bared river reaches the project area, it is
anticipated that farmers will increase their cropping intensity from the
current 104 percent to about 150 percent. This increase in cropping intensity
would be due to increases in summer crops, mainly cucurbits and summer
vegetables. The evolution of cropping patterns and intensities are indicated
in Annex 11, Table 1.
5.09        In addition to the changes in cropping patters and intensity, the
project area will also increase in size. About 42 ha of land, with about 18
ha of citrus, are now being evacuated by the military. Their irrigation
system will be rehabilitated and will receive water from the Bared diversion.
As a result the total cultivated area under irrigation for the Aarka section
of the scheme will increase from 334 to 376 ha.
5.10        South Bekaa.  According to the feasibility study, only about 920
ha of a total cultivable area of 1,622 ha are being cropped at present. The
area in fallow is located mostly in the Karaoun 1 and 2 areas, where soils are
relatively shallow and rocky. This lack of cultivation is due to several
factors, but mainly to the unavailability of irrigation water to cultivate
high return summer crops, the non-profitability of winter rainfed crops and
the degree of absentee owners who have migrated out of the area. The presently
cropped areas are cultivated to wheat (331 ha), potatoes (387 ha), summer
vegetables (156 ha) and watermelon (46 ha). Fallow occupies about 700 ha;
giving an overall cropping intensity of about 57 percent.
5.11          Once the irrigation system is completed and water becomes
available, it is anticipated that cropping will cover the whole area and that
over a period of 7 to 10 years, depending on the type of farms, cropping
intensity would increase to about 115 percent. In addition to the traditional
crops mentioned above, it is likely that some farmers would take up such crops
as winter broad beans (Annex 11, Table 1).



- 32 -
C. Yields and Production
5.12        Yammouneh.  As a result of the project, yields of wheat and
pulses, whose production system will change from occasionally irrigated, to
full supplemental irrigation, have been estimated to increase significantly
(Annex 11, Table 1); wheat yields increase from 1.8 t/ha to 3.5 t/ha and those
of pulses would increase from 1.2 t/ha to 1.8 t/ha. Irrigated potato yields
are estimated to incur modest increases from 20 t/ha to 25.5 t/ha. Ouasmieh
Ras El Ain. Yield increases have been estimated quite conservatively; they
vary from 12 t/ha to 14.4 t/ha for citrus, from 18 t/ha to 19.8t/ha for
bananas and from 20 t/ha to 22 t/ha for watermelon. Dannive. Yield of fruit
trees are expected to increase as a result of more reliable irrigation water,
better irrigation techniques and improved crop husbandry practices. Estimated
yield increases are highest for prunes (+30 percent) and apricots (+18
percent), which are now suffering the most from inadequate water supply.
Yield increases become smaller for the crops grown in higher altitudes and are
closer to the source of water; yields of pears, peaches and apples increase
respectively by 17, 17 and 8 percent. Akkar El Bared. Because farmers in
Aarka area are presently adopting the strategy of cultivating crops only when
there is a quasi guarantee that sufficient water is available to grow them, no
yield increases are anticipated for most crops in the future. The only
exception being citrus fruit which does suffer from lack of sufficient
irrigation water. For citrus fruit, yields are expected to increase by about
20 percent, from 15 t/ha to 18 t/ha. South Bekaa. Yield improvements would
vary from a maximum of 23 percent for summer vegetables to a minimum of 13
percent for potatoes. Yields of wheat and watermelon are expected to increase
respectively by 18 and 20 percent, from 4.5 to 5.3 t/ha and from 30 to 36
t/ha. Yields of major crops for the five sub-project areas are indicated in
Annex 11, Table 1.
VI. MARKETS. PRICES AND INCOMES
A. Marketing and Processing
Marketing
6.01        No major marketing problems are foreseen for expected incremental
project output, which would reach its peak in Year 9 of the project.
Incremental wheat production would only be a fraction of the total quantity of
cereals that Lebanon imports every year (over 440,000 tons). Import
statistics for pulses, bananas and onions which, in 1991, amounted to some
12,000 tons, 17,000 tons and 14,000 tons respectively, indicate that project
incremental output for these crops would also be easily absorbed within the
domestic market which is expected to recover as a result of gradual increase
in per capita incomes and also population growth. A substantial share of the
citrus, potato and vegetable incremental production is expected to be exported
to the Gulf market. This will be possible only if the private sector succeeds
in rehabilitating the marketing networks which have been dismantled during the
Gulf War and if the quality of Lebanese products allows them to compete with
the growing production of neighboring countries. Since the end of the war,
Lebanon has demonstrated its determination to regain its export market share
enjoyed in the 1970s. The strengthening of the extension and training and the
import/export quality control departments of MOA, as foreseen under the
project, would directly support farmers in reducing production costs and
producing high quality products suitable for export, while at the same time,



- 33 -
allowing Lebanese exporters to be more competitive and aggressive on the
export market as they were before the war.
6.02        Government purchases cover wheat, tobacco and sugarbeet.  The
marketing of the remaining agricultural produce is in the hands of the private
sector, mainly individuals and companies, with the limited participation of
some cooperatives. There are numerous buyers and sellers in the local market
and around 30 exporters. The large number of market intermediaries in
wholesaling and retailing indicates that entry to the market is not difficult
and is practically open to anyone. Main features regarding the marketing
system in Lebanon are given in Annex 12.
Processing
6.03        Import-export trade is often combined with the agroindustries
sector, which imports raw materials for processing and canning factories and
re-exports processed goods. Many private companies produce Lebanese food
specialties for the Near East market. It is expected that this sector will
have enough strength and innovative capacity to participate in the development
of a highly specialized and diversified type of agricultural production.
B. Prices
6.04        With few exceptions, prices are market responsive, and it could be
argued that production is, to a large extent, determined by market forces and
reflects Lebanon's competitive edge. This is particularly the case for most
crops grown in the project area, fruits, vegetables, cereals, etc. At present
tobacco, wheat and sugarbeet are the only exceptions. The Government purchases
tobacco from registered farmers at supported prices; it maintains a monopoly
over cigarette manufacture and regulates cigarette imports. Wheat and flour
imports by the private sector are regulated by the Cereals Office because of
subsidies on bread. At present, prices of agricultural output do not differ
considerably from their respective world market levels. On the input side,
there are no subsidies on fertilizers, pesticides and other farm inputs. The
local prices of fertilizers are slightly above world market prices. The list
of farmgate prices for agricultural outputs and inputs is given in Annex 11,
Table 7.
C. Incomes
6.05        Levels of farm income vary considerably with farm size, agro-
ecological conditions and levels of production techniques. On the basis of
crop models designed to approximate the real costs of production and crop
returns, farm models were prepared for each of the five sub-project areas, in
order to estimate the farm-level benefits that would accrue as a result of the
project. An analysis of these farm models indicates that incomes, in all farm
sizes, can be expected to increase significantly. Expected net farm incomes
for the with and without situations for the five sub-project areas are shown
in Annex 11, Table 2.
6.06        In Yammouneb, incomes for almost all farms are quite depressed,
particularly after Government enforced the banning of illicit crops. With the
project, farm incomes are expected to increase significantly by 321 and 360
percent respectively for small and medium farms. For large farms income would
increase by about 190 percent. In Ouasmieh, farm incomes are generated almost
exclusively from profitable irrigated crops. With the project, farm income
are expected to increase by 34 and 52 percent for small and medium farms



- 34 -
respectively. In Danniye farm incomes are expected to increase by 24, 14 and
8 percent respectively for small, medium and large farms. In Akkar El Bared,
future increases in farm incomes are expected to be more significant than in
Danniye; they will increase by 52, 54 and 54 percent respectively for small
medium and large farms. Finally, in South Bekaa, even though land holdings
are small and the population is relatively poor, most farmers do not cultivate
single holdings, but group a number of holdings into large farms
'exploitation". In the future, and because of the elimination of fallow on
large area (700 ha) of the subproject, farm income is expected to increase
significantly by about 200 percent for all types of farms.
D. Cost Recovery
6.07        General.  While there is a wide range of laws and regulations
which regulate water use and disposal in Lebanon, there are no references to
irrigation investment cost recovery and water charge calculation procedures.
However, despite weak management, the RWAs have been able to collect water
charges, at least partially, throughout the war. The present arrangements for
setting and recovering water charges are different from one scheme to another.
In recent years, water charge receipts for the Quasmieh system have covered
about 70 percent of O&M expenditures while for Yammouneh scheme receipts have
not exceeded 15 percent of O&M expenditures estimated at US$40/ha, due to very
low water charges and recovery rate (20 percent).
6.08        Cost Recovery Policy.  Water charges for irrigation are determined
by the RWAs' Boards and are subject to ratification by the MOHER or by the
Government. Responsibility for enforcement of the regulation is with the RWA
or RWC; these organizations are also responsible for the assessment and
collection of charges. Recently, the GOL has decided to stop subsidizing the
Authorities and Commissions which will have to cover their costs from the sale
of the services. With the support of the Emergency Reconstruction and
Rehabilitation Project (ERRP), the GOL is also working on the preparation of a
National Water Management Master Plan and on the restructuring of water
management institutions in the country. A new national water pricing policy
will result from these two studies.  The policy principles guiding the
preparation of the studies and those which will be applicable to the
irrigation schemes financed under the proposed project are detailed in a
Letter of Irrigation Sector Policy that has been officially submitted to the
Bank as a condition of negotiations (Annex 16). This letter indicates quite
advanced thinking on cost recovery issues relative to other countries. The
objective is to ultimately attain full cost recovery of both investment and of
O&M.   Assurances were obtained at neaotiations that: (i} GOL would ensure
that. upon completion of the rehabilitation of the five first schemes. water
charces be set at level sufficient to recover 100 Percent of the recurrent
costs of O&M and that recovery of rehabilitation investment costs would be
done in conformity with the policies and obiectives enunciated in the Lebanese
Government's policy letter dated May 26, 1994, for all irrigation schemes and
would commence six years after the completion of the rehabilitation works
fpara. 8.01 (k)l; and (ii) GOL would provide adeauate budaet allocations to
the relevant budgets of its public agencies, in a timely manner. to cover
recurrent cost that could not be covered through internal cash generation
[para. 8.01 (1)1.
6.09        Following current practices, water pricing will be tailored to the
specificity of each scheme, and will vary according to the type of service,
and to the season of irrigation. Water charges will be adjusted every year



- 35 -
and on-farm development costs will be left to the farmers. All rehabilitation
works to be financed under the project will include the installation of water
flow measurement devices, which will allow the introduction of volumetric
pricing in the use of irrigation water.
6.10        Farmers' Capacity to Pay.  Present water charges represent on
average 13 percent of farmers' production costs and about 7 percent of farm
income. However, these figures hide the fact that only about 20 percent of
the expenditures are devoted to maintenance, 80 percent being mainly used for
payment of the salaries of the RWAs and RWCs. Farm budget analysis shows that
under the project, incremental farm income would be substantial, O&M would be
made more efficient and water charges would be more directly related to O&M.
As a result, under the project, water charges would represent about 8 percent
of production costs and about 6 percent of farmers' income, which is
considered affordable (Annex 11, Table 3).
VII. BENEFITS. ECONOMIC JUSTIFICATION AND RISKS
A. Benefits
7.01        The primary benefits of the project would be the incremental
agricultural production resulting from increased water use efficiency in the
irrigation schemes through improved water delivery. Higher agricultural
productivity would also be achieved as a result of the adoption by farmers of
improved agricultural practices regarding irrigation as well as better use of
inputs in particular fertilizers and pesticides. Annex 11, Table 1, shows the
estimated incremental production for the most important crops. Quality of
agricultural production is also expected to improve allowing Lebanon to regain
gradually the export market share, especially for citrus and vegetables,
enjoyed before the war. As a result of the increased agricultural production,
farmers' incomes are expected to increase significantly. This is reflected in
the indicative farm models which have been prepared for the different schemes
and are summarized in Annex 11, Table 2.
7.02        Overall project incremental production for major crops would be
substantial at full development (year 9 of the project) and would include
about 5,600 tons of wheat (+80 percent), 750 tons of pulses (+ 385 percent
because of very good prices and high demand), 20,100 tons of potatoes and
similar seasonal crops (+93 percent), 14,800 tons of summer vegetables
(represented by tomatoes in the models and made possible by increased
availability of water in the summer), 5,600 tonB of mixed citrus fruit (+20
percent), 9,500 tons of apples (+8 percent) and 3,400 tons of bananas (+19
percent).
7.03        In total, in the five schemes, 21,200 farm families are expected
to benefit from the project. About 3,630 extended farm families (41,000
people) in Yammouneh, 1,200 families (8,000 people) in Quasmieh, 9,300
families (83,700 people) in Danniye, 170 families (1,903 people) in Akkar and
6,900 land owners (41,340 people) in South Bekaa would benefit from the
project. While within the institutional strengthening component of the
project some activities, such as training and extension, are designed to meet
only the needs of the farmers in the project area with special emphasis on low
income farmers and women, other support services, including applied research,
input/output quality control and import/export quality control, are expected
to benefit producers and exporters outside these project areas. A financial



- 36 -
analysis has been carried out to show the level of farm income for both the
without- and with-project situations, taking into consideration typical farm
models.
B. Economic Analysis
7.04        Economic rates of return and expected net present values have been
estimated for the project as a whole, as well as for each of the five
subprojects (Annex 11). The analysis has been carried out for a period of 25
years, using constant 1994 prices. Economic costs include all proposed
investments and incremental operating costs with the exception of the cost of
environmental measures, where benefits are not readily quantifiable and part
of the cost of institutional strengthening and studies that is not directly
related to project benefits. CIF prices have been used to cost imported
equipment, and civil works have been costed at contract prices based on
current unit prices for work in Lebanon. Duties and taxes have been excluded.
For foreign costs, the current floating rate of 1US$ = LL1,690 has been used.
Cost allocation, details on incremental operating costs and replacement costs
are given in Annex 11. Incremental production costs relative to on-farm
activities, but excluding water charges, are already taken into account in the
net project benefits calculations. The economic benefits are based on the
anticipated incremental production that is expected to occur as a result of
the project and from savings in operating costs in areas currently irrigated
with pumped groundwater. Incremental production was aggregated from
representative farm models. Commodity prices are based on World Bank
projections. Crops which are either not internationally traded or for which
no world market reference price was obtainable, i.e., lettuce, onions,
apricots, cereal straw and legume, and hay, have been priced based on local
market prices.
7.05        The overall rate of return of the project is about 19 percent.
Table 7.1 below shows ERR for various schemes. The project is expected to
generate a net present value of US$24.1 million discounted at the opportunity
cost of capital of 12 percent. Tentative estimate of the ERR from the
information available in the RIA shows an average rate of return of the 28
small and medium schemes to be about 40 percent.

Sensitivity Analvsis
7.07        The sensitivity analysis (Annex 11) indicates that economic rates
of return to the project and for the subprojects have been found to be quite
resistant to wide-ranging deviations from the base case assumptions. The
switching value analysis for the project as a whole shows that it would take a
40 percent decrease in benefits or a 70 percent increase in costs to lower the
economic rate of return to 12 percent. A 30 percent decrease in benefits
would reduce the overall rate of return to 14 percent. A 30 percent increase
in costs would decrease the rate of return to 15 percent. A two-year delay in
benefits would lower the rate of return to 15 percent. However the farm
budget analysis has been conservative in assuming that in most of the schemes,
the first benefits would occur only in year four. The risk of such a lag is,
therefore, considered limited.
C. Proiect Risks
7.08        Project risks are mainly associated with delays in construction,
institutional building and capacities, socioeconomic and environmental. As in
all construction works contracts, the principal risks concern increased costs



- 38 -
and delayed benefits. The risk that physical component will be delayed is
small as most rehabilitation design work is already substantially completed.
The project has been well prepared and the availability of detailed designs
and tender documents by project effectiveness, the use of reputable
contractors and the high standard of construction supervision to be provided
by the MOHER's WSIU, assisted by reputable consultants, will make it possible
to proceed smoothly with its implementation. Moreover, as indicated in the
sensitivity analysis (see para. 7.07), cost overruns and time delays in
project implementation will not have a major negative effect on the economic
viability of the project.
7.10        Full realization and sustainability of estimated benefits is also
dependent upon successful implementation of the water management program
proposed with complementary establishment of strong institutional support and
effective agricultural extension. Given the history of excessive water use in
the project areas, enforcement of operational rules for optimal water use will
require considerable effort by the GOL. Any serious failure in this regard
could cause a significant water shortage, particularly at the extremities of
the irrigation system, thereby causing benefit reduction. The project would
attempt to mitigate these problems by proposing a strong water management
program and by seeking GOL's strengthening of LRA and RWA with enforcement
authority.
7.11        Both the size and complexity of the proposed project appear to be
within the capacity of the public agencies involved.  The project will benefit
from the implementation capacity of CDR and MOHER which has been greatly
improved under the ERRP by the establishment of the PMU and the WSIU. The
sustainability of the development impact of the project will depend on the
success of the on-going restructuring of water management institutions,
initiated under the ERRP. The institutional strengthening by the project
would alleviate this risk by providing technical assistance and training to
the new institutions in the irrigation sector.
VIII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
8.01        The following assurances from the Borrower were obtained during
negotiations:
(a)   GOL would select the small and medium schemes on the basis of
criteria acceptable to IBRD, including: (i) internal rate of
return equivalent to at least 12 percent; (ii) promotion of
community participation in the rehabilitation; (iii) impact on
rural income; (iv) absence of adverse impact on the environment;
and (v) absence of involuntary relocation of the local population
(para. 3.07)
(b)   For all expenses made on the basis of SOEs, GOL would (i)
maintain, or cause to be maintained, records and accounts
reflecting such expenditures; (ii) ensure that all records
evidencing such expenditures are retained until at least one year
after IBRD has received the last audit report; (iii) have the
records and accounts of the SOEs, including those for the Special



- 39 -
Account, for each fiscal year, audited by independent auditors
acceptable to IBRD; and (iv) furnish to IBRD the audit reports,
including a separate opinion on the SOEs, no later than six months
after the end of each fiscal year (para. 3.25).
(c)   CDR would: (i) have its records, accounts and financial statements
for each fiscal year audited, in accordance with appropriate
auditing principles by independent auditors acceptable to the
IBRD; and (ii) furnish to the IBRD no later than six month after
the end of each year, certified copies of its financial statements
for such year and the reports of such audits (para. 3.30).
(d)   CDR would implement the project through its PMU and through
MOHER's WSIU, under arrangements similar to those already in place
for the ERRP, and would put in place alternative implementation
arrangements in agreement with IBRD, in case that any of the above
units ceases to exist (para. 4.03).
(e)   CDR would recruit no later than December 31, 1994, an irrigation
agronomist to assist MOHER in the carrying out of its project's
function, on terms of reference satisfactory to IBRD (para 4.03).
(f)   CDR, in coordination with MOHER, would prepare and submit to the
Bank a O&M program for the first year of the project would be
prepared no later than July 31, 1995 and implementation of the
plan would commence no later than December 31, 1995 (para. 4.07).
(g)   CDR would recruit, no later than March 31,1995, 5 applied research
experts, (para. 4.09), and no later than June 30, 1995, an
extension expert, (para 4.10), to assist MOA in the execution of
the project.
(h)   The TCC would coordinate the implementation of the IRMP with the
implementation of the ERRP and that representatives of the MOA,
GP, LRA and ARIL would be invited to attend the meetings of the
TCC (para. 4.12).
(i)   CDR, in coordination with MOHER and MOA, would: (i) prepare and
submit to IBRD, no later than December 31 of each year, a work
program together with a budget for the following year; and (ii)
submit, on a semi-annual basis commencing June 30, 1995, a report
describing the progress of the various components of the project
(para. 4.14).
(j)   CDR would prepare a detailed progress report for submission to
IBRD no later than October 31, 1997, for a joint mid-term review
no later than December 31, 1997 (para. 4.15).
(k)   GOL would ensure that upon completion of the rehabilitation of the
five first schemes, water charges be set at level sufficient to
recover 100 percent of the recurrent cost8 of O&M, and that

recovery of rehabilitation investment costs would be done in

(1)   GOL would provide adequate budget allocations to the relevant

8.02        With the above aBsurances from the Government, the proposed

term of 17 years, including 4 years of grace, at the standard variable
interest rate. The Borrower would be the Government of the Lebanese Republic.
The Implementing Agency would be the CDR.

LEBANESE REPUBLIC
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION AND MODERNIZATION PROJECT
THE YAMMOUNEH IRRIGATION SCHEME
I. THE SUBPROJECT AREA
1.1         Location, Climate and Soils.  The Yammouneh scheme is located in
the northeastern part of the Central Bekaa Valley, at the separation limit
between the Litani and Orontos river watersheds (Map IBRD 25865). The plain
is wide and covers more than 8,000 ha; it is at an average altitude of 950 m.
The climate is of the Continental type, with an average rainfall of 400-450
mm/year. Precipitation is erratic and mostly concentrated over five months,
from November to March. Summers are dry and hot and the average temperature in
June-July is 350C. Winters are cool with average minimum temperatures of below
0C in January. Frost risk spans from November till April, with an average of
20 days/year. Total annual evapo-transpiration is estimated at 1,550 mm/year.
The soils are colluvial and classified as red-Mediterranean soils. In the
plain they are deep, fairly well drained and do not show any salinity or
adverse characteristics. In the western part of the plain, where the terrain
is broken and hilly, soils are shallow with a calcareous strata of varying
thickness.
1.2         Population, Target Group and Land Tenure.   The population in the
Yammouneh area is relatively poor and numbers about 41,000 people . Per
capita and farm incomes have become among the lowest in Lebanon since the
prohibition to cultivate of cannabis and poppy (estimated farm income is about
U.S.$508 for small farms). Presently, only about half the population resides
permanently in the area, many of the active young people work in urban areas
for extended periods of time to supplement their families income. Farming
households directly related to the project area of 5,600 ha, have been
estimated at 3,700. Small farmers are by far the largest population group in
the Yammouneh scheme, representing 99 percent of the rural population of the
area and farming over 80 percent of the agricultural land. They can be
subdivided into two groups, the first with an average farm size of 0.85 ha (51
percent) and the other with farms of around 1.55 ha (48 percent). However,
there are also 25 large owners (1 percent), whose average farm size is over 50
ha; they control about 20 percent of the land. Farming systems adopted by
small and medium farmers generally integrate crops with small ruminants;
occasionally cattle, bee keeping and pisciculture can also be part of the
farming activities. Female participation in agriculture varies widely from
village to village, from 10 percent to 80 percent. In all cases women play an
active role in small ruminant husbandry and poultry.
1.3         Present Cropping Patterns and Yields.  In Yammouneh, present
cropping intensity is low (75 percent) due to the limited supply of surface
water during summer and the high cost of pumping from ground water. High risk
of late frosts in March and April further limit crop growth potential in the
northern Bekaa plain. Until 1991 the major rainfed and irrigated crops were
cannabis, white poppy, wheat (more than 10 percent of the area benefitted from
supplemental irrigation) and cucurbits (cucumber and melon), as well as



- 42 -                            Annex 1
Page 2 of 6
potatoes, onions, summer vegetables, etc. In 1992, GOL destroyed all illicit
crops and banned their future culture. It also promised to assist in
introducing alternative crops, through expansion of irrigation, and to
alleviate poverty through integrated rural development. Even though studies
indicate that under current cultural practices no crop can compete, in terms
of net return per ha, with cannabis and poppy, farmers could be persuaded to
make the transition. This entails that appropriate conditions are created
for them to grow irrigated legal crops (rehabilitation, extension, and
modernization of irrigation systems).
1.4         To help eradicate the cultivation of cannabis and poppy and
alleviate poverty, Government has placed the rehabilitation/ completion of the
Yammouneh scheme at the top of its priorities. Under strict control, farmers
have, so far, refrained from growing illicit crops and reverted to irrigated
vegetables, cucurbits and potatoes over 15 percent of the total cultivable
area, to rainfed wheat and pulses over 57 percent of the area and to
traditional stone fruit trees and grapes over 5 percent of the area; the rest
of the land (23 percent) is left in fallow. At present, farmers' most pressing
need is to increase the irrigated area, which presently stands at a little
over 1200 ha in the winter and about 1,000 ha ( of which 718 ha from tube
wells and not from the scheme) in the summer.
1.5         Agricultural machinery is available in the project area; most of
which was acquired by private farmers with profits from illicit crops. There
are 100 tractors with ploughing equipment, 10 potato planters, at least one
combine harvester, 3 threshers, 3 sprayers and 5 fertilizer spreaders. Wheat
and pulse seed drills are non existent. The existence of a large number of
tractors is due to their diversified utilizations on the farm and for
transport. Usually the owners of agricultural equipment hire out their
services (mainly for land preparation and spraying) to small farmer
1.6         Irrigation SYstem. The Yammouneh subproject area is supplied by
irrigation water from a series of springs situated in synclinal structural
depression along the mountainous slope in the West Bekaa valley. The average
annual flow of about 60 to 80 mcm, which is not regularly distributed during
the year, where discharge drops to 10 percent of peak spring season flow in
the dry months.
II. DEVELOPMENT CONSTRAINTS AND POTENTIAL
2.1         The single most important constraint to improved agricultural
production in the area is the inadequacy between availability and requirements
for water to sustain optimum plant growth and production. For rainfed crops,
mainly winter cereals and spring cucurbits, the erratically distributed
rainfall is not enough to produce decent yields consistently. For irrigated
crops, the first problem is that there is not enough water to grow large
surface areas under irrigation, secondly, even for the presently irrigated
crops, the water made available is not sufficient to optimize crop yields.
Much of the potentially available water is lost through the archaic and poorly
managed conveyance system. In addition, even if water availability was not a
constraint, agricultural productivity would not be maximized as a result of
inadequate crop husbandry practices, including poor on-farm water management,
inadequate use of expensive and doubtful quality inputs, lack of credit and a



- 43 -                             Annex 1
Page 3 of 6
risky marketing environment, where produce dumping from other countries is not
infrequent.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Prolect Obiectives
3.1         The main objectives of the project are to increase
agriculture-based income and employment in the Yammouneh subproject area and
achieve a sustainable and improved management of water resources, through (i)
the rehabilitation of surface irrigation infrastructure; and (ii) the
provision of basic public support services.
B. Proiect Description
3.2         The project consists of two major components: (a) the
rehabilitation and modernization of irrigation infrastructure; and (b) the
provision of support to institutions involved in (i) irrigation and water
management; (ii) agricultural support services; and (iii) project
implementation and coordination. The later component is described in annex 6.
C. Detailed Features
3.3         Project Works would include: (i) improvements to tunnel entrance,
head gates and critical repairs to tunnel; (ii) repairs to covered canal,
modernization of 20 turnouts on covered canal, sealing of about 30 outlets,
and replacement of gates for six offtake at head of chute; (iii) construction
of a new open chute to ensure efficient conveyance of irrigation water from
the end of the covered canal to the Bekaa plain with a 3.5 m3/sec capacity or
rehabilitation and repairs to existing chute (and stilling basin and
distribution system at Chifla); (iv) rehabilitation and repair of concrete
lining on four main canals (38 km) with structures and gates; (v) repair or
lining of concrete and earth canals in the chute area (18 km) and plain area
secondary and tertiary canals (80 km) with outlets and gates; and (vi)
rehabilitation of the access road to the head of the chute.
D. Proiect Cost
3.4         The base total cost for rehabilitating the irrigation
infrastructure at Yammouneh would amount to U.S.$6.16 million. Breakdown of
costs per sub-category are detailed in appendix 1.
IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
4.1         Implementation of the Irrigation Rehabilitation Works would be the
responsibility of the existing Water Sector Implementation Unit (WSIU). The
WSUI is under the authority of MOHER's Director General for Investments and is
assisted by a team of international consultants (WSIUC) financed for a period
of three years by the European Community (EC). Rehabilitation works would be
contracted out to private contractors. The WSIU would have full
responsibility and accountability for the technical aspects of the
rehabilitation works to be financed by the project. Procurement and
disbursement would be handled by CDR' Program Management Unit (PMU).



-44-                              Annex I
Page 4 of 6
4.2         Overation and Maintenance of main headwork, main and secondary
canals, would be initially under the responsibility of existing Baalbek-Hermel
Water Authority and would be automatically transferred to the new Regional
Water Irrigation Authority (Bekaa RWIA) as soon as it is established. Water
users associations organized around the selection and hiring of ditch riders
(Shawi), would be responsible for O&M of tertiary canals. The composition of
the Water Committee's board, currently designated by the Minister of Hydraulic
and Electrical resources, will be revised to include representatives of
farmers.
4.3         Water Users' Involvement.  Although farmers are currently involved
in the operation of the schemes through the designation of the Shawis, they
are not fully involved in the management of the schemes. This has two major
consequences: (i) farmers are not aware of the real cost of irrigation O&M,
especially when they see no improvement in water distribution and are
therefore sometime reluctant to pay the corresponding water charges, and (ii)
simple O&M tasks, which could be more effectively handled by farmers
themselves are neglected by the Authorities. To increase the role and
responsibilities of farmers in the management of downstream irrigation
systems, measures need to be taken to allow for their involvement in the
management of schemes through the support and empowerment of existing WUAs
grouping farmers at the outlet or village level.
4.4         The rehabilitation sequence would include, as a necessary step,
beneficiaries' review and approval of rehabilitation proposals; this dialogue
has been initiated by a social study conducted in April-May 1994. To improve
the dialogue between the different areas of the scheme, a Scheme Water
Management Committee (SWMC) would be created. The SWMC would comprise
representatives from the Water Authority and representatives designated by the
WUAs. The SWMC would make decisions based on discussions in which users'
representatives would voice their demands; and users would receive guidance on
management of the secondary and tertiary networks.
V. AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION AND FARM INCOME
A. Expected Changes in Croppina Patterns
5.1         Present cropping patterns indicate (see Annex 11, Table 1) that of
a total of 5,600 ha of cultivable land only about 3,995 ha are being cropped
to annuals, giving an overall low cropping intensity of 75 percent. In terms
of cultivated area, winter rainfed subsistence crops are the most important
(see table 1), wheat occupies 3,025 ha and pulses 160 ha, representing
together about 80 percent of the total cropped area. The most important fully
irrigated crops are potatoes (520 ha), winter and summer vegetables (290 ha)
and fruit trees (305 ha). Since the eradication of cannabis and papaver,
irrigated crops, particularly summer vegetables, potatoes and cucurbits have
become the major source of cash for farmers in the project area. The area
devoted to these crops is rather limited, because the water conveyance system
covers only a portion of the cultivable area and because of the lack of
sufficient irrigation water in the summer. In the zones where some irrigation
canals remain, farmers provide winter wheat and pulse crops with supplemental
irrigation.



- 45 -                            Annex I
Page 5 of 6
5.2         Because irrigation water availability will continue to be limited
during the summer months, even after the rehabilitation of the scheme, it is
anticipated that many farmers will take advantage of the abundant water supply
available in the winter to intensify their farming operation by eliminating
the fallow, significantly expanding the area under wheat and pulses and
providing their winter crops with supplemental irrigation. Summer crops will
benefit from a more reliable supply of irrigation water and better irrigation
techniques (better levelling, irrigation dosage and frequency). It is
expected that the area under summer irrigation will expand by about 1,000 ha,
corresponding to the amount of water saved through improved conveyance
efficiency and better on-farm water management. At full development it is
anticipated that about 5,295 ha will be under cultivation to annual crops in
Yammouneh, giving an overall cropping intensity of 109 percent. Because of
the high demand for these commodities, the area devoted to winter pulses would
expand significantly at the expense of winter wheat, which has a lower return
per hectare. In the summer, the additional irrigated area will be devoted to
varying types of crops, depending on market demand and prices. However,
because of their particular adaptation to the area, potatoes and cucurbits
will continue to be major crops.
B. Yields and Production
5.3         As a result of the project, yields of wheat and pulses, whose
production system will change from occasionally irrigated, to full
supplemental irrigation, have been estimated to increase significantly (see
Annex 11, Table 1.); wheat yields increase from 1.8t/ha to 3.5t/ha and those
of pulses would increase from 1.2t/ha to 1.8t/ha. Irrigated potatoes and
vegetable crop yields are estimated to incur modest increases from 20t/ha to
25.5t/ha.
C. Incomes
5.4         Farm incomes were estimated by the feasibility study and further
reviewed during the appraisal mission. They vary considerably with farm size
and levels of production techniques. Incomes for almost all farms are quite
depressed, particularly after Government enforced the banning of illicit
crops. At present the value of estimated incomes (see Annex 11, Table 2)
ranges from LL 863,876 ($511) in small (0.85 ha)farms to LL 1,415,497 ($838)
in medium (1.55 ha) farms and LL45,066,000 ($26,666) in the rare (less than 1
percent) large farms (50 ha). On the basis of crop models designed to
approximate the real costs of production and crop returns, farm models were
prepared for the sub-project area in order to estimate the farm-level benefits
that would accrue as a result of the project. In the with-project situation
farm incomes are expected to increase significantly by 321 and 360 percent
respectively for small, medium farms. For large farms income would increase
by about 190 percent.
