LEBANESE REPUBLIC
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION AND MODERNIZATION PROJECT
THE OUASMIEH/RAS EL-AIN IRRIGATION SCHEME
I. SUBPROJECT AREA
1.1         Location. Climate and Soils.  The Quasmieh/Ras El-Ain (QRA) scheme
is located in the southern coastal area of the country. The QRA scheme was
planned to irrigate the narrow plain extending from Saida in the North to
Nakoura in the South. It includes about 3,850 ha of agricultural lands
situated between 0 and 70 meter above sea level. The climate is Mediterranean,
with relatively mild winters characterized by high precipitation and moderate
temperatures. Average yearly rainfall amounts to about 750 mm, concentrated
mainly from December to early March. The average summer temperature is 270C.
Despite the occurrence of high winter rains the area remains in need of
irrigation water for six to seven months per year. Total evapo-transpiration
is estimated at 1165 mm/year. Soils are mostly deep and generally loamy with a
high clay content, although they range from loamy clay to heavy clay loams.
They are generally calcareous, non-saline and non-sodic. Most soils are
suitable for irrigated agriculture.
1.2         Population. Target Group and Land Tenure.  The total population in
the QRA area is about 98,000, resident in over 24 villages excluding the city
of Sour. The agricultural population totals almost 19,500, and land is by far
the foremost source of income. Absentee land owners constitute some 52
percent, holding around 20 percent of the land. Approximately 1,200 farming
households are found in the 3,900 ha dominated by the Quasmieh/Ras El-Ain
irrigation scheme. Holdings vary from less than 0.5 ha to more than 20 ha per
family; for the more representative categories average farm size is around 1.2
ha for the small farmers (73 percent of the farmers and 26 percent of the
land) and 9.2 ha for the medium and large land owners (27 percent of the
farmers and 74 percent of the land). Small farmers frequently rent land from
the large owners, or work under crop sharing arrangements. Large owners
usually entrust daily management of their farms to a foreman who, in addition
to his salary, usually receives free housing and a percentage of the annual
production. In some large holdings, agricultural production techniques are
often exceptionally advanced, highly specialized ( cold-resistant,tissue-
cultured banana varieties, high producing citrus cultivars, floriculture etc.)
and allow these farmers to operate at a level of productivity significantly
higher than that of the average farmer in the area.
1.3         Present Cropping Patterns and Yields,    Agricultural production
in the area is relatively intensive, it is, however, suffering from
unorganized water distribution and low crop husbandry practices. Major crops
include citrus fruit (oranges, mandarins,grapefruit and particularly lemons),
which covers about 60 percent of the total cultivable area and bananas (21
percent) at lower elevations (below 50 m), where risks of frost are rare.
Some relatively new plantations of mango and loquat are expanding slowly.



- 48 -                             Annex 2
Page 2 of 6
Intercropping seasonal vegetable crops into young citrus or banana plantations
is frequent; cucumbers, tomato, aubergine, melon and zucchinis are used
frequently to get revenues before the perennial fruit trees enter into full
production.
1.4         Cropping patterns vary according to farm size: small farmers
mostly grow vegetables and bananas, while the medium to large farms
predominantly cultivate citrus and often, bananas in association with citrus.
The present situation is characterized by the poor quality of citrus orchards,
which is a direct consequence of the lack of effective pest control measures
and of suitable husbandry techniques. Most serious agronomic constraints are
related to pest control techniques which are very costly but not always
effective particularly for citrus and greenhouse cultivation of vegetables.
Many farmers sell their crops (vegetables, oranges and bananas) while still on
the trees, thus avoiding labor-intensive harvesting and grading. Present
cropping intensity and yields are presented in the Annex 11, Table 1.
1.5         Irrigation System.  The main source of irrigation water for the
Quasmieh/Ras El-Ain is primarily the Litani River System in addition to the
Ras El Ain springs which are free flowing artisian aquifers with a relatively
stable flow. Groundwater is also used to supplement the shortfall from canal
supplies, particularly at the tail end of the main canals with an estimated 20
percent of the Quasmieh scheme relying on supplemental groundwater supplies.
II. DEVELOPMENT CONSTRAINTS AND POTENTIAL
2.1         In Quasmieh, the non-reliability of water and the long irrigation
intervals are a major constraints to increased productivity. To alleviate the
risk of water stress, farmers tend to systematically irrigate their orchards.
This causes high humidity related plant diseases (Fungal attacks,Gummosis) in
addition to the existing viral infections (Posorosis) and contributes to the
rapid leaching of plant nutrients. Because of the varying potency of
pesticides and the lack of proper pesticide applications, productivity, in
many orchards suffers from extensive insect damage. The second most important
problem in Quasmieh and all the other citrus producing areas of Lebanon,
relates to the uncertainties of marketing, which has been affected by the Gulf
war and the deterioration of the quality standards of Lebanese citrus produce.
2.2         The establishment and control of Quality standards for citrus
produce, destined for export would help Lebanese fruit regain its previous
good reputation and markets. It is also anticipated that a well focussed
support to agricultural production and a better control of pesticide quality
and use, respectively by MOA's Pesticide Control and Quarantine Service and
its Extension Service, would improve crops husbandry practices and yield as
well as reduce the hazards to the environment from potential excessive uses of
pesticides.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Proiect Obiectives
3.1         The main objectives of the project are to increase the income of
farmers in the Quasmieh/Ras El Ain area and achieve a sustainable and improved



- 49 -                           Annex 2
Page 3 of 6
management of water resources, through (i) the rehabilitation of surface
irrigation infrastructure; and (ii) the provision of basic public support
services.
B. Proiect Description
3.2         The project consists of two major components: (a) rehabilitation
and modernization of irrigation infrastructure; and (b) provision of support
to institutions (see Annex 6) involved in (i) irrigation and water management;
(ii) agricultural support services; and (iii) project implementation and
coordination.
C. Detailed Features
3.3         Works to be financed by the project would consist of : (i) repair
and reconstruction of the Zrarieh diversion structure; (ii) reconstruction of
damaged segments of the main canals, with related structures and water control
equipment; (iii) reconstruction of the Zahrani siphons (750 m), to irrigate
300 ha at the tail end; (iv) construction of a diversion weir on the Litani to
divert water to the existing pump station; (v) rehabilitation of the existing
pump station of Quasmieh and provision of five new pump sets; (vi)
installation of a high voltage power line (15 km) to the Quasmieh pump
station; (vii) rehabilitation and concrete lining of secondary canals (25 km);
(viii) reconstruction and equipping of about 100 turnouts with new calibrated
offtakes on the main canals and closing up about 300 unofficial offtakes; (ix)
construction of 8 km of lateral canals to feed directly into secondary or
tertiary canals: (x) other works, such as repairs to the Quasmieh siphon,
opening of expropriated land along the main canals, construction of an 8 km
long service road to the Zrarieh diversion structure, weed clearing and
desilting of canals.
D. Prolect Cost
3.4         The total base cost for rehabilitating the irrigation
infrastructure at Quasmish would amount to U.S.$7.84 million. Breakdown of
costs per sub-category are detailed in appendix 1.
IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
4.1         Execution of the Irrigation Rehabilitation Works would be the
responsibility of the existing MOHER's Water Sector Implementation Unit
(WSIU), which would work in close cooperation with the Litani River Authority.
The WSUI is under the authority of MOHER's Director General for Investments
and is assisted by a team of international consultants (WSIUC) financed for a
period of three years by the European Community (EC). Rehabilitation works
would be contracted out to private contractors. The WSIU would have full
responsibility and accountability for the technical aspects of the
rehabilitation works to be financed by the project. Procurment and
disbursements would be handled by CDR's Program Management Unit(PMU).
4.2         Operation and Maintenance of main headwork, main and secondary
canals, would remain under the Litani River Authority. Water users



- 50 -                             Annex 2
Page 4 of 6
associations organized around the selection and hiring of ditch riders
(Shawi), would be responsible for O&M of tertiary canals. The composition of
the Water Committee's board, currently designated by the Minister of Hydraulic
and Electrical resources, will be revised to include representatives of
farmers.
4.3         Water Users' Involvement.  Although farmers are currently involved
in the operation of the schemes through the designation of the Shawis, they
are not fully involved in the management of the schemes. This has two major
consequences: (i) farmers are not aware of the real cost of irrigation O&M,
especially when they see no improvement in water distribution and are
therefore sometime reluctant to pay the corresponding water charges, and (ii)
simple O&M tasks, which could be more effectively handled by farmers
themselves are neglected by the Authorities. To increase the role and
responsibilities of farmers in the management of downstream irrigation
systems, measures need to be taken to allow for their involvement in the
management of schemes through the support and empowerment of existing WUAs
grouping farmers at the outlet or village level.
V. AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION AND FARM INCOME
A. Expected Changes in Croppina Patterns
5.1         Existing cropping patterns ( Annex 11, Table 1) make citrus the
most important crop in the area with 2,325 ha or about 60 percent of the
cultivable 3,850 ha. Bananas are next in importance, with 825 ha, followed by
watermelon (and other vegetable crops), with 500 ha, and the citrus/banana
association, with 370 ha. The poor condition of the conveyance network and
the lack of sufficient irrigation water moving through the system in the
summer, has compelled many of the farmers who can afford it, to dig tubewells
in order to make up for the short fall in surface water availability. This
has made irrigation frequency low and dosage very high, causing the leaching
of essential nutrients from the soil, increasing the incidence of cryptogamic
and other diseases, hence increasing production costs and reducing crop
yields. The incidence of frost which either reduced yields or totally
destroyed banana plantations is also a constraint to banana production.
5.2         Many of the large farmers in the Quasmieh/Ras El Ain area are
financially secure and highly receptive to new production techniques and plant
materials. Some have established contacts with public and private research
institutions abroad to obtain and introduce, inter alia, tissue-cultured, cold
tolerant banana cultivars, tropical fruit and ornamental trees as well as
flowers. Hence, banana cultivation and specialty crops (kiwi, passion fruit,
mango) are expected to expand at the expense of citrus. As a result of
increased urbanization and touristic activity, vegetable growing would very
likely see further expansion (+30 percent) and intensification, through green
houses, protective plastic tunnels and intercropping. The total area
cultivated in annual crops is expected to increase from 500 ha to 660 ha even
when the area cultivable to annual will decrease slightly from 330 ha to 325
ha. As a result, the cropping intensity would increase from 151 percent to
203 percent.



- 51 -
Annex 2
Page 5 of 6
B. Yields and Production
5.3         Yield increases have been estimated quite conservatively, they
vary from 12t/ha to 14.4t/ha for citrus, 18t/ha to 19.8t/ha for bananas and
20t/ha to 22t/ha for watermelon.
C. Incomes
5.4         Farm incomes were estimated by the feasibility study and further
reviewed during the appraisal mission. They vary considerably with farm size
and levels of production techniques. Farm incomes are generated almost
exclusively from profitable, irrigated crops. Their level is much higher than
those at Yammouneh, they range from LL 4,156,170 ($2,459) for small farms
irrigated from tube wells to LL 5,271,770 ($3,119) in small farms irrigated
with surface water and to LL 17,928,240 ($10,608) in medium sized farms
irrigated from surface water. On the basis of crop models designed to
approximate the real costs of production and crop returns, farm models were
prepared for the sub-project area in order to estimate the farm-level benefits
that would accrue as a result of the project. In the future farm income would
increase by 34 percent to LL 7,088,440 ($4,194) for small farms and by 52
percent to LL 27,277,840 ($16,141) for medium farms.

LEBANESE REPUBLIC
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION AND MODERNIZATION PROJECT
THE DANNIYE IRRIGATION SCHEME
I. THE SUBPROJECT AREA
1.1         Location. Climate and Soils.  The Danniye irrigation scheme is
located on the north-western flank of Mount Lebanon, 20 Km east of the town of
Tripoli. The area is in essence an escarpment called the " Sukkar Valley"
where terraces have been established on steep slopes varying from 10 to 30
percent. The width of these terraces averages at about 4 m and rarely reach
12 m; their altitude from sea level varies between 290 to 1200 m.
Precipitation averages annually at about 1,100 mm and is distributed over a
period of six months (November through April) with little or no rainfall
during the rest of the year. Fruit trees production cannot be sustainable
without summer irrigations. Monthly mean temperatures vary from a minimum of
6.5 degrees in February to maximum of 22 degrees in August, relative humidity
is almost consistently at about 60 percent. As elevation increases
temperatures are no longer influenced by the mediterranean sea and become
continental. Risks of spring frosts are limited, because of the morning and
evening air movements that characterize this mountainous region. Closer to
the lower elevations and on the valley flank facing south, fruit ripen early
and benefit from significantly higher prices. Those located at the highest
elevation are closer to the sources of water and better irrigated; they are
also of good quality and are actively sought by traders. Soils are of
volcanic origin on the terraces facing south and of calcarious origin on the
terraces facing north.
1.2         Population, Target Group and Land Tenure. There are about 91,500
people living permanently in the project area, in the summer the total
population increases to about 153,000 people. The additional population is
mainly made up of tourists and owners of relatively large farms, who move to
their land only during the warm months of the year. The feasibility study
estimates the total number of farming households in the 4,800 ha project area
at about 9,300, of which about 5,000 derive their income solely from
agriculture. The others, who own non viable small pieces of land, cannot make
a living for their relatively large families (about 9 persons per household on
the average) and are compelled to seek other employment. Family labor
provides more than 90 percent of the labor requirements of small farms, more
than 50 percent of medium farms and less than 10 percent of the labor
requirements of large farms. The average farm size is around 0.52 ha.
However, a sampling of 192 properties, indicated that 39 percent of farms had
a size of less than one ha, 48 percent were between 1 and 4 ha and 13 percent
were over 4 ha. Geographically small farms are located in the lower
elevations, below 600 m, medium farms were in the middle and larger farms were
above the 1,000 m elevation.
1.3         Present Croppina Pattern and Yields. In Danniye, the whole project
area is occupied by fruit trees. The major species include prunes and
apricots in the lower areas, pears and peaches in the middle elevation areas
and apples in the high areas. Tree densities per hectare are extremely high



Annex 3
Page 2 of 6
and may reach up to 1600 trees/ha. In addition to irrigation water shortages,
particularly in the lower areas of the scheme, present inefficient cultural
practices are not conducive to maximizing crop productivity. Annual plant
mortality is also reportedly high, farmers have to replace about 3 percent of
their tree population every year.
1.4         Because orchards are made up of tree mixtures, it would not be
meaningful to express yields per unit area for the whole mixture. Through the
surveys conducted for the feasibility study, the consultants were able to
derive yields per hectare for each of the major fruit tree species, these are
indicated in Annex 11, Table 1. Yields are generally good where irrigation
water and cultural practices are rational. Differences between successful and
less successful farmers can be noticed in the level of yields ( up 50% more),
but also in the quality of the produce and therefore its acceptability by
traders and exporters. Contrary to wide spread beliefs, good quality fruits
has no marketing problems.
1.5         Irrigation System. The Danniye scheme is irrigated from two main
and two secondary springs with large variations of flow between spring and
summer season (40 to 0.35 m3/sec). Due to poor condition of conveyance
system, 50 percent of water is lost in conveyance
II. DEVELOPMENT CONSTRAINTS AND POTENTIAL
2.1         The major constraints to increasing agricultural productivity and
farm incomes stem from the lack of sufficient irrigation water in the summer,
particularly for the lower and middle areas of the scheme and the inadequate
cultural techniques followed by many farmers in the project area. In the
downstream areas of the scheme water does not always reach fruit orchards.
Instead of providing the 4 to 5 irrigations required by their crops, farmers
are able to irrigate only once or at most twice a year because of water losses
during conveyance. Reduction in crop productivity resulting from the poor
conveyance efficiency would be resolved by the proposed rehabilitation works.
2.2         Traditional irrigation methods, where water cascades from one
terrace to the next, and because of poor water reliability, farmers have had
the tendency to over-irrigate when the water was available. This has caused
drainage problems, plant diseases such as root-rot and leaching of plant
nutrients, which in turn reduced crop yields. A well focused on-farm
technology testing program would demonstrate to farmers irrigation and crop
husbandry techniques that would improve their crop productivity.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Project Obiectives
3.1         The main objectives of the project are to increase agricultural
income of farmers in the Danniye area and achieve a sustainable and improved
management of water resources.
B. Project Description
3.2         The project consists of two major components: (a) rehabilitation
and modernization of irrigation infrastructure; and (b) the provision of



- 55 -                            Annex 3
Page 3 of 6
support to institutions dealing with irrigation water distribution, providing
support to farmers and project implementation. The institutional support
activities financed by the project are described in annex 6.
C. Detailed Features
3.3         In Danniye, project works would include: (i) rehabilitation and
construction of seven concrete main canals (42.2 km) with related structures
and gates to improve irrigation and water availability to about 1,700 ha; (ii)
rehabilitation and construction of associated secondary canals (41.4 km) with
outlets and gates; (iii) improvements to headworks and urgent selective
repairs and rehabilitation for the remaining main canals of the schemes; (iv)
containment and improved diversion for Buraisa spring; and (v) construction of
10 km of service roads. The project will also rehabilitate critical works on
main headworks and main canals over the entire scheme of 5,000 ha area.
D. Prolect Cost
3.4         The base cost for rehabilitating the irrigation infrastructure at
Danniye would amount to U.S.$4.92 million. Breakdown of costs per sub-
category are detailed in appendix 1.
IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
4.1         Implementation of the Irrigation Rehabilitation Works would be the
responsibility of the existing MOHER's Water Sector Implementation Unit
(WSIU). The WSUI is under the authority of MOHER's Director General for
Investments and is assisted by a team of international consultants (WSIUC)
financed for a period of three years by the European Community (EC).
Rehabilitation works would be contracted out to private contractors. The WSIU
would have full responsibility and accountability for the technical aspects of
the rehabilitation works to be financed by the project. Procurement and
disbursement would be handled by CDR's Program Management Unit (PMU).
4.2          Oleration and Maintenance of main headwork, main and secondary
canals, would be initially under the responsibility of existing Water Comittee
and would be automatically transferred to the new North Lebanon Water
Irrigation Authority as soon as it is established. Water users associations
organized around the selection and hiring of ditch riders (Shawi), would be
responsible for 0&M of tertiary canals. The composition of the Water
Committee's board, currently designated by the Minister of Hydraulic and
Electrical resources, will be revised to include representatives of farmers.
4.3         Water Users' Involvement.  Although farmers are currently involved
in the operation of the schemes through the designation of the Shawis, they
are not fully involved in the management of the schemes. This has two major
consequences: (i) farmers are not aware of the real cost of irrigation O&M,
especially when they see no improvement in water distribution and are
therefore sometime reluctant to pay the corresponding water charges, and (ii)
simple O&M tasks, which could be more effectively handled by farmers
themselves are neglected by the Authorities. To increase the role and
responsibilities of farmers in the management of downstream irrigation
systems, measures need to be taken to allow for their involvement in the



- 56 -
Annex 3
Page 4 of 6
management of schemes through the support and empowerment of existing WUAs
grouping farmers at the outlet or village level.
4.4         The rehabilitation sequence would include, as a necessary step,
beneficiaries' review and approval of rehabilitation proposals. This dialogue
will be initiated by a social study that MOHER intend to conducted in 1994.
To improve the dialogue between the different areas of the scheme, a Scheme
Water Management Committee (SWMC) would be created. The SWMC would comprise
representatives from the Water Authority and representatives designated by the
WUAs. The SWMC would make decisions based on discussions in which users'
representatives would voice their demands; and users would receive guidance on
management of the secondary and tertiary networks.
V. AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION AND FARM INCOME
A. Expected Chanaes in Cropping Patterns
5.1          Surveys conducted by consultants for the feasibility study
indicate that the whole project area is planted to perennial fruit tree crops.
The survey also indicated that plant population per unit area are high and may
reach 1,600 to 1,800 plants per ha, and are not planted on regular lines or
spacings. Most orchards, located in the upper part of the valley near the
water springs benefit from 3 to 4 irrigations per season. They are usually
cropped to apples and some pears and produce high quality fruit readily sold
at relatively high prices. Those that are further down, in the middle part of
the valley get a little less water and are devoted to pears and peaches. The
quality of the produce in this area is not up to standards, mainly because of
inadequate cultural practices (poor pruning and fertilization, untimely
treatments and irrigations, poor on-farm water management), as is the case for
the other areas in the scheme. Production reaches the market at the peak of
the season and is therefore sold at low prices. At the lowest part of the
valley, the availability of irrigation water becomes problematic. Fruit trees
grown in this part of the valley are mainly composed of prunes and apricots.
Because of favorable winter temperatures and exposition to the south, the
prunes and apricots are produced early in the season and therefore benefit
from good markets and high prices despite their doubtful quality.
5.2         Present cropping pattern is not expected to change even after the
rehabilitation of the water conveyance systems. Waterlogging in some terraces
is expected to decrease with the improvement in irrigation practices. As
Lebanon regains lost export markets, it is likely that farmers will change
tree species in response to market demand.
B. Yields and Production
5.3         Yield of fruit trees are expected to increase as a result of more
reliable irrigation water, better irrigation techniques and improved crop
husbandry practices. Increases are highest for prunes (+30 percent) and
apricots (+18 percent), which are now suffering the most from inadequate water
supply. Yield increases become smaller for the crops grown in higher
altitudes and are closer to the source of water; yields of pears, peaches and
apples increase respectively by 17, 17 and 8 percent each.



- 57 -
Annex 3
Page 5 of 6
C. Incomes
5.4         Farm income. were estimated by the feasibility study and further
reviewed during the appraisal mission. They vary considerably with farm size
and levels of production techniques. Present estimated incomes vary from LL
3,039,750 ($1,799) to LL 15,561,800 ($9,208) and LL 29,300,000 ($17,337)
respectively for small, medium and large farms. On the basis of crop models
designed to approximate the real costs of production and crop returns, farm
models were prepared for the sub-project area in order to estimate the farm-
level benefits that would accrue as a result of the project (Annex 11, Table
2). With the implementation of the proposed project farm incomes are expected
to increase by 24,14 and 8 percent respectively for small, medium and large
farms.

LEBANESE REPUBLIC
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION AND MODERNIZATION PROJECT
THE AKKAR EL BARED IRRIGATION SCHEME
I. THE SUBPROJECT AREA
1.1       Location. Climate and Soils   The irrigation scheme of Akkar El
Bared is located in the Akkar plain in the Mohafazat of North Lebanon, Caza of
Akkar. The scheme proposed for rehabilitation under the IRMP, is divided into
two schemes referred to as EL Bared and Aarka. After the rehabilitation
works, both of the schemes would utilize the El Bared river as their source of
irrigation water. The area under the project extends from the Mediterranean
sea to the east to elevation 75 m in the west. The closest town is Minieh,
located south of the irrigation scheme. Annual precipitation is concentrated
during the winter and early spring months (from October to April) and is
around 780 mm. Soils of the project area, north of the Tripoli/Halba road are
alluvial and heavy in texture. Those that are south of this same road are red
type soils covering a calcarious substratum. Temperatures are moderated by
the proximity of the sea. Minimum monthly temperature in January is usually
at about 8.7 degrees and the maximum is in the month of August when the
temperature reaches about 31 degrees. There are no incidence of frost below
the 50 m altitude where most of the project area is located.
1.2       Population. Target Group and Land Tenure   The total population of
the project area of 800 ha is estimated at about 2,600 persons. Sample
surveys indicate that families are composed of 11 members, giving therefore a
total of 236 households. Those that will benefit the most from the
rehabilitation works are about 173 families (1,903 people), farming a total
area of approximately 377 ha located in the Aarka area.  Farm labor is
provided by families in the small and medium farms and hired in the larger
holdings.  Women labor is hired but little family labor is provided by women.
The feasibility study indicates that 64 percent of the farms have a total area
of 1 ha or less, 14 percent have between 2 and 4 ha and 22 percent have an
area of more than 4 ha. The feasibility study also indicates that about 45 ha
used by the military will be returned to agricultural production as soon as
their conveyance system is rehabilitated. Generally, citrus and other fruit
tree orchards are directly managed by their owners, whereas, 72 percent of
annual crops are grown by share croppers.
1.3       Present Cronvina Pattern and Yields.  Agricultural production is
not as intensive as it could be, mainly because of the difficulties farmers in
the Aarka area have in getting irrigation water for their summer crops. This
has been the case for the last 15 to 20 years when, because of losses in the
conveyance system, the water available from the El Bared river no longer
sufficed for the irrigation of both the El Bared and Aarka schemes. After
being cut off from the El Bared waters, Aarka farmers formed an association
and diverted their water needs directly from the Aarka river at very high
costs (200,000 LL/ ha/ crop). This did not encourage agricultural



- 60 -                             Annex 4
Page 2 of 5
intensification, as most farmers preferred to grow either winter rainfed crops
or spring irrigated crops rather than double crop.
1.4       Crop yields in this area are generally higher(20 to 30 percent)
than the national average yields. Major crops include wheat, lettuce and
carrots, grown under rainfed conditions in the winter in addition to okra,
melons and potatoes; the later are either grown in the spring for early
production "Semi primeur" or during the fall and early winter for late season
production "Arriere saison". In addition to the above annual crops a variety
of citrus fruits are grown, they include oranges, lemons and mandarins.
Larger farms grow more citrus fruit than small farms. Cropping patterns vary
with farm size and proximity to the source of water. As indicated above,
larger farms tend to grow more citrus and less annuals than small holdings.
Small farmers, particularly those near the Aarka river, grow more winter and
summer vegetable crops and have an intensive, labor demanding farming
operation.
1.5       Irrigation System. The Akkar Bared has a current irrigated area of
800 ha. Irrigation water is diverted from the Nahr El Bared River by two
diversion structures with about 16 mcm being diverted annually. Water use
(20,000 m3 per ha) is considered excessive due to large conveyance losses in
the old lined main canal and earthen secondary canals.
II. DEVELOPMENT CONSTRAINTS AND POTENTIAL
2.1       Farmers in Aarka are well aware of the limitations in irrigation
water availability and they have learned not to grow more than they can
irrigate. Their present yields are therefore adequate but they can only grow
one crop a year. After the rehabilitation of the scheme farmers will be able
to benefit from a larger share of water and a more reliable water source. Its
cost will also be lower than their present water because they will no longer
have to pay for the very right-of-passage fees. This will allow farmers to
stabilize their yields at their relatively high levels, but would also allow
them to intensify by growing more than one crop a year.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Proiect Obiectives
3.1       The main objectives of the project are to increase
agriculture-based income in both the Bared and Aarka areas and achieve a
sustainable and improved management of water resources, through (i) the
rehabilitation of surface irrigation infrastructure; and (ii) the provision of
basic public support services.
B. Proiect Description
3.2       The project consists of two major components: (a) rehabilitation
and modernization of irrigation infrastructure; and (b) provision of support
to institutions dealing with irrigated agriculture (see annex 6).



- 61 -                            Annex 4
Page 3 of 5
C. Detailed Features
3.3       To rehabilitate and modernize the Akkar irrigation scheme, the
project would finance: (i) rehabilitation of the existing main canal (11.1 km)
feeding 1,600 ha of total irrigated area; (ii) rehabilitation of El Abde canal
(5.8 km); (iii) reconstruction of concrete secondary canals to replace
existing deteriorated concrete channels (11.2 km) and earth channels (22.8 km)
and repairs to existing secondary concrete canals (5.7 km), feeding 800 ha;
and (iv) provision of water control equipment.
D. Proiect Cost
3.4       The total base cost for rehabilitating the irrigation
infrastructure at Akkar El Bared would amount to U.S.$1.83 million. Breakdown
of costs per sub-category are detailed in Appendix 1.
IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
4.1       Implementation of the Irrigation Rehabilitation Works would be the
responsibility of the existing Water Sector Implementation Unit (WSIU). WSUI
is under the authority of MHER's Director General for Investments and is
assisted by a team of international consultants (WSIUC) financed for a period
of three years by the European Community (EC). Rehabilitation works would be
contracted out to private contractors. The WSIU would have full
responsibility and accountability for the technical aspects of the
rehabilitation works to be financed by the project. Procurment and
disbursement would be handled by CRD' Program Management Unit (PMU).
4.2       Operation and Maintenance of main headwork, main and secondary
canals, would be initially under the responsibility of existing Water
Committee and would be automatically transferred to the new North Lebanon
Regional Water Irrigation Authority as soon as it is established. Water users
associations organized around the selection and hiring of ditch riders
(Shawi), would be responsible for O&M of tertiary canals. The composition of
the Water Committee's board, currently designated by the Minister of Hydraulic
and Electrical resources, will be revised to include representatives of
farmers.
4.3       Water Users' Involvement.  Although farmers in Aarka are currently
involved in the operation and maintenance of the diversion and canal they
created, they are not involved in the management of the original scheme
connected to El Bared river. To increase the role and responsibilities of
farmers in the management of downstream irrigation systems, measures need to
be taken to allow for their involvement in the management of schemes through
the support and empowerment of existing WUAs grouping farmers at the outlet or
village level.
4.4       The rehabilitation sequence would include, as a necessary step,
beneficiaries' review and approval of rehabilitation proposals; this dialogue
will be initiated by a social study conducted that MOHER intends to conduct in
July 1994. To improve the dialogue between the different areas of the scheme,
a Scheme Water Management Committee (SWMC) would be created. The SWMC would



- 62 -                             Annex 4
Page 4 of 5
comprise representatives from the Water Authority and representatives
designated by the WUAs. The SWMC would make decisions based on discussions in
which users' representatives would voice their demands; and users would
receive guidance on management of the secondary and tertiary networks.
V. AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION AND FAR! INCOME
A. Expected Changes in Cropping Patterns
5.1       This irrigation scheme is also called "Aarka", because it presently
gets its irrigation water from the Aarka river through a canal built and
maintained by farmers in the area. The total currently irrigated project area
is about 344 ha of which 142 ha are occupied by citrus. The remaining 199 ha
are cropped to wheat (35 ha), rainfed lettuce (52 ha) in the winter and okra
(33 ha) and potatoes (79 ha) in the summer. In the future, when the cheaper
and more dependable irrigation water from the Bared river reaches the project
area, it is anticipated that farmers will increase their cropping intensity
from the current 104 percent to about 150 percent. This increase in cropping
intensity would be due to increases in summer crops, mainly cucurbits and
summer vegetables. The evolution of cropping patterns and intensities are
indicated in Annex 11, Table 1.
5.2       In addition to the changes in cropping patters and intensity, the
project area will also increase in size. About 42 ha of land, with about 18
ha of citrus, are now being evacuated by the military. Their irrigation
system will be rehabilitated and will receive water from the Bared diversion.
As a result the total cultivated area under irrigation for the Aarka section
of the scheme will increase from 334 to 376 ha.
B. Yields and Production
5.3        Because farmers in Aarka area are presently adopting the strategy
of cultivating crops only when there is a quasi guarantee that sufficient
water is available to grow them, no yield increases are anticipated for most
crops in the future. The only exception being citrus fruit which does suffer
from lack of sufficient irrigation water. For citrus fruit, yields are
expected to increase by about 20 percent, from 15 tons per ha to 18 tons per
ha.
C. Incomes
5.4       Farm incomes were estimated by the feasibility study and further
reviewed during the appraisal mission. They vary considerably with farm size
and levels of production techniques. At present, farm incomes range from LL
3,369,026 ($1,994) for small farms, LL 10,113,596 ($5,984) for medium and LL
16,752,180 ($9,913) for large farms. On the basis of crop models designed to
approximate the real costs of production and crop returns, farm models were
prepared for the sub-project area in order to estimate the farm-level benefits
that would accrue as a result of the project Annex 11, Table 2). An analysis
of these farm models indicates that future increases in farm incomes are
expected to be more significant than in Danniye; they will increase by 52 and
54 percent respectively for small farms and for medium and large farms.

LEBANESE REPUBLIC
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION AND MODERNIZATION PROJECT
THE SOUTH BEKAA IRRIGATION SCHEME
I. THE SUBPROJECT AREA
1.1         Location. Climate and Soils.  The South Bekaa project area extends
over a gross area of approximately 2,000 ha.   It is located on the western
flank of the Anti Liban mountain, on the left bank of the Litani river, just
upstream of the Karaoun dam. It is part of the first tranche (Left Bank
Sector: 8,600 ha) of a much larger scheme, which when completed would cover
nearly 20,000 ha of net irrigated area. The South Bekaa project area is
divided into three zones: Karaoun 1 (250 ha), Karaoun 2 (450 ha) and Joub
Janine (1,300 ha). Climatic information gathered in the project area show
that total average rainfall varies between 1,000 mm per year in Khirbet
Kanafar and 700 mm per year in Joub Janine. It falls within a relatively
short period between November and April, making the growth of summer crops
impossible without irrigation. Temperatures are moderate and do not fall
below 5.8 degrees in January and remain below 25 degrees during the month of
july. Crop water requirements (gross) have been estimated to vary between
6,300 m3 and 9,100 m3 per growing season respectively for sprinkler and
gravity irrigations. Soils are variable from clay loams to sandy loams over
calcarious or gravely substratum. From the cultivable 2,000 ha project area,
only about 1,620 ha are suited for irrigation. The remainder is too rocky or
too shallow to sustain intensive irrigated agricultural production.
1.2         Population, Target Group and Land Tenure. The total present
population of the 2,000 ha project area has been estimated at about 26,000
people, of whom only about 9,700 are residents of the project area. The
remainder have migrated to foreign countries (more than 52 percent) or to
urban centers in Lebanon (about 10 percent). The number of land holdings is
extremely high, particularly in the Karaoun zones where the 700 ha total area
represents approximately 4,500 holdings of an average size of about 0.15 ha.
In Joub Janine, the total land area of 1,300 ha represents approximately 2,390
land holdings of about 0.5 ha. The total number of holdings that will receive
irrigation water as a result of the project are owned by at least 6,890
families, corresponding to about 41,340 people. Most of these fragmented
holdings are now consolidated under rental or share cropping arrangements to
allow for their exploitation; farm sizes therefore vary from 3 to 30 ha. Very
few are being farmed by relatives of the owners and still fewer are being
farmed directly by their owners. It is expected that in addition to present
land usufructuary arrangements, direct farming by owners as well as some land
consolidations (through sales) would occur in the future, once the irrigation
system has been rehabilitated and completed.
1.3         Present Cronpina Pattern and Yields.  Except for a few hectares
being irrigated from the Karaoun lake and from a private deepwell, most of the
land in the Karaoun 1 and 2 zones are only marginally cultivated to wheat;
most of the time they are left in fallow and are grazed by livestock. In the



- 65 -                            Annex 5
Page 2 of 5
Joub Janine zone, the land is cultivated to wheat, potatoes, and summer
vegetables including cucurbits and tomatoes. Three crops are planted every
two years, giving a global cropping intensity of about 150 per cent. Many
farmers irrigate with advanced irrigation techniques, such as sprinkler and
localized types of irrigation and use adequate levels of fertilizers and other
inputs. Crop yields (Annexll, Table 1) are therefore relatively high. It is
anticipated that the rehabilitation of the irrigation infrastructure would
improve the cropping intensity more than the yields, the later would improve
only modestly.
1.4         Irriaation System. The South Bekaa scheme was designed in the
1970s to use water from the Karaoum reservoir and from wells and springs in
the region. The main pumping station at the foot of the dam was destroyed in
1982 shortly after its completion. Since then, the main canal (13 km) has been
used to store the groundwater pumped by private pumps. The subproject
includes for the rehabilitation of part of the left bank Litani Command in the
Karaoun, Joub Janine areas forming 2,000 ha of the total 8,600 ha of this
command for which about 10 mcm per annum will be supplied as part of the 44
mcm for the total left bank command and additional 4 mcm will be provided by
groundwater.
II. DEVELOPMENT CONSTRAINTS AND POTENTIAL
2.1         The rehabilitation works proposed under the project would lift
several constrains. The first would be to make irrigation water available to
area that could not previously be irrigated. The second would be that land
owners would have more incentive to invest and farm their land than in the
past. Future new farmers that would take up irrigated farming would most
likely be those that are relatively poor, and could not, in the past, have
access to irrigation water to cultivate their land. Once a source of
irrigation water is secured, the area would be farmed at a much higher
intensity than at present allowing for incomes to increase substantially.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Proiect Obiectives
3.1         The main objectives of the project are to increase
agricultural-based income in the subproject area and improve the management of
groundwater resources, through (i) the rehabilitation of surface irrigation
infrastructure; and (ii) the provision of basic public support services.
B. Prolect Description
3.2         The project consists of two major components: (a) rehabilitation
and modernization of irrigation infrastructure; and (b, see annex 6) the
provision of support to institutions involved in (i) irrigation and water
management; (ii) agricultural support services; and (iii) project
implementation and coordination.
C. Detailed Features
3.3         For the rehabilitation and modernization of the South Bekaa
scheme, the project would intervene on only 2,000 ha, first phase development



- 66 -
Annex 5
Page 3 of 5
of 8,600 ha on left bank of the Litani river, and would include:(i)
rehabilitation of main pump station, water delivery pipeline and main canal
with its structures and water control system to eventually serve 8,600 ha of
irrigated area; (ii) rehabilitation of pump stations reservoirs and piped
distribution network for irrigation water in Karaoun I and II areas (700 ha);
(iii) extension of main canal (4.4 km); (iv) construction of new Joub Janine
pumping station, reservoir and irrigation water piped distribution system to
irrigate 1,300 ha; (v) provision and installation of equipment for main
pumping station, water control equipment, irrigation pumping station,
tubewells and irrigation hydrants; and (vi) rehabilitation or construction of
800 m2 of existing office and service buildings and 500 m2 of workshops.
D. Proiect Cost
3.4         The total base cost for rehabilitating the irrigation
infrastructure at South Bekaa would amount to U.S.$12.25 million. Breakdown
of costs per sub-category are detailed in Appendix 1.
IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
4.1         Implementation of the Irrigation Rehabilitation Works would be the
responsibility of the existing Water Sector Implementation Unit (WSIU), which
will work in close collaboration with the Litani River Authority. The WSUI is
under the authority of MHER's Director General for Investments and is assisted
by a team of international consultants (WSIUC) financed for a period of three
years by the European Community (EC). Rehabilitation works would be
contracted out to private contractors. The WSIU would have full
responsibility and accountability for the technical aspects of the
rehabilitation works to be financed by the project. Procurement and
disbursement would be handled by CDR's Program Management Unit (PMU).
4.2         Operation and Maintenance of pumping stations, main canal,
secondary system, and hydrants would be remain under the responsibility of the
Litani River Authority (LRA). A Scheme Water Management Committee would be
established by the LRA. Its board would include representatives of farmers.
V. AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION AND FARM INCOME
A. Expected Changes in Cropping Patterns
5.1         According to the feasibility study, only about 920 ha of a total
cultivable area of 1,622 ha are being cropped at present. The area in fallow
is located mostly in the Karaoun 1 and 2 areas, where soils are relatively
shallow and rocky. This lack of cultivation is due to several factors, but
mainly to the unavailability of irrigation water to cultivate high return
summer crops, the non profitability of winter rainfed crops and the degree of
absentee owners who have migrated out of the area. The presently cropped areas
are cultivated to wheat (331 ha), potatoes (387 ha), summer vegetables (156
ha) and water melon (46 ha). Fallow occupies about 700 ha; giving an overall
cropping intensity of about 57 percent.
5.2         Once the irrigation system is completed and water becomes
available, it is anticipated that cropping will cover the whole area and that
over a period of 7 to 10 years, depending on the type of farms, cropping



- 67 -                             Annex 5
Page 4 of 5
intensity would increase to about 115 percent. In addition to the traditional
crops mentioned above, it is likely that some farmers would take up such crops
as winter broad beans sold green in pods (Annex 11, Table 1).
B. Yields and Production
5.3         Yield improvements would vary from a maximum of 23 percent for
summer vegetables to a minimum of 13 percent for potatoes.  Yields of wheat
and water melon are expected to increase respectively by 18 and 20 percent,
from 4.5 to 5.3 tons per ha and from 30 to 36 tons per ha.  Yields of major
crops for the five sub-project areas are indicated in Annex 11, Table 1.
C. Incomes
5.4         arm incomes were estimated by the feasibility study and further
reviewed during the appraisal mission. They vary considerably with farm size
and levels of production techniques. At present, farm incomes vary from LL
8,056,539 ($4,767) for small farms (average 3.7 ha) to LL 33,532,620 ($19,842)
and LL 70,549,149 ($41,745) for medium (average 15.4 ha)and large farms
(average 32.4 ha) respectively. In South Bekaa, even though land holdings are
small and the population is relatively poor, most farmers do not cultivate
single holdings, but group a number of holdings into large farms
'exploitation". Crop and farm models were prepared for the sub-project area
in order to estimate the farm-level benefits that would accrue as a result of
the project. An analysis of these farm models (Annex 11, Table 2) indicates
that in the future and because of the elimination of fallow on large area (700
ha) of the project, farm income is expected to increase significantly by about
200 percent for all types of farms. It is also anticipated that some of the
migrating population will return to the area and with the money earned abroad,
they would consolidate and increase the size of their holdings through
purchases from family member and neighbors. Farm size (exploitation) and
number of holdings may therefore decrease, because more land holders would
want to farm their land directly rather than renting it to large operators.
Farm numbers on the other hand are likely to increase as a result of returning
emigrants, who take up cultivating again on consolidated owned farms irrigated
with water supplied by the project scheme.
