LEBANESE REPUBLIC
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION AND MODERNIZATION PROJECT
INSTITUTIONAL SUPPORT
1. BACKGROUND
A. The Irrigation Subsector
1.1         Water Resources.  The country-wide water balance shows that out of a
net potentially available surface flow of 3,375 mcm (of which 2,557 mcm is wet
season and 818 mcm is dry season flow), only 1,800 mcm is readily available for
use in addition to the 400 mcm of exploitable groundwater. The present total
irrigated area of about 87,000 ha currently has an annual water consumption of
about 670 mcm of which about 250 mcm is from groundwater. Future annual water
requirements (year 2015), for domestic, industrial and agricultural uses have
been estimated by the Ministry of Hydraulic and Electric Resources (MOHER) at
900, 240 and 1,415 mcm, respectively. Hence, by that year, the deficit in the
overall water supply would be of the order of 350 mcm. More than 50 sites have
been recommended in the past for further geological investigations as potential
dam sites, however, only the Karaoun Dam has been implemented with the large
expected leakages from reservoirs found on Karstic rocks being the main reason
for rejecting potential dam sites. Hydrological data collection and processing
has been severely disrupted over the last two decades as a result of the
destruction of many of the weather and gauging stations, the abandonment of
stations in areas of unrest by the staff and the unavailability of funds to
replace or maintain equipment and to pay staff. There is currently a serious
need for a revised integrated national development plan for the management of
water resources. This will require updating the existing studies for each
watershed to assess available surface and groundwater resources and present and
future water needs for municipal/industrial and irrigation needs. Plans will
have to be drawn up for an integrated development program for water use and were
needed institutional reorganization will have to be introduced to ensure
sustainable systems. With the support of the EERP the Government is preparing a
Water Management Strategy and is considering the establishment of a National
Water Council, which would be the highest water resources policy formulating
body which would also supervise the implementation of the development plan
ensuring the enforcement of policy and compliance with norms and regulations.
B. Water Sector Institutions
1.2         The Ministry of Hydraulic and Electrical Resources (MOHER1 is the
main institution involved in irrigation through its General Directorate of
Hydraulic and Electrical Equipment (DGEHE), which has overall responsibility for
water development planning and the development and exploitation of natural water
resources including irrigation through its Irrigation Service. The Litani River
Authority (LRA) is a public corporation, created in 1954 for the multi-purpose
development of the Litani river basin and is governed by a Board of Directors
nominated by the Council of Ministers. The LRA is in charge of irrigation and



- 70 -
Annex 6
Page 2 of 23
hydropower production in the Litani river basin and for hydraulic flow
measurements throughout the whole country. Nineteen Reaional Water Authorities
IRWA1 and 209 Reaional Water Committees (RWC), come under the administrative
supervision of the MOHER's General Directorate for Operations, and are
responsible for water supply management for both domestic and irrigation water
supplies, including systems operation and maintenance. Although, RWA and RWC
are autonomous commercial enterprises, the revenues they derive from water sales
are not sufficient to cover O&M expenses with government providing annual
subsidies to cover the deficit which it has decided to discontinue this year.
Unofficial Water User Associations at the village or irrigation module level are
operational in most irrigation schemes. However, in some of the schemes, there
is a need for intermediate institutions (Schemes Associations or Water
Districts) to facilitate dialogue among all the users of the same irrigation
water in order to solve conflicts, collect water charges and organize water
distribution. The Ministry of Agriculture's Green Plan (GP) (para. 12) is
responsible for small hydraulic works and farm-level infrastructure, as well as
soil conservation, land reclamation, development of rural roads, and has
maintained a reasonable number of its technical personnel and operational
capability.
C. Agricultural Institutions
1.3         The Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) is mandated to set agricultural
policy, plan and support its sustainable development through its central and
regional services and satellite agencies. These include, inter alia, the
Agricultural Research Institute of Lebanon (ARIL) and the Green Plan (GP). MOA
has been extremely weakened by the civil war, in fact both its Central and
Regional Offices are operating at low capacity. Only ARIL and the GP maintain
some operational capacity at the moment. Present level of staffing is very low,
as only about 15 percent of the allowed positions are filled (310 over 2,200).
The Agricultural Research Institute of Lebanon (ARIL) is an autonomous body,
with a central research station at Tell Amara, in the Bekaa Valley and six
specialized regional stations, including Fanar(Beirut), Kfar Dan (Baalbek), and
Sour (South Lebanon). Before the hostilities began, ARIL conducted valuable
applied research for practical needs and problems of agriculture in the country.
However, most of the ARIL's stations were heavily damaged and put out of
operation. Notwithstanding the hostilities, private and international research
institutions have remained operational: the Agricultural Research and Education
Center (AREC) of the American University of Beirut (AUB), at Haouch Sneid, the
Experimental Research Center of the School of Agriculture of the Saint Joseph
University and the research stations of the International Center for
Agricultural Research in the Dry Areas (ICARDA) at Terbol and Kfardan. Tha
Green Plan (GP) is an autonomous agency of the MOA responsible for land
preservation, land reclamation, road construction, small hydraulic developments
and on farm level infrastructure works. It has maintained a valuable part of its
personnel and operation capacity in five regional branches and has about 300
agents, of which 50 are engineers.
1.4         Agricultural Suovort Services.  Although theoretically represented
in six major regions covering the whole of Lebanon, MOA's extension services are
practically non operational and most of the technological improvements are
selectively introduced and spread through the private sector, mainly through
input suppliers and a few of the existing agricultural produce storage and
marketing cooperatives. The remaining extension staff, although experienced,



- 71 -                                Annex 6
Page 3 of 23
has been inactive during the war and has lost touch with field realities.
Salaries are extremely low and are not competitive with the private sector. New
staff would have to be recruited ,trained, placed in direct permanent contact
with farmers and provided with the necessary incentives. Input supply is in the
hands of very active traders in fertilizers, pesticides and seeds; they also
provide a form of credit to farmers, to be recovered after harvest, through
higher prices charged for the inputs sold. Input supply companies are also the
only available source of technical assistance to the farmers, but such
assistance is limited to the marketing of their own products. The quality of
inputs is often reported by farmers to be below standards and to not correspond
to the label they carry; this is particularly true for fertilizers and
pesticides. There is no source of institutionalized credit for farmers in any
of the project areas, although NGOs, such as the Save the Children Federation,
are developing rural credit schemes targeted to the rural poor. Within the
present banking system in Lebanon, small and medium sized farmers have
practically no access to formal credit. Farmers may receive credit assistance
from local money lenders and commercial firms (input suppliers and/or
wholesalers) for periods which do not exceed 12 months. Credit from input
suppliers is usually denominated in USD equivalent at competitive interest
rates. However, the interest charged by local traders and wholesalers on
informal credit in local currency is frequently very high (above 100 percent per
year equivalent). A common complaint on the part of farmers is the sale of
vegetables at low prices from neighboring countries, and for delays in payment
on the part of some exporters.
D. The Role of the Private Sector in Agriculture
1.5         In sharp contrast to the public institutions, the private sector
remained remarkably active throughout the nearly two decades of civil strife and
has filled many of the voids created by the absence of the public sector.
However, private supply of agricultural services remains limited to advice on
the use of chemical inputs by firms making those products and to geographically
limited research results obtained by research stations. Essential services such
as phytosanitary regulation, technical support, and quality control of
agricultural export produce are still weak. At the grass root level, water
users groups exist in almost all schemes and villages, in the large schemes,
their participation in the operation and the management of the irrigation is
generally limited to the most downstream segments of irrigation systems. There
are also farmers cooperatives and chambers of commerce. The cooperatives are
generally organized according to specific agricultural commodities (citrus or
fruit cooperatives); they supply their members with some farm inputs, provide
cold storage facilities and marketing assistance. Chambers of commerce are
organized on a regional basis, they provide their members with marketing
information and certificates of origin for their agricultural export products.
The Saida chamber of commerce, one of the most active in Lebanon, is working
with other chambers to get the GOL to approve and act on their proposals to set
and enforce quality standards on agricultural commodities marked for export.



- 72-                                 Annex 6
Page 4 of 23
II. THE PROJECT
A. Proiect Obiectives
2.1         The main objectives of the project are to increase agriculture-based
income and employment in the project areas and achieve a sustainable and
improved management of water resources, through (i) the rehabilitation of
surface irrigation infrastructure; and (ii) the provision of basic public
support services.
B. Prolect DescriRtion
2.2         The project consists of two major components: (a) the rehabilitation
and modernization of irrigation infrastructure, which has been described in
annexes 1 through 5; and (b) the provision of support to institutions involved
in (i) irrigation and water management; (ii) agricultural support services; and
(iii) project implementation and coordination.
2.3         Under institutional strengthening, support would be provided to
MOHER, MOA, the Agronomic Institute of Lebanon (ARIL), the Litani River
Authority (LRA) and the Green Plan (GP) to enhance their capacities, rebuild
their technical expertise and enable them to resume their active role in
providing the necessary technical support to insure the attainment of project
benefits. This would also enable these organizations to implement the project
efficiently. The support would include additional contractual staff, office
equipment, vehicles and training. Consultancy services and technical assistance
would be provided for preparation of feasibility studies, preparation of designs
and bidding documents and for supervision of works.
C. Detailed Features
2.4         Irrioation and Water Management Institutions. The project would
support selected departments of MOHER, and LRA as well as selected Regional
Water Authorities directly involved in the implementation and the operation of
the subprojects.
(a)   Irriaation Services. The project would support: (i)  MOHER's
Irriaation Deoartment, (US$1.35 million, base cost), to supervise
and control execution of studies and construction work by financing
office equipment, 2 vehicles, rehabilitation of offices, studies and
detail designs, technical assistance 78 sm of civil and irrigation
engineers, training (overseas: 10.5 sm and local: 70 sm),
incremental O&M of vehicles and office equipment; (ii) LRA's
Department of Studies and Prolect US$0.97 million, base cost), to
supervise and control the studies and the construction works for
irrigation schemes in the Litani basin and in South Lebanon, by
providing office equipment, 3 vehicles, rehabilitation of offices,
technical assistance (51 sm of engineers), training for engineers
and technicians (overseas: 10.5 sm and local: 45 sm), incremental
O&M of vehicles, buildings, office equipment; and (iii) MOA'Green
Plan (US$0.77 million, base cost): strengthen present capacity to
implement small schemes through provision of office equipment, 6
vehicles, rehabilitation of offices, studies for detailed designs of
small schemes, technical assistance (24 sm each of an irrigation and



- 73 -                                Annex 6
Page 5 of 23
rural engineer), training of engineers and technicians (10.5 em of
overseas training and 70 Bm of local training); and (iv) RWA, RWC
and Water Users' Associations (US$0.70 million, base cost), would be
involved in the detailed design of the rehabilitation works and will
progressively take over O&M. To facilitate this transition, the
project would finance 12 sm of overseas training, 45 staffweeks of
local training, workshops and in-service training for ditch-riders
(Shawis), and technical assistance (15 sm of expatriates and 204 sm
of local consultants). The project would support the Regional Water
Authorities and Water Users' Association by providing projects
facilities including O&M equipment (US$4.85 million, base cost),
through providing 5 office equipment sets, 25 vehicles, 59
motorcycles, and 8 loader-backhoe, as well as incremental operating
costs.
(b)   Environmental Monitoring. The project would strengthen MOHER and LRA
capacity to monitor the quality of groundwater aquifers, especially
in the coastal areas where the danger of sea water intrusion exists.
It would also support the gathering and interpretation of
hydrogeological data in the project area. The project would
rehabilitate MOHER's Hydroceoloaical Department (US$0.50 million,
base cost), to initiate a groundwater monitoring program in the
project area by providing laboratory and office equipment, one
vehicle, technical assistance (9 sm of local and international
consultants as computer specialist, hydrogeologist and
environmentalist), studies (Yammouneh storage reservoirs),
incremental O&M of vehicles, etc. The project would also support
LRA's Department of Water Resources (Us$0.37 million, base cost) by
providing hydrometric and office equipment, rehabilitation of office
building, 2 vehicles, technical assistance (7 Bm of local computer
specialist and international hydrologist), incremental O&M of
vehicles and equipment.
2.5         Agricultural Services. The project would support the rehabilitation
of essential public services in applied research as well as quality control of
agricultural inputs and pesticide residues in agricultural produce. The project
would also finance an innovative approach to provide farmers in the various
subproject areas with on-farm technology testing and extension services.
(a)   Applied Research (US$1.88 million, base cost).  Under this sub-
component, the project would support on-station applied research.
This research would be conducted by ARIL and would focus, inter
alia, on subjects dealing with verification trials for new cereals,
pulses, vegetables and tree crops varieties and species, irrigation
techniques, plant disease control, integrated pest management and
soil investigations, mapping and classification, etc. To enable
ARIL to undertake the above research, the project would finance the
construction and rehabilitation of laboratories, the procurement of
equipment and vehicles, human resources development, long term
technical assistance, short term consultancies and incremental costs
for the operation and maintenance of laboratories, equipment and
vehicles. Civil works to be financed by the project would include
the construction of a 250 m2 soil and plant protection laboratory at
Sour, 2,000 m2 of green house space at Fanar, the rehabilitation of



- 74-                                 Annex 6
Page 6 of 23
the soils, plant protection and irrigation labs (2,000 m2) at Tell
Amara as well as 1,000 m2 of green houses at Fanar. Equipment would
include computers, laboratory, irrigation and field equipment as
well as vehicles. Human resources development would include training
in-country (42 sm) and abroad (10 sm). Long term technical
assistance (144 sm) would be financed by the project to enable ARIL
to implement its incremental research program. Short term
consultancies would be provided for programming, analysis of
research results and training of local staff (6 sm of expatriate
TA).
(b)   On-farm Technoloav Testina and Extension: (US$1.43 million, base
cost). At present, the Ministry of Agriculture does not have an
operational public extension service and the expectation is, that
its reactivation would take at least a few years. In order to
insure that expected project benefits (increased crop productivity
and cultural intensification) are attained, the project would
finance demand-driven On-farm Technology Testing and Extension
(OFTT-Extension) services. These would be specific to each of the
subproject areas, but would encompass all agricultural activities
(irrigated and rainfed crop productions, including fruit trees,
livestock, bee keeping and silk production) in the area and would
complement the existing, private, supply-driven extension services.
Implementation of OFTT-Extension activities would be contracted out
to private institutions ( such as AUB-Housh Sneid, the Experimental
Research Center of the School of Agriculture of the Saint Joseph
University ICARDA's Terbol station, private consulting firms and
consultants), who would be asked to conduct, on the basis of
baseline surveys to determine farmers needs, on-farm demonstrations
and field testing of relevant technologies as well as organize
information field seminars for farmers on topics of immediate
concern to them. These topics would include, among other,
supplemental irrigation and mechanization of wheat and pulses
productions (in Yammouneh for example); crop pest and disease
control (Akkar, Dannieh, Quasmieh for example); improvement of on-
farm irrigation techniques and water use (all irrigation schemes);
rational fertilization of irrigated crops and use Integrated Pest
Management Techniques (IPM); etc. OFTT-Extension activities would be
financed through an OFTT-Extension facility under the control of
MOA. In addition to the long term consultant in charge of OFTT-
Extension planning and monitoring, the project would finance 5 sm of
expatriate expertise in OFTT-Extension (research/development and
extension), 4 sm of training overseas for MOA's extension staff, 2
computers, the purchase and production of extension documentation
and materials (leaflets, audio-video messages, etc.), 2 cars and
incremental recurrent costs for the operation and maintenance of
office equipment and vehicles. For the testing and demonstration of
agricultural technologies the project would finance, from the OFTT-
Extension facility, the procurement of equipment and inputs required
by the contractors for the conduct of the testing/demonstration and
extension activities. All equipment with a life longer than the
duration of the OFTT-Extension contract would be returned to MOA on
completion of the contract.



- 75 -                               Annex 6
Page 7 of 23
(C)   Quality Control of Agricultural Inputs and Pesticide Residues
Monitoring: (US$0.61 million base cost). To implement existing and
future farm input quality control regulations, aimed at
rationalizing the use of pesticides and enforcing quality standards
on agricultural produce (pesticide residues), the project would
finance the rehabilitation of two ARIL laboratories at Fanar, where
pesticide formulation, residues and chemical fertilizer analysis are
made. The project would also finance the procurement of laboratory
equipment as well as 2-2X4 pick ups, for sample collection and field
inspections, 3 and 7 person months each of expatriate and local
technical assistance and 8 person months of training overseas for
laboratory technicians. In addition to the above, the project would
enhance the capabilities of MOA's planned Plant Protection and
Quarantine Services Department, by financing the procurement of one
computer and accessories, two 2X4 double cabin pick-ups and 4 person
months of overseas training. Costs of recurrent expenditures for
operation and maintenance of vehicles, buildings and laboratories
would also be financed under the project.
2.6         Support to Prolect Implementation (US$0.25 million, base cost). The
project would complement the existing MOHER's Water Sector Implementation Unit
(MOHER) by providing a computer, a vehicle and 12 sm of an international
irrigation agronomist.
D. Proiect Cost
2.7         The total base cost of the institutional support component of the
project would amount to US$ 8.8 millio and project facilities would amount to
US$4.8 million. Breakdown of costs per sub-component are given in Appendix 1 to
11.
III. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Implementation Arrangements
3.1         General.  To ensure the IRMP's full integration in the National
Emergency Reconstruction Program (NERP) and its full coordination with the on-
going Emergency Reconstruction and Rehabilitation Project (ERRP), the proposed
project would be managed by CDR. CDR would be assisted by the Program
Management Unit (PMU) established under the ERRP. Actual project execution
would be carried out by MOHER and MOA, assisted by the Water Sector
Implementation Unit (WSIU). This unit was recently established in MOHER to
implement the water sector program of the NERP and has the mandate and the
capability to handle irrigation. Procurement and disbursement under the project
would be handled by the CDR's PMU. This would avoid the cumbersome and time-
consuming procurement procedures under standard government regulations and would
benefit from the streamlined procurement procedures of the CDR. The PMU would
also keep the project's accounts and manage the Special Account.
3.2         Execution of the Irrigation Rehabilitation Component would be the
responsibility of the existing Water Sector Implementation Unit (WSIU). The
WSIU is under the authority of MOHER's Director General for Hydraulic and
Electric Equipment and is assisted by a team of international consultants
(WSIUC) financed for a period of three years by the European Union (EU) under



- 76 -                               Annex 6
Page 8 of 23
the Technical Assistance project to the Lebanese Administration. The team
leader of the WSIUC is the manager of the WSIU. Three Deputy Managers have been
nominated by MOHER to work as counterparts to the WSIU manager and, after
completion of the WSIU contract, are intended to become managers of their
respective water subsectors: water supply, wastewater and irrigation programs.
The Head of MOHER's Irrigation Service has been designated as deputy manager for
the irrigation subsector. This will ensure the appropriate coordination with
the water supply and wastewater rehabilitation projects implemented by the same
Sector Implementation Unit (SIU). Rehabilitation works would be contracted out
to private contractors.
3.3         The WSIU would have full responsibility and accountability for the
technically related project features and would be responsible for the following
tasks: (i) preparation of feasibility studies for specific schemes through
consultants and preparatory work for the recruitment of consultants; (ii)
supervision of the preparation of detailed engineering and tender documents and
contract packaging; (iii) management of consultants; (iv) all technical aspects
of procurement of goods and services; (v) monitoring of civil works contracts
and project supervision; (vi) periodic reporting; (vii) management of studies
and actions required for organizational and institutional development, and
adequate cost recovery; (viii) liaison and coordination with MOA, LRA and RWAs.
The WSIUC consists of the following units: technical services, contract
management, program monitoring and control, and administration, already staffed
by five international consultants in addition to the team leader (215 sm)
financed by the EU. Short term specialists (140 sm) will also be financed by
the EU. The proposed project would complement EU assistance by providing an
irrigation agronomist, as well as a four wheel drive vehicle and a computer.
3.4         operation and Maintenance of main headwork, main and secondary
canals, would be initially under the responsibility of existing Water
Authorities and Water Committees and would be automatically transferred to the
new Regional Water Irrigation Authorities (LRA, North Lebanon, North Bekaa,
Mount Lebanon) as soon as they are established (Annex 16).  Water users
associations would be responsible for O&M of tertiary canals. The Government's
Letter of Irrigation Sector Policy (Annexl6) indicates that the composition of
the board of the new Regional Water Irrigation Authorities will be revised to
include representatives of farmers. Project implementation would lead, after
rehabilitation of the selected schemes, to their substantial self-management and
self-financing. In line with GOL's policy, present scheme management and
administration procedures would be revised with the purpose of introducing
tighter management control, stricter financial control and greater
accountability. To ensure efficient management and to establish fiscal
accountability, the GOL intends to establish Regional Water Irrigation
Authorities (RWIAs) to handle O&M of headworks, main and secondary canals and
organize cost recovery. RWIA will have a lean structure and will contract out
O&M to private contractors and would progressively transfer their contributions
to autonomous Scheme Water Management Committees (SWMC). Water users'
representation in the boards of the RWIAs will ensure control of their operating
expenses.
3.5         Water Users' Involvement.  Although farmers are currently involved
in the operation of the schemes through unofficial Water Users' Associations
organized around ditch riders (Shawis), they are not involved in the management
of the schemes. This has two major consequences: (i) farmers are not aware of



- ~77 -
Annex 6
Page 9 of 23
the real cost of irrigation O&M, especially when they see no improvement in
water distribution and are, therefore, sometimes reluctant to pay the
corresponding water charges, and (ii) simple O&M tasks, which could be more
effectively handled by farmers themselves, are sometimes neglected by the
Authorities. To increase the role and responsibilities of farmers in the
management of downstream irrigation systems, measures need to be taken to allow
for their involvement in the management of schemes through the support and
empowerment of existing WUAs grouping farmers at the outlet or village level.
3.6         The rehabilitation sequence would include, as a necessary step,
beneficiaries' review and approval of rehabilitation proposals; this dialogue
would start at the appraisal stage, for schemes yet to be selected. To that
effect, MOHER is launching social studies in all 28 schemes to evaluate social
priorities, and asses the willingness of the population to participate in the
project. Following completion of the feasibility studies, MOHER or LRA
personnel would visit the schemes and explain the engineering rehabilitation
plans to farmers' representatives, and Shawis, at village level. If the
farmers' representatives wish the works to go forward, they would, through the
RWA, formally approve the plans, and commit themselves and the farmers they
represent to using their own resources to fully finance and to carry out the
scheme maintenance as foreseen in the plans. In selected complex schemes like
Yammouneh, a Scheme Water Management Committee (SWMC) would be created. The SWMC
would comprise representatives from the Water Authority and representatives
designated by the WUAs. The SWMC would make decisions based on discussions in
which users' representatives would voice their demands; and users would receive
guidance on management of the secondary and tertiary networks.
3.7         During project implementation, RWAe and RWIAs would support the WUAs
in the operation and maintenance of the tertiary system, through: (a) informing
water users on O&M and cost-recovery procedures; (b) training the Shawis in the
O&M of the rehabilitated structures; and (c) regular supervision and assistance
to the Shawis. As execution of the civil works nears completion, the WSIU and
the RWIAs would prepare a manual of O&M which would describe in detail the
maintenance and other operations and provide adequate training. They would also
propose an annual O&M program that would detail the division of responsibilities
and functions for O&M between RWIAs and WUAs.
3.8         Execution of Aaricultural SuDvort Activities would be the
responsibility of MOA. However, the volume of work in implementing the
agricultural support activities sub-component of the IRMP does not warrant the
creation of a specific SIU at MOA. The local technical assistant in charge of
the OFTT-Extension at MOA and one of the local technical assistants to be hired
by ARIL would, with some assistance from the SIU at MOHER, prepare the required
documentation for tendering project financed procurement and consultant
services, etc. Procurement and disbursement would be handled by the CDR's PMU.
3.9         On-Station Agricultural Research would be executed by ARIL's staff
and the four applied research experts to be financed by the project would be
recruited before March 31, 1995. After their recruitment, the experts would
each produce an applied research program aimed at resolving the immediate
problems facing Lebanese agriculture in general and those of the subproject
areas in particular. ARIL's research would be coordinated with the OFTT-
Extension activities by the MOA group in charge of evaluating the performance of
the OFTT-Extension contractors.



-                                    Annex 6
Page 10 of 23
3.10        On-Farm Technology Testing and Extension (OFTT-Extension) activities
would be implemented through multi-year service contracts with private
institutions or consultants such as the American University of Beirut Housh
Sneid station, Saint Joseph University's Tanayel station, ICARDA's Terbol
station, NGOs, foreign universities or development institutions, consulting
firms or private consultants. Planning (formulation of TORs, evaluation of
consultants proposals, etc.), supervision and monitoring of this activity would
be undertaken by a long term expert, to be financed by the project.
3.11        Quality Control of Inputs and Produce.  Input and agricultural
produce quality control activities would be conducted by the inspectors and
assistants of MOA's Department of Quality Control and Quarantine Services and by
laboratory staff of ARIL at Fanar. Staff of MOA's Department of Quarantine
services would inspect produce packing houses, markets and input supply outlets,
collect product samples, and have them analyzed at ARIL's laboratories at Fanar.
Traders, farmers, public health institutions and private agro-processors and
exporters are also expected to have the Fanar laboratories analyze their produce
and input samples for a fee.
Coordination Arranaements
3.12        Technical Coordination Committee. Overall coordination of project
implementation would be under the responsibility of the Technical Coordination
Committee (TCC) established under the ERRP and composed of the PMU and of NERP's
SIUs. MOA, GP and ARIL would be invited to join TCC meetings on an ad-hoc
basis. The main functions of the TCC in its dealings with the IRMP would be to:
(i) ensure coordination with the ERRP projects on water supply, waste water
management and solid waste management; and (b) coordinate with other Government
Departments and resolve problems that might emerge during execution.

Annex 9
Appendix 1
LEBANESE REPUBLIC
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Modernization Prolect
Technical Assistance to the Irrigation Department
Irriaation Encineer
Terms of Reference
1.    The MHER requires the services of an experienced civil/irrigation
engineer to advise and assist the Chief of the Irrigation Department in
matters connected with the Irrigation Rehabilitation and Modernization Project
which is being considered for financing by the World bank.
2.    The candidate shall be a qualified civil engineer with a minimum of 15
years of experience of which a minimum of ten years would be in the design,
implementation and Operation and Maintenance of irrigation projects.
3.    The candidate will have a University Degree with a higher qualification
(M.Sc.or equivalent) in a related subject.
4.    Candidates must be fluent in English and French with a working knowledge
of Arabic.
5.    The irrigation engineer will be required:
(a)   to assist the Irrigation Department in preparation of terms of
reference for consulting services, review feasibility studies,
bidding documents, bid evaluation award, and in the subsequent
supervision during implementation.
(b)   to give advise and assistance with the establishment of the
operation and maintenance of schemes developed under the project.
(c)   to participate in discussions and meetings with the WSIUC attached
to the HER and visiting delegations from the financing agencies.
(d)   to travel in Lebanon to visit and supervise works on project sites
and to verify payment certificates prior to clearance by the Chief
of the Irrigation Department.
6.    The irrigation engineer will prepare a quarterly progress report which
he will discuss and clear with the Chief of the Irrigation Department and
WSIUC prior to submission to the project financing agencies.
7.    The candidate will be able to use computer facilities available in the
Irrigation Department.
8.    The period of engagement will be for 18 month.



- 103 -
Annex 9
Appendix 2
LEBANESE REPUBLIC
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Modernization Proiect (IRMP)
On-Farm Technolociy Testing and Extension Svecialist
Terms of Reference
In order to insure the attainment of its expected benefits, the IRMP
would finance demand-driven, private-sector-executed On-farm Technology
Testing and Extension (OFTT-Extension) services. These would be specific to
each subproject areas, but would encompass all agricultural activities
(irrigated and rainfed crop productions, including fruit trees, livestock, bee
keeping and silk production) in the area and would complement the existing,
private, supply-driven extension services. The incumbent should have a
University Degree with a higher qualification with at minimum of 10 years
experience in extension and on-farm technology testing and have some
experience in the implementation of internationally funded projects. The
specialist would be attached to the Extension Department of MOA for a period
of 4 years and would be based in Beirut. His responsibilities would include
the following:
(a) After becoming familiar with the schemes, as they come on-stream for
the rehabilitation of their irrigation infrastructure, the incumbent should
prepare an action plan and initiate the process of identifying suitable
consultants to design and implement the OFTT-Extension activities.
(b) For each of the irrigation schemes to be rehabilitated by the
project, the OFTT-Extension specialist shall prepare detailed terms of
reference, indicating the contents of the global proposals and the yearly
action plans to be presented by the consultants to MOA.
(c) The specialist shall prepare the necessary documentation and
participate in the evaluation of the consultants global OFTT-Extension
proposals and of their yearly action plans.
(d) The incumbent shall be responsible for all aspects of supervision,
monitoring and evaluation of the performance of OFTT-Extension contractors. He
pay particular attention to their diligence in monitoring and analyzing
project impact indicators (as indicated in the SAR) related to agricultural
development.
(e) Provide MOA's Extension Supervision Group (ESG) with semestrial and
annual comprehensive evaluation reports on the performance of the OFTT-
Extension contractors and make recommendations on whether or not their
contracts should be renewed annually.

LEBANESE REPUBLIC
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Modernization Project
ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
A. Benefits
1.1         The primary benefits of the project would be the incremental
agricultural production resulting from increased water use efficiency in the
irrigation schemes through improved water delivery. Higher agricultural
productivity would also be achieved as a result of the adoption by farmers of
improved agricultural practices regarding irrigation as well as better use of
inputs in particular fertilizers and pesticides. Appendix 1 shows the
estimated incremental production for the most important crops. Quality of
agricultural production is also expected to increase allowing Lebanon to
regain gradually the expert market share, especially for citrus and
vegetables, enjoyed before the war. As a result of the increased agricultural
production, farmers' incomes are expected to increase significantly as shown
in Table 2. The typical small farmer's net income would increase within 8
years by 320 percent in Yammouneh, 34 percent in Quasmieh Ras Al Ain, 24
percent in Danniye, 52 percent in Akkar Bared, and 200 percent in South Bekka.
1.2         Overall project incremental production for major crops would be
substantial at full development (year 9 of the project) and would include
about 5,600 tons of wheat (+80 percent), 750 tons of pulses (+ 385 percent
because of very good prices and high demand), 20,100 tons of potatoes and
similar seasonal crops (+93 percent), 14,800 tons of summer vegetables
(represented by tomatoes in the models and made possible by increased
availability of water in the summer), 5,600 tons of mixed citrus fruit (+20
percent), 9,500 tons of apples (+8 percent) and 3,400 tons of bananas (+19
percent).
1.3         In total 21,200 farm families are expected to benefit from the
project. About 3,630 extended farm families (41,000 people) in Yammouneh,
1,200 families (8,000 people) in Quasmieh, 9,300 families (83,700 people) in
Danniye, 170 families (1,903 people) in Akkar and 6,900 land owners (41,340
people) in South Bekaa would benefit from the project. While within the
institutional strengthening component of the project some activities such as
training and extension are designed to meet only the needs of the farmers in
the project area, other support services, including applied research,
input/outp'ut quality control and import/export quality control, are expected
to benefit producers and exporters outside these project areas. A financial
analysis has been carried out to show the level of farm income for both the
without and with project situations.



- 106 -
Annex 11
Page 2 of 25
B. Economic Analysis
1.4   A separate economic analysis has been carried out for each of the five
identified subprojects. The five economic analyses were subsequently
aggregated to obtain the result of the economic analysis for the currently
identified component of the entire project. The specific approach used in
each of the analyses is described in this annex as well as the general
assumptions. The analysis has been carried out over a 25-year period.
Cost Stream
1.5         The cost streams are based on the economic project costs as
calculated by COSTAB. This implies exclusion of taxes and price
contingencies. Civil works have been costed at likely contract prices.
Imported equipment has been costed at CIF prices, Considering that there are
no restrictions on the trade of goods and services, the Lebanses Pound is
freely convertible, and that domestic markets are generally free from price
distortions, no conversion factor has been applied to the investment cost
stream and the analysis has been carried out taking into account the official
exchange rate of US$ 1 - L.L. 1690. Since this project uses the program
approach, costs not directly related to the individual sub-project have been
excluded from the economic analysis for those sub-projects.   The aggregate
economic analysis includes the costs for the institutional strengthening
component proportional to the area covered by the identified schemes (63
percent). Costs related to the Green Plan are not taken into account, because
Green Plan will be involved only in the small and medium schemes program.
Component                                  COSTAB    Total US$   Allocated
Table        Cost        Cost
Irrigation Services (MOHER)                  17             1375         847
Studies and Projects (LRA)                   13              970         611
Green Plan (MOA)                             11              775           0
Regional Water and WUAs                      12              699         440
Hydrogeological Services (MOHER)             15              499         314
Department of Water Resources (LRA)          16              367         231
Applied Research (ARIL)                      9              1882        1185
Support for Extension                        10             1431         902
Quality Control                              18              610         384
Water Sector Implementation Unit             14              254         160



- 107 -
Annex 11
Page 3 of 25
1.6         Annual recurrent costs include: incremental operation and
maintenance costs of the subprojects; and for the aggregate analysis
incremental operating costs of institutional strengthening components.
Replacement of vehicles and equipment has been provided at intervals of six
years during the project life.
Benefit Stream
1.7         The benefit streams of the economic analysis are based on the
aggregation at the farm level taking into account evolving cropping patterns
and adoption rates for each of the subprojects. The project benefits are
calculated by subtracting the without-project returns (which are assumed to
remain constant over time) from the with-project returns.
1.8         Prices. The analysis (Appendix 3) has been carried out in constant
1994 prices. The following are the basic principles on which the prices used
in the analysis are based:
(i)   Traded output crops have been priced on the basis of import parity
for wheat, bananas, and pulses for which Lebanon is a net
importer. Export parity prices have been taken into account for
oranges, potatoes and vegetables which account for a large share
of total Lebanon agricultural exports. These prices have been
brought to the point of first sale, i.e. at the farm gate.
Transportation costs have been included from the field to the
farm. Projected world market prices in constant 1994 terms for
wheat, bananas and oranges have been calculated from the Word Bank
Commodity Price Projections. For oranges, the economic price has
been derived from the Cif Paris, assumed to be the same as in Riad
which is considered the main market outlet for Lebanon produce. On
the assumption that only 55 percent of Lebanese orange production
would be suitable for export, the final economic price for oranges
is an average between the economic price (55 percent) of LP 718/kg
as derived from WB Commodity Price Projections and the farm gate
price to the local market (45 percent) of LP 300/kg. Based on the
above, a conversion factor of 1.7 has been used to convert oranges
farm gate prices into economic prices. The same conversion factor
has been applied to the financial prices of mandarins and other
citrus. For vegetables, mainly green beans, cucumbers and
potatoes, recent export parity prices have been used based on
indications given to the mission by the main Lebanese exporters.
For pulses, the import parity price has been used taking into
consideration the Cif Beirut price for import from Syria.
(ii) Other output crops, which are either not internationally traded or
for which no world market reference price was obtainable, i.e.
lettuce, onions, apricots, cereal straw and legumes, hay, have
been priced based on local financial prices.



- 108 -                            Annex 11
Page 4 of 25
(iii) Irriaation Water charges have been excluded from the economic
analysis as the schemes investment and O&M costs have already been
included in project costs. Since water savings in each of the sub-
projects are used to increased cropped areas or cropping
intensity, total irrigation water usage in the project areas does
not change with the implementation.
(iv) Labor. The economic wage rates were assumed to correspond to the
actual wage structure. There is virtually no structural
unemployment of labor forces in Lebanon and during harvest seasons
external laborers, mainly from Syria are employed. On this basis,
a conversion factor of 1 has been used for agricultural labor
wages.
(v)   Fertilizers have been priced at import parity taking into account
WB Commodity Price Projections for Urea, TSP and Potassium
Chloride.
(vi) The financial prices of other tradeable aaricultural inputs
(mechanization, seed, pesticides) are not subsidized and do not
differ significantly from their respective economic values and
have been used in the economic analysis.
1.9         The overall rate of return of the project is about 19 percent
Appendix 4). The project is expected to generate a net present value of
US$24.1 million discounted at the opportunity cost of capital of 12 percent.
At Yamouneh, the cropping pattern would increase from 75 percent to 109
percent. The economic rate of return would be 31 percent.  At Ouasmieh,
cropping intensity of high value crops will increase from 1551 to 203 percent;
the economic rate of return would be 23 percent. In the case of Dannive there
will be no changes in cropping intensities of fruit trees,increase in yields
will result in an economic rate of return of 20 percent. At Akkar EL Bared,
cropping intensities would increase from 104 to 150 percent, the economic rate
of return would be 24 percent. The South Bekaa project will have the more
sophisticated irrigation system resulting in the he highest cost per ha. The
economic rate of return will be achieved by a large increase in cropping
intensity, from 57 percent to 115 percent the rate of return would be 16. The
small and medium schemes selected from the 28 already identified would have a
minimum economic rate of return of 12 percent. Tentative estimates of the ERR
from the information available in the RAI shows an average rate of return of
the 28 schemes would be about 40 percent.

Sensitivity Analysis
1.11        The sensitivity analysis, indicates that economic rate of returns
to the project and for the subprojects, have been found to be quite resistant
to wide-ranging deviations from the base case assumptions. The switching
value analysis for the project as a whole shows that it would take a 35
percent decrease in benefits or a 70 percent increase in costs to lower the
economic rate of return to 12 percent. A 30 percent decrease in benefits
would reduce the overall rate of return to 13 percent. A 30 percent increase
in costs would decrease the rate of return to 14 percent. A two-year delay in
benefits would lower the rate of return to 14 percent. However the farm
j/ Tree crops
2/Not yet available



- 110 -
annex 11
Page 6 of 25
budget analysis has been conservative in assuming that in most of the schemes,
the first benefits would occur only in year four. The risk of such a lag is
therefore considered limited.
C. Proiect Risks
1.12        Project risks are mainly associated with delays in construction,
institutional building and capacities, socioeconomic and environmental. As in
all construction works contracts, the principal risks concern increased costs
and delayed benefits. The risk that physical component will be delayed is
small as most rehabilitation design work is already substantially completed.
The project has been well prepared and the availability of detailed designs
and tender documents by project effectiveness, the use of reputable
contractors and the high standard of construction supervision to be provided
by the MOHER's WSIU, assisted by reputable consultants, will make it possible
to proceed smoothly with project implementation. Moreover, as indicated in
the sensitivity analysis (see para. 7.07), cost overruns and time delays in
project implementation will not have a major negative effect on the economic
viability of the project.
1.13        Full realization and sustainability of estimated benefits is also
dependent upon successful implementation of the water management program
proposed with complementary establishment of strong institutional support and
effective agricultural extension. Given the history of excessive water use in
the project areas, enforcement of operation rules for optimal water use will
require considerable effort by the GOL. Any serious failure in this regard
could cause a significant water shortage, particularly at the extremities of
the irrigation system, thereby causing benefit reduction. The project has
attempted to mitigate these problems by proposing a strong water management
program and by seeking GOL strengthening of LRA and RWA with enforcement
authority.
1.14        Both the size and complexity of the proposed project appear to be
within the capacity of the public agencies involved. The project will benefit
from the implementation capacity of CDR and MOHER which has been greatly
improved under the ERRP by the establishment of the PMU and the WSIU. The
sustainability of the development impact of the project will depend on the
success of the on-going restructuring of water management institutions,
initiated under the ERRP. The institutional strengthening of the project
would alleviate this risk by providing technical assistance and training to
the new institutions in the irrigation sector.

LEBANESE REPUBLIC
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION AND MODERNIZATION PROJECT
MARKETING
A. Domestic Markets
1.          The domestic market for Lebanese grown agricultural products often
proves to be more risky than foreign markets. Lebanon is surrounded by
countries that highly subsidize agricultural production. At the same time,
the Lebanese economy is very open, which means that domestic production does
not enjoy protection through tariffs, quotas, or import bans. Although the
Ministry of Agriculture has established an Agricultural Calendar to protect
domestic producers at certain times of the year, it has been unable to enforce
the calendar in any significant way. This is particularly true for products
form Syria. Trade data seem to suggest that Syria has been able to export
large amounts of agricultural products to Lebanon. Farmers complain that
domestic market prices for products that are grown in Syria and sold in
Lebanon often fall to levels where Lebanese products are no longer profitable.
Such price fluctuations, however, are difficult to predict as they depend on
actual Syrian production.
2.          Except for the marketing of wheat, tobacco, and sugarbeet, the
government does not interfere in the production and sale of agricultural
produce. Even in areas where the government markets the products, prices are
usually set around the world level, which means that relatively few
distortions are introduced (see Text Box 1). More commonly, farmers sell
their produce to traders at the local or even farm level. However, while
traders and wholesalers are well organized and informed about prices and
quantities available in various local market, farmers have so far been unable
to organize themselves to increase their information base and bargaining power
vis a vis the traders. As a result, the producers' share of the domestic
retail price has been limited to about 30-45 percent1.
3.          Several agro-businesses are engaged in the processing of locally
produced fruits and vegetables. This provides the opportunity to sell year
round otherwise highly seasonable fruits for the domestic market, but also
allows for an easier marketing abroad. The food processing industry in
Lebanon, however, is limited by the relatively low level of agricultural
production suitable for food processing. Many processing plants run at very
low levels of capacity due to the lack of suitable fruits and vegetables. As
a result, many agro-businesses import part of their input requirements from
Syria and Turkey.
1/    FAO (1993), "Agricultural Marketing Study" in preparation for Baalbeck-
Hermel Area Integrated Rural Development Program, LEB/92/016.



- ]31 -
Annex 12
Page 2 of 4
The governmcnt of Lebanon depends heavily on imports to meet the domestic demand for
wheat and wheat flour. It imports annually about 400,000 tons to meet domestic demand and
accumulate some reserves. Imports are scheduled over two month intcrvals. Until 1992 the
government was the sole importer but since the beginning of 1993 this monopoly has been abolished.
Now traders and mill owners can import wheat without restrictions. There are about 14 private
flour mills operating in Lebanon.
The wheat produced locally is subsidized at the farmer's level. The government, through
the Cereals Office, sets the procurement price to the flour mills. For 1991 the procurement price
was set at US$ 0.10 per kg, and the total quantity delivered to the mills was 15,000 tons. In 1992,
the announced price was US$ 0.14 per kg for soft wheat, and US$ 0.15 per kg for durum wheat, but
there were no deliveries on the part of farmers because the open market prices were higher than the
declared government price. For 1993, procurement prices have been announced at US$ 0.17 and
US$ 0.18 per kg for soft and durum wheat, respectively. The government expects farmers to deliver
about 12,000 tons,
The wheat delivered by farmers at declared government prices is sold to the local flour
mills. The difference, however, between local wheat prices and imported wheat prices to the mills is
negligible. The government prices for this year's delivery to mills are set at US$ 150 per ton and the
imported wheat prices range between US$ 140 and US$ 150 per ton.
Source: FAO Agricultural Marketing Study, 9/93, LEB/92/016.
1:
The Marketing of Wheat
B. Foreign Markets
4.          Before the onset of the internal conflict, agricultural products
used to be sold at a premium in foreign markets, mostly the Gulf States.
Lebanese produce had a reputation for being of very high quality both because
of the climatic conditions for growing fruits and vegetables in Lebanon as
well as the proximity to export markets. During the course of the war,
however, supply lines from Lebanon were at times interrupted and as a result
importing countries increasingly turned toward other suppliers of agricultural
products. Traditional supply routes disintegrated which meant that those
traders who had reliable counterparts in importing countries, such as close
family members, were the most successful in marketing their products. Another
disruptive event for Lebanon's agricultural exports was the Gulf conflict in
late 1990 and early 1991 when it became increasingly difficult to sell
produce in the Gulf States. This may in part have been responsible for the
lackluster performance of agricultural exports over the past two years.
5.          Despite the difficulties in maintaining a marketing network
abroad, a large part of Lebanon's agricultural production is still exported to
surrounding arab countries and the Gulf States. Lebanon has, to some extent,
lost its reputation as an exporter of high quality produce, but it continues



- 13.1 -
Annex 12
Page 3 of 4
to enjoy an advantage due to its proximity to the Gulf States, which can be
reach by truclk in a relatively short period of time. Each day, over 50
refrigerated trucks leave Lebanon for Saudi Arabia and the other Gulf
countries.
6.          To engage in the exportation of agricultural products requires no
specific qualifications or licenses on behalf of traders, nor does it require
large amounts of capital. As a result, there are currently about 30 traders
active in the agricultural export market. Most exporters secure the necessary
quantities of products with the help of middlemen who contact farmers
directly. The middlemen negotiate with farmers over the price shortly before
harvest. The price quoted varies considerable, depending on whether the sale
is on a cash basis or payment is made in installments, with the cash price
being as much as 30 percent the below nominal price when payment is made in
installments. However, when the farmer does not receive payment on the spot,
there are no explicit interest charges for deferred payment. As a result,
farmers may suffer significant losses when middlemen delay payment of the
installments beyond the agreed upon timetable. This tends to happen
frequently when exporters experience difficulties in marketing their products
abroad and delay in turn their payments to middlemen.
C. General Marketing Issues

the future is usually already committed to repaying suppliers if inputs.

8.          The absence of any regulatory body and limited cooperation among
producers and traders has also meant that no significant grading standards
have been introduced. As a result, prices are not differentiated on the basis
of quality. Without the prices serving as a signal for the relative value of
different qualities, however, farmers are given no incentive to be responsive
to consumer tastes. This is true for both the domestic and foreign market and
helps explain the loss of reputation that Lebanese agricultural products have
experienced abroad2.
2/    The Fruit Office, an autonomous body under the MOA, used to enforce
quality controls on Lebanon's agricultural exports. However, its
abilities have been weakened by the civil war to the point where
existing regulations are completely ineffective.

IMPLEMENTATION AND SUPERVISION PLAN
1.1         Project Monitoring and Coordination would be the responsibility of
the WSIU who would submit progress reports to the PMU by mid-March and mid-
September of each year. These would be consolidated and distributed within
the Government and dispatched to the Bank by the CDR at the end of March and
the end of September of each year. These reports would cover: (a) progress
with surveys, design and preparation of tender documents for civil works in
each subproject area; (b) the bidding process (by contract) for civil works
and for procurement of equipment; (c) the physical achievement (by contract)
of civil works and the delivery of equipment; (d) the status of staff
training, technical assistance and specialized studies; (e) results (in
tabular form) of monitoring of water quality and other environmental aspects;
(f) agricultural performance (inputs and outputs in summarized tabular form in
the September report only); (g) results of the social monitoring and
evaluation program (including household income and expenditure, land tenure
and exploitation, and social welfare indicators); (h) the annual work program
and budgetary requirements for the Bank loan and for local funds for both
investment and O&M (March report only); (i) the status of counterpart funds in
relation to the approved budget and annual work program; (j) the status of
Bank disbursements, including pending withdrawal applications and projected
disbursements for the following six months; (k) the status of miscellaneous
issues, such as legal covenants and, accounts and audits; and (1) a summary of
the issues raised by the previous supervision mission and their resolutions.
1.2         Review Missions would normally be held in May and November of each
year. They would require the participation of WSIU's deputy manager for
irrigation, and of representatives of MOA, LRA, ARIL, LRA, and managers of the
concerned Regional Water Authorities. The May mission will generally be of
four weeks in-country duration and will be composed of an engineer, an
agricultural economist, and an agriculturist, and one or two other specialists
(e.g. environmentalist or social scientists) as needed. The November mission
will generally be of three weeks duration and will be composed of an engineer,
an agricultural economist or an agronomist and one or two specialists as
needed.
1.3         On arrival, mission briefing meetings would be chaired by the WSIU
manager and attended by representatives of the TCC, by the managers of the
RWA, and by other relevant staff from the MOA, LRA, ARIL, and Green Plan.
Missions would normally be accompanied on field visits by the WSIU's deputy
manager for irrigation. A wrap-up meeting at the conclusion of each mission
would be held in Beirut. A tentative supervision plan is shown in Table 1
providing for 19 supervision missions, including the PCR mission, over the
life of the project, including a mid-term review requiring 22 staff weeks.
Because of the size of the Project, a minimum supervision coefficient of 25-30
staff weeks will be required in the first three years of the Project.



- 1 38 -
Annex 14
Page 2 of 3
Tentative list of indicators to monitor physical progress, agricultural yields
and farmers income is shown in Annex 15.
1.4         Mid-term Review and Completion Reports  A mid-term review of all
project activities would be conducted jointly by CDR, MHER, MOA, ARIL, LRA,
Green Plan, RWAs, and the Bank and other co-financiers. To facilitate the
review, the WSIU with assistance from consultants would prepare a detailed
report for submission to the Bank by October 31, 1997, for a joint review by
December 31, 1997. Section II of the Project Completion Report would be
prepared by the CDR and submitted to the Bank not later than six months after
the loan closing date.

LEBANESE REPUBLIC
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Modernization Prolect
Letter of Irrigation Sector Policy
With the support of the Emergency Reconstruction and Rehabilitation
Project (ERRP), the Government of Lebanon has developed a policy and strategy
framework for the water and sanitation sector and is working on the
preparation of a national Water Management Master Plan.
Water Sector Policy
The main features of the GOL's water sector policy are the following:
(a)   Water supply and sanitation, and irrigation sector investments
should support GOL overall development objectives for the country
and reflect GOL environmental policies and standards;
(b)   Sector investments should be the economic and financial least-cost
solutions to identified problems, and should conform to GOL water
resource allocation and utilization guidelines;
(c)   All costs of water supply sanitation, and irrigation services
(operation and maintenance, loan amortization) should be borne by
the users of such services, through the payment of tariffs, fees
and taxes, to ensure the long-term sustainability of services
(with tariffs the preferred method because they can reflect the
actual cost of providing the service);
(d)   GOL may provide short-term financial assistance in the case of
population groups unable to meet such requirements immediately,
under guidelines and criteria of eligibility to be determined from
time to time by the Government, but in any event only after all
possibilities of internal cross-subsidies within the sector have
been fully exhausted;
(e)   Sector policy and regulatory functions should be assigned to
national organizations, and executive functions should be the
responsibility of regional and local operating organizations.
These regional and local organizations, publicly-owned, should
operate on a commercial basis, following a business plan which
clearly defines objectives and ensures the organization's
financial viability, and which is approved by the organization's
board and by the tutelage ministry;



- 142 -
Annex 16
Page 2 of 3
(f)   Authority and responsibility for the operation and maintenance of
services should be vested in the lowest level of organization
capable of assuming this function, with appropriate efforts being
made to enable such organizations to do so. Regional institutions
should be given the authority to devise systems (and combinations
of systems) of direct operation of facilities and of providing
technical assistance to communities willing to operate and
maintain their own facilities, in order to combine the benefits of
economy of scale (e.g. central purchasing, common management,
major repair facilities, etc) with the benefits of local
administration of services, operation and maintenance;
(g)   The private sector should be encouraged to carry out specific
activities (such as equipment maintenance, etc.) or to assume all
service functions (for example, management of entire systems)
wherever this would result in greater efficiency and a reduction
in demand of state resources.
(h)   Users shall be consulted (for example, through market studies)
prior to investment decision and, specially in the rural areas,
plans for service extension and their implementation and operation
shall be based on community participation;
(i)   To reduce investment cost and to ensure adequate operation and
maintenance of completed activities, preference shall be given to
the use of technologies appropriate to local, cultural, financial
and physical conditions and to those which the community served
has the capacity to operate and maintain (either directly or by
paying others to provide such services)
Institutions
The policy would be implemented through the following institutions:
At the National Level
(a)   A National Water resource Authority INWRA would be responsible for
the strategic planning of water resource development and use of
conventional and non-conventional sources, for their allocation to
different sectors, and for the selling of their waters to the
Regional Water Authorities.
(b)   A Finance Reaulatorv Commission (FRC) would be responsible for
setting cost recovery, tariff, accounting and auditing policies,
the monitoring of the financial performance of operating
authorities, and for tariff approval for water supply and
sanitation services and for irrigation.



- 143 -
Annex 16
Page 3 of 3
At the Regional Level
There would be two different institutions at the regional level.
One for irrigation and drainage and the other for water supply and sanitation.
Four Reaional Water Irrigation Authorities (RWIA) would be
responsible for operation and maintenance of headworks, pumping stations, main
and secondary canals for irrigation schemes. In the North Bekaa, the North
Bekaa Irrigation Authority will cover all irrigation schemes located in the
Aassi watershed, North of the city of Baalbek, including the Yammouneh
irrigation scheme. The North Lebanon Water Authority would cover the Danniye
and the Akkar El Bared schemes. The Mount Lebanon Water Authority would cover
the small schemes located in the Mount Lebanon Province. The Litani River
Authority would continue to cover irrigation schemes in the Litani watershed
and in South Lebanon.
The Minister of Water and Electrical Resources would appoint the
RIA board as well as its President and its General Manager. The RWIA would
have full financial and managerial autonomy to be able to conduct business in
the manner of a commercial enterprise. Water users would be represented in
the Board of Directors. RWIAs would also be organized to delegate maximum
responsibility to local units (Scheme Water Management Committees, Water Users
Associations) or to private companies with tariffs set to reflect local costs.
The Government intends to establish the RWIA before December 31, 1994.
Management of the Schemes
During project implementation, and before the new Regional Water
Authorities are fully operational. The current involvement of water users in
the operation of the schemes, through informal water users associations
organized around the selection and hiring of ditch riders (Shawi), will be
maintained where existing, and encouraged where it does not exist. In
addition, farmers will be formally represented in Scheme Operation Committees
which will be organized by the scheme office, through their elected Shawis.



- 144 -                             Annex 17
Page 1 of 3
LEBANESE REPUBLIC
IRRIGATION REHABILITATION AND MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Methodologv Proposed
For the Selection of the Program of
Small and Medium Schemes (Other Schemes)
General
The program of small and medium irrigation schemes (other schemes) iB a
component of the IRMP. The program has two objectives: (i) increase rural
income and provide employment opportunities in the poorest areas of the
country; (ii) promote local initiative and farmers' participation in
rehabilitation, operation and maintenance of irrigation schemes. The social
objectives of the program will not be achieved without an adequate economic
rate of return. Therefore, in order to be considered under the program, the
rehabilitation of a particular scheme will need to have an economic rate of
return of at least 12 percent.
To preserve social equity, the area to be rehabilitated will be distributed
among the different regions of the country: North Lebanon, Mount Lebanon,
Bekaa, South Lebanon, and Nabatieh.
Selection Methodology
To assign priorities inside each region, the following methodology is
proposed:
1/ Rapid Initial Assessment (RIA)
A general assessment of the status of agricultural and irrigation resources,
support services and market situation as well as of the scope of work, cost
estimate, expected benefits, and socio-economic environment has already been
completed for 28 schemes, and will be conducted on each additional scheme.
2/ Base Social Study
To complement the social information collected by the RIA, a base social study
will be done on each scheme. The MOHER intends to finance and to contract the
study to an NGO assisted by local universities. The study will focus on the
following: assessment of socio-economic conditions, identification of the
target group, implication of women and youth in agriculture, social
organization of operation and maintenance, recent achievement in community
participation in the rehabilitation of the scheme, opinion of the population
on the proposed project.
3/ Selection of the Schemes for Feasibility Stage
The information collected by RIAs and the social studies will be
analyzed under 5 types of criteria:



- 145 -                           Annex 17
Page 2 of 3
a) Socio-Economic Needs
- current agricultural income.
- reliance or agricultural income
- market value of the land
- school enrollment rate
- social conflicts linked to the scarcity of water for irrigation
- return to their land of people displaced by the war
- population density (% population involved in agriculture)
- size of holdings
- etc.
b) Agriculture and Irrigation Potentialities
- status of the irrigation infrastructure and scope for
improvement.
- availability and quality of support services
- marketing facilities
- etc.
c) Community Participation
- existence and dynamism of water users associations
- level and rate of recovery of water charges
- recent or ongoing participation of the population in
rehabilitation works organized by: NGO, municipality, or
cooperative
- Actual participation in operation and maintenance
- Water user's willingness to cover full O&M costs
- Water user's commitment in participating in the rehabilitation
(financial or labor participation)
- etc.,
d) Socio-economic Impact
- impact on women and youth
- employment generation
- financial rate of return
- economic rate of return
- etc.
c) Environmental, Riparian and Resettlement Issues
- the design of the rehabilitation works will aim at a positive
environmental impact and will not involve compulsory relocation of
the local population
Priority will be given to schemes operated and maintained by efficient
water authorities and/or Water Users' Associations, where public investment
would complement local initiative, and where sustainability of the development
impact will be more likely to be achieved.



1 146 -                             Annex 17
Page 3 of 3
4/ Feasibility Studies
Schemes selected under step 3/ would be subject to feasibility
studies under term of reference similar to those used for the first five main
schemes. To reduce investment cost and to ensure adequate operation and
maintenance of completed activities, the feasibility studies will give
preference to the use of technologies appropriate to local, cultural,
financial and physical conditions of the community which has the capacity to
operate and maintain (either directly or by paying others to provide such
services). The water users will be consulted on the choice of technology and
on the scope of the rehabilitation. The studies will address environmental
issues and will include a financial and economic analysis. To be selected for
financing under the project the ERR of the proposed investment will have to be
at least 12 percent. The actual rate of return will be taken into account in
the final selection.
5/ Final Selection of the Schemes
The final selection of the schemes will be done with respect to the
following criteria:
(a) Agriculture and irrigation potentialities
(b) Socio-economic needs
(c) Community participation
(d) Socio-economic benefits
(d) Economic rate of return of at least 12 percent
Preparation of the TOR and review of the studies will be done through
the MHER's WSIU and CDR's PMU. The selection will be endorsed by the Technical
Coordination Committee before formal submission to the Government and to the
Bank for approval.
6/. Detailed Designs and Preparation of Bidding Documents
Procurement procedures will be in compliance with the Loan and Project
Agreements.
